What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Distinguishing Muqaddimat al-Kitab from Muqaddimat al-Ilm
Author defines muqaddimat al-kitab and contrasts it with muqaddimat al-ilm in meaning and relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muqaddimat al-kitab and muqaddimat al-ilm by identifying their respective natures (words vs. cognitions) and their logical relations as given in the passage.

Core Idea
The preface of the book is words; the preface of the science is cognitions; they are distinct but overlap in one respect.

In the study of logic and other Islamic sciences, understanding the terminology used to describe a text's structure is essential. This lesson clarifies the difference between muqaddimat al-kitab (the preface of a book) and muqaddimat al-ilm (the preface of a science), enabling you to analyze any introductory material with precision. By the end, you will be able to classify an introduction and explain its logical relationship to the science it precedes.

Guided Path
1
Define muqaddimat al-kitab
Locate the definition in the Arabic: 'عبارة عن ألفاظ قدمت أمام المقصود لارتباط له بها، وانتفاع بها فيه.' Identify the genus (ألفاظ, words) and differentia (قدمت أمام المقصود مع الارتباط والانتفاع, placed before the intended with connection and benefit).
The definition is explicitly given in the first sentence.
2
Contrast with muqaddimat al-ilm
Note that muqaddimat al-ilm is defined as 'مجموع الإدراكات الثلاثة السابقة' (the sum of the three prior cognitions). Recognize that this is a set of mental cognitions, not physical words. The key difference: one is verbal, the other is cognitive.
Cognitions include tasawwur, tasdiq, etc., which are mental.
3
Analyze the logical relations
The passage lists several relations: between the madlul of muqaddimat al-kitab and the dall of the subject of muqaddimat al-ilm (tabayun, complete divergence); between muqaddimat al-kitab itself and that dall (umum wa khusus min wajh, generality and particularity in one respect). Trace each relation carefully, noting how they differ.
Drawing a diagram of the four terms (dal, madlul, kitab, ilm) may help.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider an actual introduction, such as the preface of a usul al-fiqh text. According to the passage, this preface is a muqaddimat al-kitab (words) but its meaning may relate to muqaddimat al-ilm if it conveys the three cognitions. The footnote by al-Sharnubi adds that the same text can be both depending on perspective: words vs. meanings.
Practice classifying other introductions you encounter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا مقدِّمة الكتاب؛ فهي عبارةٌ عن ألفاظ قدمت أمام المقصود لارتباط له بها، وانتفاع بها فيه؛ سواء كانت تلك الألفاظ دالّة على متعلّق الإدراكات الثلاثة المعبَّر عنها بمقدّمةِ العِلْم فقط، أو على غيرها من المعاني فقط، أو عليها وعلى غيرها من المعاني، فَمدلولُ مُقدِّمة الكتابِ أعم(۱) مِن مُتعلّقٍ مُقدّمةِ العِلْم وظهرَ مِنْ هذا أنَّ مُقدِّمة الكتابِ مُباينةٌ لِمُقدِّمةِ العِلْم ؛ إذ الأولى ألفاظ، والثانية مَجموعُ الإدراكاتِ الثَّلاثةِ السَّابقة، وأنَّ النِّسبة بين مدلول مُقدِّمة الكتاب وبين دال مُتعلّق مُقدِّمةِ العِلْم ؛ التَّبايُن، وأنَّ النِّسبة بينَ مُقدِّمة الكتاب ودالّ مُتعلّق مُقدِّمة العِلْم ؛ العمومُ والخصوص من وجه فيجتمعان في ألفاظ دالة على المعاني قُدِّمَتْ أمام المقصود، وينفردُ مُقدِّمةُ الكتاب فى ألفاظ دالة على غير المعانى الثَّلاثةِ قُدِّمَتْ أمامَ المقصود، وينفرد دالُّ مُتعلّق مُقدِّمةِ العِلْمِ في ألفاظ دالة على المعاني الثَّلاثةِ، أُخْرَتْ عن المقصود، وكذلك النسبة بين مدلول مُقدِّمة الكتاب وبين مُتعلّق مُقدِّمة العِلْم. وإذا عَلمْتَ هذا ؛ ظهر لكَ أنَّ هذا البعضَ الَّذي جَرَتِ العادةُ بِذكرِهِ قبل الشروع في المقصودِ؛ مُقدِّمةُ كتاب لَا مُقدِّمةُ عِلْمٍ، وأنَّ قولَهُ: وَيُسقُونَهُ؛ أي: وَيُسقُونَ متعلق مدلولهِ مُقدِّمة الشروع في العِلْمِ إذا كانَ مدلول ذلكَ البعضِ مُتعلِّقَ الإدراكات الثَّلاثةِ فقط، أو وَيَسقُونَ متعلّق بعض مدلوله؛ حيث كانَ مدلول ذلك البعض متعلّق الإدراكاتِ الثَّلاثة ،وغيره، فظهر لَكَ أنَّ مُقدِّمةَ العِلْم مَجموع الإدراكات الثلاثة؛ لا ذلكَ البعضُ وَلَا مُتعلِّقُ الإدراكات المذكورة، تأملْ
(۱) قوله: تأمل تأملناه فوجدناه خلاف المنصوص عليه من أنها مقدمة كتاب باعتبار الدال، ومقدم علم باعتبار المدلول، إذ الأولى ألفاظ والثانية معاني. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
As for the preface of the book (muqaddimat al-kitab), it is an expression of words placed before the intended (subject) due to some connection to it and benefit in it, whether those words signify the subject of the three cognitions expressed as the preface of the science (muqaddimat al-'ilm) only, or other meanings only, or both them and other meanings. So the referent of the preface of the book is more general (a‘amm) than the subject of the preface of the science. And from this it becomes clear that the preface of the book is distinct from the preface of the science: the former is words, the latter is the sum of the three prior cognitions, and that the relation between the referent of the preface of the book and the signifier of the subject of the preface of the science is complete divergence (tabāyun), and that the relation between the preface of the book and the signifier of the subject of the preface of the science is generality and particularity in one respect (al-‘umūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh): they meet in words signifying meanings placed before the intended, the preface of the book is unique in words signifying non-three meanings placed before the intended, and the signifier of the subject of the preface of the science is unique in words signifying the three meanings postponed from the intended. Similarly the relation between the referent of the preface of the book and the subject of the preface of the science. Once you know this, it becomes clear to you that this part customarily mentioned before commencing the intended is a preface of the book, not a preface of the science, and that his statement 'and they are given to drink' means: 'and they are given to drink' refers to the referent of the preface of the commencement in the science if the referent of that part is the subject of the three cognitions only, or it refers to some of its referent when the referent of that part is the subject of the three cognitions and other things. So it becomes clear to you that the preface of the science is the sum of the three cognitions, not that part nor the subject of the mentioned cognitions. Reflect! (Footnote: Sharnubi says: We reflected and found it contrary to the explicit statement that it is a preface of the book in terms of the signifier, and a preface of the science in terms of the signified, for the first is words and the second is meanings.)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides a precise definition of 'muqaddimat al-kitab' (preface of the book) and distinguishes it from 'muqaddimat al-'ilm' (preface of the science). The author begins by defining muqaddimat al-kitab as words placed before the main subject of the book, serving some connection and benefit. These words may indicate only the subject of the three prior cognitions (i.e., tasawwur, tasdiq, etc.), only other meanings, or both. The key point is that the referent (madlūl) of the book's preface is more general than the subject (muta‘allaq) of the science's preface. The author then contrasts the two: muqaddimat al-kitab consists of words, while muqaddimat al-'ilm is the sum of the three prior cognitions themselves. Using logical relations, he notes the complete divergence (tabāyun) between the referent of the book's preface and the signifier (dāll) of the science's preface. However, between the book's preface itself and the signifier of the science's subject, there is a relation of generality and particularity in one respect: they coincide when words signify meanings placed before the intended, but each has unique instances. The author then applies this to the common practice of mentioning a certain part before the main subject: it is a preface of the book, not of the science. He clarifies that the phrase 'and they are given to drink' refers to the referent of the preface of the science under certain conditions. The footnote from al-Sharnubi critiques a possible misunderstanding: the text under discussion is considered a preface of the book regarding the signifier (words) and a preface of the science regarding the signified (meanings), emphasizing the two perspectives.

Key Terms
مقدِّمة الكتاب (muqaddimat al-kitāb): A verbal expression placed before the intended subject of a book, having some connection and benefit to it. [Note: No significant shift; this definition is standard in the commentary tradition.]
مقدِّمة العلم (muqaddimat al-‘ilm): The sum of three prior cognitions (tasawwur, tasdiq, etc.) that precede the study of a science. [Note: In earlier texts, muqaddimat al-‘ilm sometimes refers only to the subject of the science; here it is expanded to the entire set of prior cognitions.]
مدلول (madlūl): The referent or meaning signified by the words of the preface. [Note: Stable term; no shift.]
دال (dāll): The signifier, i.e., the words themselves. [Note: Stable term; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between the referent (madlūl) of muqaddimat al-kitab and the signifier (dāll) of the subject of muqaddimat al-ilm?
A) Complete divergence (tabāyun)
B) Generality and particularity in one respect (umūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh)
C) Identity (tamāthul)
D) Partial overlap (tadākhul)

Key Takeaway
Muqaddimat al-kitab is a verbal preface; muqaddimat al-ilm is the sum of prior cognitive attainments; they are distinct but can intersect in one respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the introduction of any classical Islamic text (e.g., a usul al-fiqh manual). Identify which parts of that introduction serve as muqaddimat al-kitab (words) and which convey muqaddimat al-ilm (cognitions). Write a brief analysis.
?
Consider the footnote from al-Sharnubi: why does he say the preface is both a book preface and a science preface depending on perspective? Describe a scenario where ignoring this dual perspective would lead to a misunderstanding of the text's purpose.

Etymology and Definition of Muqaddima (Premise)
Author discusses derivations of 'muqaddima' from qadima (lazim) or qaddama (mut'addi).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two derivations of 'muqaddima' and explain why the commentator prefers 'ma'khuudha' over 'mushtaqqa'.

Core Idea
The term 'muqaddima' can mean either 'that which precedes' or 'that which advances the student'.

How does a simple term like 'premise' carry layers of meaning? In this passage, the commentator unpacks the word 'muqaddima' by tracing it to two possible Arabic roots. Understanding this etymological debate not only clarifies the logical term but also reveals how grammar (Basran vs. Kufan) influences the choice of words in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two verbs
The passage derives 'muqaddima' from either intransitive 'qadima' (to precede) or transitive 'qaddama' (to advance). Note the example of 'the vanguard of the army' for the first derivation.
2
Compare 'ma'khuudha' and 'mushtaqqa'
The commentator uses 'ma'khuudha' (taken) instead of 'mushtaqqa' (derived via ishtiqaq). 'Ma'khuudha' is broader and avoids committing to a specific grammatical school.
This choice aligns with the Basran view that derivation is from the masdar.
3
Understand the Basran–Kufan debate
Using 'mushtaqqa' would apparently side with the Kufan school, which allows derivation from the verb directly. The commentator favors the Basran view, which is considered stronger.
The Basran view is that ishtiqaq is from the masdar; 'ma'khuudha' is neutral enough to accommodate both.
4
Interpret the metaphor of advancement
The second derivation (from 'qaddama') sees the premise as 'that which advances the student' in knowledge. The commentator clarifies that the 'advancement' is intellectual, not literal.
This highlights the pedagogical role of the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مأخوذة من «قدم» لازماً بمعنى تَقَدَّمَ»، كما يقال : مُقَدِّمَةُ الجيش للجماعة المتقدّمة منه . (قَوْلُهُ : مَأْخُوْذَةٌ مِنْ قَدمَ) عبر بمأخوذة دونَ مُشتقة الذي هو أخص ؛ إذ الأخذ أعم من الاشتقاق ؛ ليوافق بحسب ظاهره مذهب البصريين : أنّ الاشتقاق من المصدر وهوَ الرَّاجح ، ولو عبَّرَ بِمُشتقة ؛ لوافق بحسب ظاهره مذهب الكوفيين دونَ البصريين، وإن كان يمكن تمشيتُهُ على مذهبهم بأنْ يُقالَ : مُشتق من مصدرٍ قدم. (قَوْلُهُ : لَازِمَاً) حالٌ مِن قدم، ولا يُقالُ : صاحب الحال لا يكونُ إلَّا اسماً ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : قدم قصد لفظه، والكلمة إذا قصد لفظها ؛ كانَتِ اسماً بنفسها، وقوله: (قدمَ لازماً)؛ احترزَ بِهِ مِن قدم المتعدّي . (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ) هذا تنظير بكون مُقدِّمة هنا - بكسر الدَّالِ ـ بمعنى مُتقدّمة ؛ أي : كَالقولِ الَّذي قالُوه في مُقدِّمة الجيش، وقولُهُ: (للجماعة)؛ أي: الموضوعة للجماعةِ ؛ مُتعلّقٌ بِيُقالُ. وَقِيلَ : مِن قَدَّمَ مُتَعدِّياً ؛ لأنَّ معرفة الأمور المشتملة عليها المقدِّمة تجعلُ الشَّارعَ ذا بصيرة، فكأَنَّها تُقَدِّمُهُ على أقرانه. (قَوْلُهُ : وَقِيلَ : مِنْ قَدَّمَ») أي : قيلَ: إنَّها مأخوذةٌ من قدَّمَ حال كونِهِ مُتعدِّياً، وحينئذ؛ فمعني مُقدِّمة: مُقدِّمةُ الشَّارع . (قَوْلُهُ : الْأُمُوْرِ) أي: الثلاثة، وهي التّعريف، والحاجة، والموضوع. (قَوْلُهُ : الْمُشْتَمِلَةِ عَلَيْهَا أي: من اشتمالِ الدَّالِّ على المدلول. (قَوْلُهُ : فَكَأَنَّهَا . . . إِلَخ) أي: والمقدّم في الحقيقة فهمها، وهو تفريع على قوله: تجعل، وضمير كأنَّها ؛ لمعرفة الأمور المشتملة عليها المقدمة، والمراد بالتقديم المسلَّط عليه الكأنّيَّة ؛ التَّقديم الحسّيُّ.
Derived from 'qadima' (intransitive) meaning 'to precede,' as in 'the vanguard of the army' for the group proceeding ahead of it. (His statement: 'Derived from qadima') He used 'derived' (ma'khuudha) instead of 'derived via ishtiqaq' which is more specific, because 'taking' is more general than derivation, to apparently agree with the Basran school that derivation is from the verbal noun, which is the stronger view. If he had used 'mushtaqqa' it would apparently agree with the Kufan school over the Basrans, although it could be reconciled with the Basran school by saying 'derived from the source qadim'. (His statement: 'laziman' (intransitive)) is a circumstantial accusative from 'qadima', and it is not said that the circumstance's owner must only be a noun, because we say: 'qadima' is intended as its word, and a word when its word is intended becomes a noun by itself. He said 'qadima laziman' to exclude transitive 'qaddama'. (His statement: 'as is said') is an analogy that muqaddima here – with kasra of the dal – means 'preceding,' like the expression used in 'the vanguard of the army'. And his statement: 'for the group' means the group is the intended referent, related to 'yuqalu'. And it was said: from transitive 'qaddama' because the knowledge of the matters contained in the premise gives the student insight, as if it advances him over his peers. (His statement: 'and it was said: from qaddama') meaning it was said that it is derived from 'qaddama' while it is transitive, and then the meaning of muqaddima is: the one that advances the student. (His statement: 'the matters' means the three: definition, need, and subject matter. (His statement: 'contained in it' means containment of the signified in the signifier. (His statement: 'as if it… etc.') meaning the real advanced thing is the understanding of it, and this is a branching from his statement 'makes', and the pronoun in 'as if it' refers to the knowledge of the matters contained in the premise, and what is meant by the advancement over which the 'as if' applies is sensory advancement.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a lexicographical and grammatical analysis of the word 'muqaddima' (premise) as used in logic. The commentator begins by affirming that the term is taken from the verb 'qadima' (intransitive) meaning 'to precede', using an example from military terminology: the vanguard (muqaddimat al-jaysh). He then justifies why the author used the word 'ma'khuudha' (taken) instead of 'mushtaqqa' (derived via ishtiqaq): because 'taking' is broader and aligns with the Basran grammatical school's view that derivation is ultimately from the verbal noun (masdar), whereas 'derived' would seem to favor the Kufan school. However, he notes that even 'mushtaqqa' could be interpreted according to the Basran view if one posits derivation from the source word 'qadim'. Next, he explains the circumstantial accusative 'laziman' as a condition of the verb 'qadima', emphasizing that the word 'qadima' is being treated as a noun (since it is the topic of discussion) and that this specification avoids the transitive meaning of 'qaddama'. The commentator then clarifies the analogy to 'muqaddimat al-jaysh', confirming that the word means 'preceding'. Finally, the passage presents an alternative view: that 'muqaddima' is derived from the transitive verb 'qaddama' (to make someone precede), because the knowledge contained in the premise advances the student (shāriʻ) over his peers. This interpretation explains the term as 'that which advances the student'. The commentary notes that the 'matters' (al-umūr) are the three mentioned in the definition of logic: definition (taʻrīf), need (ḥāja), and subject matter (mawḍūʻ). The phrase 'as if it advances him' is explained as a sensory advancement metaphor, not a real one, since the actual advancement is intellectual.

مُقَدِّمَة
اِشْتِقَاق
قَدِمَ
قَدَّمَ

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer the expression 'ma'khuudha' (taken) over 'mushtaqqa' (derived) when discussing the origin of 'muqaddima'?
A) Because 'mushtaqqa' implies the Kufan view which is weaker.
B) Because 'ma'khuudha' is broader and does not commit to a specific grammatical school.
C) Because 'ma'khuudha' indicates that the word is borrowed from military terminology.
D) Because 'mushtaqqa' would contradict the Basran view that derivation is from the masdar.

Key Takeaway
The term 'muqaddima' can be understood either as 'that which precedes' (from qadima) or 'that which advances the student' (from qaddama), and the commentator's word choice reflects a careful stance in the Basran–Kufan grammatical debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Senses of Muqaddimah in Islamic Logic
Commentators explain the two senses of muqaddimah: book preface and syllogistic premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two technical senses of 'muqaddimah' in Islamic logic: the book preface and the syllogistic premise.

Core Idea
Muqaddimah in logic is not an introduction—it is the raw material of proof.

The term 'muqaddimah' might seem straightforward, but in the Islamic logical tradition it carries a precise technical meaning. This passage from a super-commentary on the Tadhheeb clarifies how logicians distinguish between the common usage (book preface) and the specialized usage (premise in a syllogism). Understanding this shift is crucial for reading any later logical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts of muqaddimah
Notice that the commentator distinguishes between 'here' (fi awwa'il kutub al-mantiq) and 'elsewhere among logicians' (fi mabahith al-qiyas). The first is the book's introduction; the second is the syllogistic premise.
The passage begins by clarifying that in the current section, 'muqaddimah' refers to the propositions introduced at the start.
2
Recognize the signal word 'hahuna'
The Arabic 'hahuna' (هَهُنا) flags a shift in meaning. It tells you that the term is being used in a specialized sense that may differ from its ordinary language meaning or from its use in other disciplines.
In Islamic scholarship, such signals are critical for avoiding category errors.
3
Extract the technical definition for syllogism
The commentator explicitly states: 'in the topics of syllogism, it is used for a proposition that has been made a part of a syllogism or argument.' This is the core technical meaning.
This definition restricts muqaddimah to a building block of reasoning, not just any statement.
4
Note the conditions for premises in the first figure
The passage gives an example: in the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative and actual, and the major premise must be universal. These conditions are part of what makes a muqaddimah valid within a proof.
This shows that 'muqaddimah' can also refer to the conditions required for the soundness of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ : المراد بالمقدّمة هَهُنا : هذه القضايا التي هي مدلول لفظ مُقدِّمة المترجم بها؛ لأنَّها اسم للألفاظ المخصوصةِ الدَّالَّةِ على المعاني المخصوصة . (قَوْلُهُ: هَهُنَا ) أي : في أوائل كتب المنطق، وهذا مُشعرٌ بِأَنَّ لها معنى آخر في غيرِ هذا الموضع عند المناطقةِ، فإنَّها في مباحثِ القياس تُطلق على قضيَّةٍ جعلت جزءً قياس أو حُجَّة، وقد تطلقُ ويُراد بها ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَةُ الدليل؛ فتتناول مقدّماتِ الأدلة وشرائطها كإيجاب الصُّغرى وفعليَّتها ، وكُلّيَّة الكُبرى في الشكل الأول مثلاً.
In summary: what is intended by 'al-muqaddimah' here is: these propositions which are the signification of the word 'muqaddimah' as translated; because it is a name for the specific words that indicate specific meanings. (His statement: 'here') means: in the beginnings of books of logic, and this suggests that it has another meaning elsewhere among logicians. For in the topics of syllogism, it is used for a proposition that has been made a part of a syllogism or argument, and it may be used intending by it that upon which the correctness of the proof depends; thus it includes the premises of proofs and their conditions, such as the positivity of the minor premise and its actuality, and the universality of the major premise in the first figure, for example.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage, part of a super-commentary on the Tadhheeb, clarifies the technical term 'muqaddimah' as used in logic. The commentator begins by stating that in the context of the book's introduction, 'muqaddimah' refers to the specific propositions that are the meaning of the translated term. He then signals that 'here' (at the start of logic books) the term has a specialized meaning, different from its common usage. He explains that in the section on syllogism (qiyas), 'muqaddimah' is technically defined as a proposition that serves as a part of a syllogism or argument (hujja). Furthermore, it can also refer to what the correctness of a proof depends upon, thereby encompassing both the premises themselves and their necessary conditions. For example, in the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative and actually true, and the major premise must be universal. This definition distinguishes the logical 'muqaddimah' from a mere introduction, anchoring it as a building block of valid reasoning. The commentary shows the precision required in Islamic logic: every term must be sharply delineated to avoid ambiguity.

Key Terms
المقدمة
قياس
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the primary technical meaning of 'muqaddimah' in the context of syllogism?
A) The introduction of a book
B) A proposition made part of a syllogism or argument
C) The conclusion of a syllogism
D) A universal judgment

Key Takeaway
The term 'muqaddimah' in Islamic logic has a precise technical meaning as a premise in a syllogism, distinct from its ordinary sense of a book introduction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the following argument from usul al-fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (shar'an); nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the two muqaddimat and explain why each qualifies as a muqaddimah in the logical sense. Then, explain what would change if one were to treat the word 'muqaddimah' as a book introduction instead.

What Is Mantiq?

The Two Meanings of Muqaddima
Dassuqi and al-Attar refine the definition of muqaddima, distinguishing its usage in qiyas from its broader meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given use of 'muqaddima' refers to (a) a proposition that is part of a syllogism, or (b) what the correctness of a proof depends on, and give examples of each from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Muqaddima has two distinct technical meanings; confusing them leads to misinterpretation of logical arguments.

When reading logical works, you will encounter the term 'muqaddima' repeatedly. However, it does not have a single meaning. In the passage, Dassuqi and al-Attar highlight that in the chapter on syllogism, muqaddima can mean either a proposition that forms part of a syllogism, or more broadly, any condition upon which the correctness of a proof depends. This lesson will help you distinguish these two usages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Is the passage discussing the structure of a specific syllogism, or is it discussing the general requirements for a proof to be sound? The first usage (a) appears when the focus is on the components of a qiyas; the second (b) appears when discussing conditions like the positivity of the minor premise.
2
Check for the phrase 'juzʼ qiyās'
If the phrase 'made part of a syllogism' is present, it is likely the first meaning. If the phrase 'mā yatawaqqafu ʻalayhi ṣiḥḥat al-dalīl' appears, it is the second meaning.
3
Look for examples
Dassuqi gives examples of conditions: 'ījāb al-ṣughrā' (affirmative minor) and 'kulliyyat al-kubrā' (universal major). If the text lists such conditions, it is the second meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه الشُّروع مقدّمةُ عِلم، وحينئذ؛ فيكون ما يتوقف عليه الشروع مراداً من مدلول المقدّمة، وقيد بقولِهِ: هَهُنا؛ أي: في هذا الموضع للاحتراز عن المقدمة في باب القياس، فإنَّها تُطلق على قضيَّةٍ جُعِلَتْ جزء قياس وتطلق على ما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، فتتناولُ مقدّماتِ الأدلة، وشرائطها؛ كَإيجاب الصغرى، وكُليَّةِ الكُبرى.

العطار: قياس أو حُجَّة، وقد تطلقُ ويُراد بها ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَةُ الدليل؛ فتتناول مقدّماتِ الأدلة وشرائطها كإيجاب الصُّغرى وفعليَّتها، وكُلّيَّة الكُبرى في الشكل الأول مثلاً أفادَهُ السَّيّد، وقولُهُ: مَا جُعِلَت جزء قياس ... إلخ، هذه عبارة الشيخ في الإشارات، فإِنَّهُ قالَ فيهِ: إذا أوردت القضايا في مثل هذا الشَّيء الذي يُسمَّى قياساً أو استقراء أو تمثيلاً؛ سُمِّيت حينئذٍ مُقدّمات، والمقدّمة قضيَّةٌ جُعِلت جزء قياس أو حجة ا.هـ. واختلفَ النَّاظرونَ في كلامِهِ؛ فقال بعضُهُم: لعلَّ الشَّيخَ أرادَ بالقياس ما يتناولُ الأقسام الثَّلاثةَ، فأردفَهُ بقوله: أو حجَّةً ترديداً في العبارة وتخييراً في اللفظ؛ دفعاً لِمَا يتوهَّمُ من اختصاص القياسِ هَهُنا لِمَا يقابلُ القِسْمَينِ الأخيرين، وأرادَ بالقياسِ هَهُنا مَا يُقابل القسمين الآخرين؛ إشارة إلى شدَّةِ الاهتمام بِهِ؛ لأنَّهُ العُمدة في بابِ الاستدلال، فكانَ مَا عداه بالنَّسبة إليهِ مُلحقاً بالعدم، ثم أضرب عنه إلى قوله: أو حُجَّةٌ؛ إفادةٌ لِمَا هو الاصطلاح، ولأنَّ المقصود إذا أدّي بهذا النوع من العبارة؛ كانَ وقع في النَّفْسِ، وعلى هذا؛ تكون كلمة «أو» بمعنى «بل»، ومَا قِيلَ في توجيه هذا العطف المستصعب من أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ اصطلاح، والمعنى: جُعِلَتْ جزء قياس على اصطلاح، أو حجَّةً على اصطلاح؛ فيمكن المناقشةُ فيهِ بأنَّهُ خِلافُ الواقع ا.هـ. والذي اختاره عبد الحكيم أنَّ التُّرديد للإشارة إلى تعددِ الاصطلاح، فقيل: إنَّها مختصة بالقياس، وقيل: إِنَّهَا غيرُ مختصَّةٍ بِهِ؛ وتُقالُ لِمَا جعلت جزءً حَجَّةِ التَّمثيل والاستقراء أيضاً، وأورد على تفسير المقدّمةِ الثَّاني، وهو ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَّةُ الدَّليل ... إلخ؛ بأنَّهُ غيرُ مانع لشموله الموضوعات والمحمولات، وأُجيب بأنَّ المعنى: ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَّةُ الدَّليل بلا واسطةٍ فَلَمْ يدخل، فإنَّ صحةَ الدَّليل مُتوقفةٌ عليها بواسطة تركُبِ مقدّماتِهِ منها، وفيه أنَّ هذا القيد يُخرج المقدّماتِ البعيدةَ للدَّليل؛ فيصيرُ.
Upon it (the science) commencement is the introduction of a science. And then, what the commencement depends on is intended from the meaning of introduction. And he restricted it by his saying: 'here', i.e., in this place, to exclude the introduction in the chapter of syllogism, for it is applied to a proposition made part of a syllogism, and it is applied to what the correctness of the proof depends on, so it includes the premises of proofs and their conditions, like the positivity of the minor and the universality of the major.

Al-Attar: 'syllogism or proof.' And it may be applied and intended by it what the correctness of the proof depends on, so it includes the premises of proofs and their conditions, like the positivity of the minor and its actuality, and the universality of the major in the first figure for example, as stated by al-Sayyid. And his saying: 'what is made part of a syllogism... etc.' This is the expression of al-Shaykh in al-Isharat, for he said therein: 'When you set forth propositions in such a thing that is called syllogism, induction, or analogy, they are then called premises, and a premise is a proposition made part of a syllogism or a proof.' End quote. And the commentators differed regarding his words. Some of them said: Perhaps al-Shaykh intended by 'syllogism' what includes the three divisions, so he appended to it by his saying 'or proof' as a hesitation in expression and a choice in wording, to ward off what might be imagined of the specificity of syllogism here to what is opposed to the other two divisions. And he intended by 'syllogism' here what is opposed to the other two divisions, as an indication of the intensity of attention to it, because it is the mainstay in the chapter of inference, so what is other than it is, relative to it, attached to non-existence. Then he turned away from it to his saying 'or proof' to indicate what is the convention, and because the intended meaning, if it is conveyed by this type of expression, has an impact on the soul. And on this, the word 'or' means 'rather'. And what was said in justifying this difficult conjunction, that each one is a convention, and the meaning is: made part of a syllogism according to one convention, or a proof according to another convention, can be debated as being contrary to reality. End quote. And what Abd al-Hakim chose is that the hesitation indicates multiplicity of conventions. So it was said: it is specific to syllogism, and it was said: it is not specific to it, and it is said for what is made part of a proof of analogy and induction as well. And objection was raised against the second interpretation of premise, which is 'what the correctness of the proof depends on... etc.' that it is not exclusive because it includes subjects and predicates. And it was answered that the meaning is: what the correctness of the proof depends on without mediation, so they are not included, for the correctness of the proof depends on them through the composition of its premises from them. And in this [answer] it is [objected] that this restriction excludes the remote premises of the proof; so it becomes...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) from Dassūqī and al-‘Aṭṭār on the definition of 'muqaddima' (premise). It begins with Dassūqī's remark that the author's restriction of the term to 'here' (the introduction of a science) excludes its usage in the chapter of syllogism. In syllogism, 'muqaddima' has two meanings: (1) a proposition made part of a syllogism, and (2) what the correctness of a proof depends on, including premises and their conditions (e.g., the minor being affirmative and the major being universal). Then al-‘Aṭṭār expands, citing Ibn Sīnā from al-Ishārāt, where a premise is defined as a proposition made part of a syllogism or a proof. The commentators debate whether 'syllogism' here includes induction and analogy. Some say Ibn Sīnā intended the broad sense; others say he meant only categorical syllogism, and 'or proof' indicates a hesitation. ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm holds the hesitation indicates multiple conventions. The second definition (what correctness depends on) is critiqued for not being exclusive, as it could include subjects and predicates. The reply that it is 'without mediation' fails because remote premises (premises of the premise) are excluded, making the definition incomplete. This passage shows the depth of terminological refinement in later logic.

Key Terms
المقدمة (al-muqaddima): In this passage, muqaddima has two technical usages: (a) a proposition made part of a syllogism, and (b) what the correctness of a proof depends on, including premises and their conditions. [Note: In early logic (e.g., Ibn Sīnā), muqaddima is simply a premise of a syllogism; later commentators debate its broader or narrower scope.]
القياس (al-qiyās): Here, qiyas is debated: some restrict it to categorical syllogism (opposed to induction and analogy), others extend it to include all three. [Note: In falsafa, qiyas often means syllogism; in uṣūl al-fiqh it means analogy by 'illa.]
الحجة (al-ḥujja): Proof or argument, broader than qiyas, includes analogy and induction. In this text, it is used as an alternative term for the thing of which the premise is a part. [Note: No significant shift; stable in logical works.]
الاستقراء (al-istiqrā'): Induction; a type of inference that is not a categorical syllogism, but still considered a form of proof. [Note: In Ibn Sīnā, istiqra' is a valid but less certain inference; later logicians often classify it under 'proof'.]
التمثيل (al-tamthīl): Analogy; a type of inference similar to but distinct from categorical syllogism. [Note: In falsafa, tamthil is often considered rhetorical, but in logic it is a separate form of argument.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of muqaddima in the sense of 'what correctness depends on'?
A) A proposition like 'the world is originated'
B) The condition that the major premise be universal
C) A proposition made part of a qiyas
D) The definition of a term

Key Takeaway
Muqaddima has two meanings: a premise in a syllogism, and a condition for proof correctness; the context determines which is intended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the following sentence from a logical text: 'al-muqaddima al-ūlā fī hādhā al-qiyās hiya qadiyyatun wājibatun.' Is this usage (a) or (b)? Explain your reasoning.

What Is Mantiq?

The Debate Over Muqaddima's Scope: Syllogism or Proof?
Dassuqi and al-Attar refine the definition of muqaddima, distinguishing its usage in qiyas from its broader meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to summarize the debate among commentators about whether Ibn Sina's definition of muqaddima restricts it to categorical syllogism or includes induction and analogy, and evaluate the arguments on each side.

Core Idea
The word 'or' in Ibn Sina's definition sparks a debate about the scope of muqaddima.

Ibn Sina in al-Isharat defined a premise as 'a proposition made part of a syllogism or a proof.' The conjunction 'or' (aw) led to divergent interpretations. Some say he used 'syllogism' broadly to include induction and analogy; others say he meant only categorical syllogism, and 'or proof' indicates a shift to the conventional term. Al-Attar reports this debate and Abd al-Hakim's opinion that the hesitation signals multiple conventions.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Sina's definition
Find the quote from al-Isharat in the passage: 'al-muqaddima qadiyyatun juʻilat juzʼa qiyāsin aw ḥujjah.'
2
Identify the two interpretations
Some commentators say 'qiyas' includes the three types (qiyas, istiqra', tamthil); others say it is specifically the categorical syllogism.
3
Examine the justification for 'aw'
Note the suggestion that 'aw' might be interpretative ('bi-maʻnā bal') to indicate a shift from a narrower to a broader term.
4
Determine the chosen opinion
Abd al-Hakim chooses that the hesitation indicates multiple conventions, meaning both usages are accepted.

عليه الشُّروع مقدّمةُ عِلم، وحينئذ؛ فيكون ما يتوقف عليه الشروع مراداً من مدلول المقدّمة، وقيد بقولِهِ: هَهُنا؛ أي: في هذا الموضع للاحتراز عن المقدمة في باب القياس، فإنَّها تُطلق على قضيَّةٍ جُعِلَتْ جزء قياس وتطلق على ما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، فتتناولُ مقدّماتِ الأدلة، وشرائطها؛ كَإيجاب الصغرى، وكُليَّةِ الكُبرى.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) from Dassūqī and al-‘Aṭṭār on the definition of 'muqaddima' (premise). It begins with Dassūqī's remark that the author's restriction of the term to 'here' (the introduction of a science) excludes its usage in the chapter of syllogism. In syllogism, 'muqaddima' has two meanings: (1) a proposition made part of a syllogism, and (2) what the correctness of a proof depends on, including premises and their conditions (e.g., the minor being affirmative and the major being universal). Then al-‘Aṭṭār expands, citing Ibn Sīnā from al-Ishārāt, where a premise is defined as a proposition made part of a syllogism or a proof. The commentators debate whether 'syllogism' here includes induction and analogy. Some say Ibn Sīnā intended the broad sense; others say he meant only categorical syllogism, and 'or proof' indicates a hesitation. ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm holds the hesitation indicates multiple conventions. The second definition (what correctness depends on) is critiqued for not being exclusive, as it could include subjects and predicates. The reply that it is 'without mediation' fails because remote premises (premises of the premise) are excluded, making the definition incomplete. This passage shows the depth of terminological refinement in later logic.

Key Terms
المقدمة (al-muqaddima): In this passage, muqaddima has two technical usages: (a) a proposition made part of a syllogism, and (b) what the correctness of a proof depends on, including premises and their conditions. [Note: In early logic (e.g., Ibn Sīnā), muqaddima is simply a premise of a syllogism; later commentators debate its broader or narrower scope.]
القياس (al-qiyās): Here, qiyas is debated: some restrict it to categorical syllogism (opposed to induction and analogy), others extend it to include all three. [Note: In falsafa, qiyas often means syllogism; in uṣūl al-fiqh it means analogy by 'illa.]
الحجة (al-ḥujja): Proof or argument, broader than qiyas, includes analogy and induction. In this text, it is used as an alternative term for the thing of which the premise is a part. [Note: No significant shift; stable in logical works.]
الاستقراء (al-istiqrā'): Induction; a type of inference that is not a categorical syllogism, but still considered a form of proof. [Note: In Ibn Sīnā, istiqra' is a valid but less certain inference; later logicians often classify it under 'proof'.]
التمثيل (al-tamthīl): Analogy; a type of inference similar to but distinct from categorical syllogism. [Note: In falsafa, tamthil is often considered rhetorical, but in logic it is a separate form of argument.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some commentators think Ibn Sina used 'qiyas' narrowly?
A) Because induction and analogy are not forms of proof
B) Because he intended to emphasize the importance of categorical syllogism
C) Because he later adds 'or proof' as a correction
D) Because the term 'qiyas' in Arabic never includes induction

Key Takeaway
The scope of muqaddima in Ibn Sina's definition is debated: some restrict it to categorical syllogism, others include induction and analogy; the conjunction 'or' is the crux.

?

What Is Mantiq?

Critiquing the Second Definition of Muqaddima
Dassuqi and al-Attar refine the definition of muqaddima, distinguishing its usage in qiyas from its broader meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection raised against the second definition of muqaddima ('what correctness depends on') and explain why the attempted restriction 'without mediation' is problematic.

Core Idea
The second definition fails because it is either too broad (includes subjects and predicates) or too narrow (excludes remote premises).

The second meaning of muqaddima is 'what the correctness of a proof depends on, including premises and their conditions.' But a critic objects that this would include subjects and predicates, which are not premises. The defender adds 'without mediation' to exclude them, but then remote premises (premises of premises) are also excluded, making the definition too narrow. This reveals the difficulty of giving a precise definition.

Guided Path
1
State the second definition
It is 'mā yatawaqqafu ʻalayhi ṣiḥḥat al-dalīl' (what correctness of proof depends on).
2
Identify the objection
The definition is not exclusive (ghayr māniʻ) because it includes subjects and predicates (al-mawḍūʻāt wa al-maḥmūlāt), which are not premises.
3
Examine the attempted fix
The defender adds 'bilā wāsiṭah' (without mediation) to exclude subjects and predicates.
4
Identify the counter-objection
This restriction excludes remote premises, e.g., premises of premises, which are also necessary for the proof's correctness, so the definition becomes too narrow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه الشُّروع مقدّمةُ عِلم، وحينئذ؛ فيكون ما يتوقف عليه الشروع مراداً من مدلول المقدّمة، وقيد بقولِهِ: هَهُنا؛ أي: في هذا الموضع للاحتراز عن المقدمة في باب القياس، فإنَّها تُطلق على قضيَّةٍ جُعِلَتْ جزء قياس وتطلق على ما يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، فتتناولُ مقدّماتِ الأدلة، وشرائطها؛ كَإيجاب الصغرى، وكُليَّةِ الكُبرى.

العطار: قياس أو حُجَّة، وقد تطلقُ ويُراد بها ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَةُ الدليل؛ فتتناول مقدّماتِ الأدلة وشرائطها كإيجاب الصُّغرى وفعليَّتها، وكُلّيَّة الكُبرى في الشكل الأول مثلاً أفادَهُ السَّيّد، وقولُهُ: مَا جُعِلَت جزء قياس ... إلخ، هذه عبارة الشيخ في الإشارات، فإِنَّهُ قالَ فيهِ: إذا أوردت القضايا في مثل هذا الشَّيء الذي يُسمَّى قياساً أو استقراء أو تمثيلاً؛ سُمِّيت حينئذٍ مُقدّمات، والمقدّمة قضيَّةٌ جُعِلت جزء قياس أو حجة ا.هـ. واختلفَ النَّاظرونَ في كلامِهِ؛ فقال بعضُهُم: لعلَّ الشَّيخَ أرادَ بالقياس ما يتناولُ الأقسام الثَّلاثةَ، فأردفَهُ بقوله: أو حجَّةً ترديداً في العبارة وتخييراً في اللفظ؛ دفعاً لِمَا يتوهَّمُ من اختصاص القياسِ هَهُنا لِمَا يقابلُ القِسْمَينِ الأخيرين، وأرادَ بالقياسِ هَهُنا مَا يُقابل القسمين الآخرين؛ إشارة إلى شدَّةِ الاهتمام بِهِ؛ لأنَّهُ العُمدة في بابِ الاستدلال، فكانَ مَا عداه بالنَّسبة إليهِ مُلحقاً بالعدم، ثم أضرب عنه إلى قوله: أو حُجَّةٌ؛ إفادةٌ لِمَا هو الاصطلاح، ولأنَّ المقصود إذا أدّي بهذا النوع من العبارة؛ كانَ وقع في النَّفْسِ، وعلى هذا؛ تكون كلمة «أو» بمعنى «بل»، ومَا قِيلَ في توجيه هذا العطف المستصعب من أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ اصطلاح، والمعنى: جُعِلَتْ جزء قياس على اصطلاح، أو حجَّةً على اصطلاح؛ فيمكن المناقشةُ فيهِ بأنَّهُ خِلافُ الواقع ا.هـ. والذي اختاره عبد الحكيم أنَّ التُّرديد للإشارة إلى تعددِ الاصطلاح، فقيل: إنَّها مختصة بالقياس، وقيل: إِنَّهَا غيرُ مختصَّةٍ بِهِ؛ وتُقالُ لِمَا جعلت جزءً حَجَّةِ التَّمثيل والاستقراء أيضاً، وأورد على تفسير المقدّمةِ الثَّاني، وهو ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَّةُ الدَّليل ... إلخ؛ بأنَّهُ غيرُ مانع لشموله الموضوعات والمحمولات، وأُجيب بأنَّ المعنى: ما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ صحَّةُ الدَّليل بلا واسطةٍ فَلَمْ يدخل، فإنَّ صحةَ الدَّليل مُتوقفةٌ عليها بواسطة تركُبِ مقدّماتِهِ منها، وفيه أنَّ هذا القيد يُخرج المقدّماتِ البعيدةَ للدَّليل؛ فيصيرُ.
Upon it (the science) commencement is the introduction of a science. And then, what the commencement depends on is intended from the meaning of introduction. And he restricted it by his saying: 'here', i.e., in this place, to exclude the introduction in the chapter of syllogism, for it is applied to a proposition made part of a syllogism, and it is applied to what the correctness of the proof depends on, so it includes the premises of proofs and their conditions, like the positivity of the minor and the universality of the major.

Al-Attar: 'syllogism or proof.' And it may be applied and intended by it what the correctness of the proof depends on, so it includes the premises of proofs and their conditions, like the positivity of the minor and its actuality, and the universality of the major in the first figure for example, as stated by al-Sayyid. And his saying: 'what is made part of a syllogism... etc.' This is the expression of al-Shaykh in al-Isharat, for he said therein: 'When you set forth propositions in such a thing that is called syllogism, induction, or analogy, they are then called premises, and a premise is a proposition made part of a syllogism or a proof.' End quote. And the commentators differed regarding his words. Some of them said: Perhaps al-Shaykh intended by 'syllogism' what includes the three divisions, so he appended to it by his saying 'or proof' as a hesitation in expression and a choice in wording, to ward off what might be imagined of the specificity of syllogism here to what is opposed to the other two divisions. And he intended by 'syllogism' here what is opposed to the other two divisions, as an indication of the intensity of attention to it, because it is the mainstay in the chapter of inference, so what is other than it is, relative to it, attached to non-existence. Then he turned away from it to his saying 'or proof' to indicate what is the convention, and because the intended meaning, if it is conveyed by this type of expression, has an impact on the soul. And on this, the word 'or' means 'rather'. And what was said in justifying this difficult conjunction, that each one is a convention, and the meaning is: made part of a syllogism according to one convention, or a proof according to another convention, can be debated as being contrary to reality. End quote. And what Abd al-Hakim chose is that the hesitation indicates multiplicity of conventions. So it was said: it is specific to syllogism, and it was said: it is not specific to it, and it is said for what is made part of a proof of analogy and induction as well. And objection was raised against the second interpretation of premise, which is 'what the correctness of the proof depends on... etc.' that it is not exclusive because it includes subjects and predicates. And it was answered that the meaning is: what the correctness of the proof depends on without mediation, so they are not included, for the correctness of the proof depends on them through the composition of its premises from them. And in this [answer] it is [objected] that this restriction excludes the remote premises of the proof; so it becomes...

What Is Mantiq?

Why the Explanation of Need Gives a Descriptive Definition (Rasm) Rather Than a Proper Definition (Hadd)
Commentator explains that the explanation of need leads to a descriptive definition (rasm) of logic, not a proper definition (hadd).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a proper definition (hadd) and a descriptive definition (rasm) in the context of defining the science of logic, and identify which type is implied by the explanation of need (bayan al-haja).

Core Idea
Purpose-based definitions are descriptive, not essential—the need explanation gives a rasm, not a hadd.

In this passage, the commentator al-Dasuqi unpacks the phrase 'al-munsaqu' from the matn and shows why the opening explanation of logic's necessity only yields a descriptive definition (rasm), not a full essential definition (hadd). He also addresses a subtle objection: how can a judgment (tasdiq) about need lead to a conception (tasawwur) of logic? The answer lies in the distinction between implication (istilzam) and explicit inference (istintaj). This lesson will help you see how definitions in Islamic logic are carefully calibrated—they tell you what something does before they tell you what it is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms: hadd vs. rasm
Hadd is a definition that gives the essence (genus + differentia). Rasm is a description that identifies something by its properties or effects. In this passage, the commentator argues that the need explanation only provides a rasm.
Memorize the distinction: hadd = essence; rasm = description by attributes.
2
Locate the phrase 'al-munsaqu' and its significance
The matn uses 'al-munsaqu' (that which flows) to describe how the need explanation leads to a definition. The commentator explains that choosing 'yansaqu' over 'yasuqu' emphasizes that the implication is natural and apparent, not a forced inference.
'Yansaqu' implies automatic implication; 'yasuqu' would imply active leading.
3
Understand the objection about tasdiq and tasawwur
An objector says: the need explanation is a judgment (tasdiq) about necessity, while a rasm is a conception (tasawwur). Normally, you go from conception to judgment, not the opposite. The commentator answers that the need explanation implies the rasm without being an explicit derivation; it is istilzam (implication), not istintaj (inference).
This distinction protects the coherence of the argument: implication does not require the same epistemological order as proof.
4
Apply the distinction to the definition of logic
The commentator shows that the need explanation ends at describing logic as 'a canonical instrument that protects the mind from error in thought.' This description is a rasm because it gives the purpose (ghaya), not the essence (mahiyya). Hence, the introduction defines logic by its function.
Check: does a rasm need to include genus? In this case, 'instrument' (ala) functions as a broad category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُنْسَاقُ) صفةٌ لبيان ؛ أي: المؤدّي إلى تعريف عِلْم المنطقِ بالرَّسمِ؛ لأنَّ بيان الحاجةِ يستلزم تعريفَهُ بالرَّسم لا بالحدِّ؛ لأنَّ فائدتَهُ عصمتُه الفكر عن الخطأ، وهذا يستلزم تعريفه، وهو آلة قانونية تعصم الذهن عن الخطأ في في الفكرِ كَمَا سبق . العطار (قَوْلُهُ: يَنْسَاقُ) أي : يستلزم، وإنَّما عبر بالانسياق؛ إشارة إلى ظهورِ اللُّزومِ، بِخلافِ مَا لو عبَّرَ بِيسوقُ ؛ فربَّما يتوهَّمُ المعاناة، ففي اختيار الانسياق إشارة إلى أَنَّ استلزامَهُ إِيَّاهُ غيرُ مدخل لتحرير المصنفِ ، أفاده عبد الحكيم، ويعني بذلك التحرير قول صاحبِ الشَّمسيَّةِ : العِلْمُ إِمَّا تَصوُّرٌ، وإِمَّا تَصورٌ معه حكم، إلى قوله : فمسَّتِ الحاجة إلى قانون يعصم عنه، وهو المنطقُ ، وقد اختصرها المصنِّفُ هنا ؛ فَآخِرُ مَا ينساقُ إليه بيان الحاجة ؛ أَنَّهُ مَسَّتِ الحاجة إلى قانون يفيد عصمة الذهنِ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وهو لازِم محمولٌ مُساءٍ للمنطق، ولذا ؛ قال المصنِّفُ : وهو المنطقُ . فثبتَ أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ مُتضمِّنُ لتعريف المنطقِ برسمِهِ، وأمَّا التَّعريفُ ؛ فلا يستلزم ببيان الحاجة؛ لجواز أن يكونَ الرَّسم بشيءٍ آخر دونَ غايتِهِ، لا يُقالُ: إِنَّ بيان الحاجة مِن قبيل التّصديقِ والرَّسم تصوُّرُ ؛ فكيف يتوصَّلُ للتَّصورِ بالتصديقِ، مع أنَّ الواقع العكس؟ والجواب : أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ ينتهي إلى الرسم ويستلزمه، ولا يلزمُ من ذلك أن يكونَ مُوصلاً له، فهذا استلزام لا استنتاج
(His saying: 'al-munsaqu') is an adjective for 'bayan'; i.e. that which leads to the definition of the science of logic by way of a description (rasm); because the explanation of the necessity necessitates its definition by a description, not by a proper definition (hadd); because its benefit is its protecting the mind from error, and this necessitates its definition, and it is a legal instrument that protects the mind from error in thought, as previously mentioned. al-'Attar: (His saying: 'yansaqu') i.e. necessitates, and he only expressed it with 'yansaqu' (lit. flows) to indicate the apparentness of the implication, unlike if he had expressed it with 'yasuqu' (leads); for perhaps a struggle might be imagined. So in the choice of 'yansaqu' is an indication that its necessitation of it is not an introduction to the author's editing. 'Abd al-Hakim conveyed this, and by that editing he means the statement of the author of al-Shamsiyya: 'Knowledge is either a conception or a conception with a judgment...' up to his saying: 'so the need arose for a canon that protects from it, and that is logic.' The author abbreviated it here; so the furthest point to which the explanation of the need flows is that the need arose for a canon that benefits the protection of the mind from error in thought, and that is a necessary attribute predicated of logic. Hence the author said: 'and that is logic.' So it is established that the explanation of the need comprises a definition of logic by its description (rasm). As for the definition (hadd), it does not necessitate the explanation of the need; because it is possible that the description is by something else besides its purpose. It cannot be said: 'The explanation of the need is of the type of assent (tasdiq) and the description (rasm) is a conception (tasawwur); so how is a conception reached through assent, while the actual situation is the opposite?' The answer: that the explanation of the need ends at the description and necessitates it, but it does not follow from that that it leads to it; this is an implication, not an inference.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the 'explanation of need' (bayan al-haja) in the introduction does not give a precise definition (hadd) of logic but rather implies a descriptive definition (rasm). The commentator, al-Dasuqi, explains that the phrase 'al-munsaqu' (that which flows) refers to how the need explanation leads to a definition by describing logic's purpose: protecting the mind from error. This description suffices to identify what logic is. The choice of 'yansaqu' (flows) over 'yasuqu' (leads) is deliberate: it suggests a natural implication (istilzam) rather than a forced inference. The commentator cites the text of al-Shamsiyya to show the standard phrasing. An objection is raised: the need explanation is a judgment (tasdiq) while a description (rasm) is a conception (tasawwur); how can a judgment yield a conception? The answer distinguishes between logical implication (istilzam) and inferential derivation (istintaj). The need explanation implies the description without being an explicit inference. This distinction is important for understanding the role of the introduction: it sets the stage without giving a full definition.

Key Terms
منساق
رسم
حد
استلزام
استنتاج

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that the explanation of need (bayan al-haja) leads to a definition by rasm rather than hadd?
A) Because logic cannot be defined by hadd.
B) Because the need explanation only describes logic's purpose, not its essence.
C) Because the author preferred rasm over hadd.
D) Because the need explanation is a tasdiq and rasm is a tasawwur.

Key Takeaway
The explanation of need (bayan al-haja) implies a descriptive definition (rasm) of logic by its purpose, not an essential definition (hadd), and this implication (istilzam) is distinct from explicit inference (istintaj).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following definition of 'qiyas' (analogy) in usul al-fiqh is a hadd or a rasm: 'Qiyas is the application of a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a shared effective cause ('illah).' Explain your reasoning using the distinction in this lesson.

Defining 'Ilm as Absolute Perception
Author defines 'ilm as absolute perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of 'ilm as absolute perception and explain why the qualifier 'absolutely' is necessary in this definition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is perception without restriction—neither to conception nor assent.

The matn opens by defining 'ilm (knowledge) simply as 'perception absolutely.' This definition is the foundation of Islamic logic's epistemology. In this lesson, we unpack what this definition includes and excludes, and why the qualification 'absolutely' is crucial to avoid narrowing knowledge to a single type.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
The term being defined is 'ilm. Identify it in the Arabic opening (العِلْم).
It appears right after the matn's introduction.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is idrak (perception). The differentia is 'absolutely' (mutlaqan), which removes any restriction.
Restrictions would be like restricting to perception of a single concept (tasawwur) or judgment (tasdiq).
3
Understand why the qualifier is added
The commentator says it is to make the definition correct: without it, one might think 'ilm is only one specific type of perception.
Absolute perception covers both tasawwur and tasdiq, and even other forms.
4
Contrast with alternative definitions
The passage later discusses that some restrict 'ilm to acquired knowledge (husuli), excluding presential (huduri). Here we see the baseline definition is broad.

مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ ( العِلْمُ) وهو : الإدراكُ مُطلقاً الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : الْعِلْمُ أي : الحادثُ ؛ لأنَّهُ المنقسم للأقسام المذكورة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْإِدْرَاكُ مُطْلَقَاً) أي: من غير تقييد له بكونه إدراك مفرد، أو إدراك وقوع الـ النسبة أو لا وقوعها، فالمراد مُطلقُ الإدراك، وإنَّما قيَّدَهُ بِالإطلاق؛ ليصح العطار (قَوْلُهُ: العِلْمُ وَهُوَ : الْإِدْرَاكُ مُطْلَقَاً) أي: سواءٌ كان على وجه الإذعان أو لا، بناءً على أنَّ المنقَسِمَ إلى التَّصوُّرِ والتَّصديقِ؛ هو العِلْمُ الحادث الحصولي، لا مُطلَقُ العِلْمِ الشَّاملِ للحضوري والقديم؛ لأنَّ الانقسام إلى البديهي والكسبي إنَّما يجري في العِلْم الحصوليّ والعِلْم الحادِثِ ؛ دونَ العِلْم الحضوري والعِلْم القديم، وَهُوَ عِلْمُهُ تَعالى، فإنَّ العِلْمَ الحضوري بديهي ، وعِلْمُهُ تَعَالَى لا يُوصَفُ بِبَداهِةٍ وَلَا كَسب، وهذا ما اختاره جماعةٌ مِنَ الفُضلاء المحققين كالمصنفِ والسَّيِّدِ والقُطبِ الرَّازي في رسالتِهِ المؤلَّفة في تحقيقِ التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ، والعلامة الشيرازي في درَّةِ النَّاحِ وشرح حكمةِ الإشراق، واختار الجلالُ الدَّوَّانيُّ في حاشية المتنِ التَّعميم فقال : هو مطلقُ الصُّورةِ الحاضرة عند المدرك ؛ سواء كانت عين ماهيته؛ وهو في التَّصوُّرِ بالكُنْهِ أو غيرها ؛ وهو في غيره، وسواء كانت تلكَ الصُّورةُ غيرَ الصُّورةِ الخارجيَّة؛ وهو في العِلْم الحصوليّ أو عينها وهو العِلْمُ الحضوري، وسواء كانت في ذاتِ المدركِ كما في علم النَّفْسِ بالكليَّاتِ، أو في آلاتها؛ كما في عليها بالمحسوسات، وسواء كانت عين المدركِ كما في عِلْم الباري تَعَالى شأنُهُ بذاتِهِ أو غيره كما في علمِهِ بِسلسلة الممكناتِ، وقد يخصُّ هَهُنا بالعلم الحصوليّ أو الحادثِ مُعلِّلاً بأنَّ الانقسام إلى البديهية والكسبيَّةِ إنَّما يجري فيهما، ولا حاجة إليه ؛ فإنَّ الانقسام يجري في المطلق، وإن لم يجرِ في كل نوع منه على أنَّهُ تخصيص اللَّفْظِ مِن غير ضرورة داعية إليه، مع أنَّ التَّعميم أنسبُ بقواعد الفنّ ا.هـ. وأشار بقولِهِ : فإِنَّ الانقسام ... إلخ؛ لدفع ما عساه يقالُ : إِنَّ التَّعميم لهذهِ الأفرادِ ينافيهِ التَّقسيم، وحَاصِلُ الجوابِ أَنَّهُ يجوز أن يكون المقسمُ مُطلقاً، وجريان الأقسام فيهِ لا يستلزم جريانها في كل نوع منه ؛ إذْ لا يلزمُ مِنَ انقسام المطلقِ انقسامُ أنواعِهِ كُلّها ، وإلا ؛ لزمَ في كلِّ تقسيم انقسامُ الشَّيء إلى نفْسِهِ وإلى غَيْرِهِ، والحقُّ مَا
The Introduction of the Author: (Knowledge) is: perception absolutely. (Dasuqi) His statement: 'Knowledge' means: the occurrent [knowledge]; because it is what is divided into the mentioned divisions. His statement: 'And it is perception absolutely' means: without restriction to it being a perception of an individual or a perception of the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence. So what is meant is absolute perception. He only restricted it with 'absolutely' to make it correct. (al-?Attar) His statement: 'Knowledge and it is perception absolutely' means: whether it is in the mode of assent or not, based on the fact that what is divided into tasawwur and tasdiq is occurrent acquired knowledge, not absolute knowledge that includes presential and eternal [knowledge]; because the division into the intuitive and the acquired only applies to acquired knowledge and occurrent knowledge, not to presential knowledge and eternal knowledge, which is His knowledge, exalted. For presential knowledge is intuitive, and His knowledge is not described as intuitive or acquired. This is what a group of the virtuous investigators chose, like the author, al-Sayyid, al-Qutb al-Razi in his treatise on the investigation of tasawwur and tasdiq, and the scholar al-Shirazi in his work 'Durrat al-Najah' and his commentary on Hikmat al-Ishraq. And al-Jalal al-Dawani chose in his gloss on the matn generalization, saying: It is the absolute form present to the perceiver, whether it is the very essence of it, which is in tasawwur bi al-kunh, or other, which is in other than that; and whether that form is other than the external form, which is in acquired knowledge, or its very self, which is presential knowledge; and whether it is in the essence of the perceiver as in the soul's knowledge of universals, or in its instruments as in its knowledge of sensibles; and whether it is the very essence of the perceiver as in the knowledge of the Creator, may His status be exalted, of His essence, or other as in His knowledge of the chain of contingent things. And it may be specified here to acquired or occurrent knowledge, on the grounds that the division into intuitive and acquired only applies to them, but there is no need for [that specification]; for the division applies to the absolute, even if it does not apply to every type of it, and it is a specification of the word without necessity compelling it, and generalization is more appropriate to the rules of the discipline. He indicated by his statement: 'for the division...' etc. to repel what might be said: that generalization to these individuals contradicts the division. The summary of the answer is that it is permissible for the divided to be absolute, and the running of the divisions within it does not entail their running in every type of it; otherwise it would necessitate in every division the division of a thing into itself and into other. And the truth is...
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of 'ilm (knowledge) as the starting point of the matn. The author defines it simply as 'perception absolutely.' The commentators then explain the nuances. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that this knowledge is the occurrent (hadith) knowledge, because it is what is divided into the categories. Al-?Attar expands, explaining that the definition is unrestricted to distinguish it from being restricted to single perception or judgment. Then he discusses a major disagreement: some scholars restrict 'ilm here to acquired (hudhuli) and occurrent knowledge, excluding presential (hudhuri) and divine knowledge, because the division into necessary and theoretical only applies to acquired knowledge. Others, like al-Dawani, generalize the definition to include all forms of perception: presential, divine, sensory, universal. They argue that the division into necessary and theoretical does not have to apply to every type; the absolute can be divided even if some types are not subdivided by that division. The text ends with a defense of generalization, stating that it is more consistent with the rules of logic.

Key Terms
العِلْم
الإدراك
مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why did the author specify 'absolutely' in the definition of 'ilm?
A) To exclude acquired knowledge.
B) To make the definition correct by not restricting it to a specific type of perception.
C) To limit knowledge to conception only.
D) To include only divine knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The matn defines 'ilm as absolute perception, a broad genus that requires no further restriction at this stage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition of 'ilm given by al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani in his gloss on the Tahdhib: 'Ilm is a perception that is decisive and corresponds to reality.' Compare with the absolute definition.
?
Using the definition from this lesson, explain whether the knowledge of Allah (divine knowledge) fits the definition of 'ilm as absolute perception.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
7 min
The Scope of 'Ilm: Acquired vs Presential Knowledge
Scholars debate whether 'ilm includes presential and divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the arguments for and against restricting the definition of 'ilm to acquired knowledge, and identify the logical principle used to defend the broader definition.

Core Idea
The division of a term does not require division of every one of its types.

After defining 'ilm as absolute perception, the commentators raise a critical dispute: does this definition cover only acquired (husuli) and occurrent knowledge, or does it include presential (huduri) and divine knowledge as well? Al-Dawani defends the broader definition using a logical principle about the division of absolute terms. This lesson walks through the arguments and the key objection that restricts the scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
One group (al-Sayyid, al-Qutb, al-Shirazi) restricts 'ilm here to acquired/occurrent. Another (al-Dawani) generalizes to all perception, including presential and divine.
The passage names scholars on both sides.
2
Understand the restriction's rationale
The restrictive side argues that the division into necessary and acquired (the divisions of the matn) only applies to acquired knowledge, so the defined term must be that.
3
Examine the generalization's argument
Al-Dawani says the division can apply to the absolute term even if not to every type. He gives the logical principle: the division of the absolute does not entail division of each type.
This is the key logical tool used.
4
Assess the rebuttal and conclusion
The text indicates that generalization is more appropriate to the rules of logic, and that restricting without necessity is arbitrary.
The passage ends with 'the truth is...' (unfinished), but the argument for generalization is presented as stronger.

What logical principle does al-Dawani use to defend the generalization of the definition of 'ilm?

The Judgmental Relation: Definition and Terminological Nuance
Author defines the judgmental relation and discusses its terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) and the informatory relation, and explain the commentator's preference for the latter.

Core Idea
The judgmental relation is not inherently judgmental; it becomes so only through the act of judgment.

This passage unpacks the term 'judgmental relation' and offers a subtle terminological critique. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how propositions are constructed in Islamic logic. We will walk through the definition, the role of judgment, and why the commentator suggests a better term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'al-nisba al-hukmiyya' in the Arabic. What is it being compared to?
The commentator equates it to the relation of predicate to subject in a nominal sentence.
2
Understand the components of judgment
Judgment (hukm) is the apprehension that the relation is occurring or not. The relation is the locus of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salab).
3
Analyze the terminological critique
The commentator argues that 'nisba khabariyya' (informatory relation) would be better because the relation is not inherently judgmental; judgment attaches to it after the fact.
The relation exists before judgment, but the attribute 'judgmental' depends on the act of judging.
4
Contrast the two scholarly views
One view holds that the judgmental relation is the affirmation of predicate to subject in both affirmative and negative propositions. Another view says it differs: affirmation in affirmatives, negation in negatives.
The commentator follows the second view, as indicated by his later statement.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : لِلنِّسْبَةِ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ كَثُبُوتِ الْخَبَرِ لِلْمُبْتَدَأِ أَيْ : إِدْرَاكاً ; لِكَوْنِ النَّسْبَةِ وَاقِعَةً أَوَّلاً , وَالْحُكْمِيَّةِ : نِسْبَةٌ لِلْحُكْمِ ; لِكَوْنِهَا مُتَعَلَّقَةً , فَهِيَ مَوْرِدُ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلَّبِ الْمُعَيَّرِ عَنْهُمَا بِالْإِيقَاعِ وَالْإِنْتِزَاعِ ; وَهُوَ إِدْرَاكُ عَدَمِ الْوُقُوعِ . وَإِدْرَاكُ الْوُقُوعِ . وَبِعِبَارَةٍ أُخْرَى : قَوْلُهُ : لِلنِّسَبَةِ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ ; أَيْ : الْمَنْسُوبَةِ لِلْحُكْمِ لِتَعَلُّقِهِ بِهَا ; لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ إِدْرَاكُ أَنَّ النِّسْبَةَ وَاقِعَةٌ أَوْ لَيْسَتْ بِوَاقِعَةٍ , وَلَا تَتَّصِفُ النِّسْبَةُ حَقِيقَةً بِكَوْنِهَا حُكْمِيَّةٌ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَلُّقِ الْحُكْمِ بِهَا لَا قَبْلَهُ , فَلَوْ قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ : لِلنِّسَبَةِ الْخَبَرِيَّةِ بَدَلَ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ ; كَانَ أَوْلَى . وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْحُكْمِيَّةُ هِيَ ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ وَالسَّالِبَةِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ . وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهَا ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ فِي الْمُوجَبَةِ , وَانْتِفَاءُ الْمَحْمُولِ عَنِ الْمَوْضُوعِ فِي السَّالِبَةِ , وَعَلَيْهِ مَشَى الشَّارِحُ فِيمَا يَأْتِي فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَلَا شَكَّ أَنَّ مَنْ أَدْرَكَ ... إِلَخْ )
His statement: 'for the judgmental relation' like the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, i.e. as an apprehension, because the relation is occurring first, and 'judgmental' is a relation to the judgment because it is related to it, so it is the locus of affirmation and negation expressed by positing and removal, which is the apprehension of non-occurrence and the apprehension of occurrence. In other words: His statement: 'for the judgmental relation' i.e. the one attributed to judgment because of its attachment to it; because judgment is the apprehension that the relation is occurring or not occurring, and the relation is not truly described as judgmental except after the judgment attaches to it, not before. So if the author had said 'for the informatory relation' instead of 'judgmental', it would have been better. And the judgmental relation is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in the affirmative and negative proposition according to the investigators. And it was said: it is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in the affirmative, and the negation of the predicate from the subject in the negative, and upon this the commentator proceeded in what follows in his statement: 'And there is no doubt that whoever apprehends... etc.'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the phrase 'for the judgmental relation' (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) from the matn. The commentator begins by equating it to the relation of predicate to subject in an Arabic nominal sentence (khabar to mubtada). He explains that the relation itself is the object of apprehension, and the term 'judgmental' indicates that this relation is the concern of judgment. The relation is the locus of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salab), expressed as 'positing' (iqa') and 'removal' (intiza'). The commentator then rephrases: the judgmental relation is that which is related to judgment because judgment is the apprehension that the relation is either occurring or not occurring. The relation does not become judgmental until after the judgment attaches to it. Therefore, the author would have done better to say 'informatory relation' (nisba khabariyya) instead, because the relation itself is not inherently judgmental but becomes so through judgment. Finally, two views are presented regarding what the judgmental relation is: according to the investigators (muhaqqiqun), it is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in both affirmative and negative propositions (i.e., it is a single relation that can be either affirmed or denied). Another view, followed by the commentator, holds that it is the affirmation in affirmatives and the negation in negatives, meaning the relation differs between the two types. This passage clarifies a key terminological point about the nature of the subject-predicate relation in propositions.

Key Terms
النسبة الحكمية
الحكم
الإيجاب
السلب
القضية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would it have been better if the author said 'informatory relation' instead of 'judgmental relation'?
A) Because the relation is not inherently judgmental until judgment attaches to it.
B) Because all judgments are about information.
C) Because the term 'judgmental' is only used in negative propositions.
D) Because the relation itself is always true.

Key Takeaway
The judgmental relation is the nexus of subject and predicate that becomes the object of judgment; its proper designation is 'informatory' to emphasize its independence from the act of judging.

What Is Mantiq?
