Defining Necessary Knowledge: Concept vs. Instances
Author defines necessary knowledge and defends against the objection that definition makes it theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why defining necessary knowledge does not make it theoretical, using the distinction between universal concept and particular instances.

Core Idea
Defining a universal concept does not turn its instances into theoretical knowledge.

This passage tackles a classic objection: if you define necessary (self-evident) knowledge, you are using reasoning, so you have turned it into theoretical knowledge. The commentator shows that the definition applies to the concept, not to the actual instances, so the objection fails. Understanding this distinction is crucial for any discussion of epistemology in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition offered
Find the definition of necessary knowledge: it is that which does not depend on consideration (naẓar) or acquisition (kasb). Note that the definition uses the word 'necessity' but refers to the 'necessary' knowledge.
The commentator explains the grammatical gender shift to avoid circularity.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is: if you define necessary knowledge, you are using reasoning (the definition itself requires thought), so the defined entity becomes theoretical, contradicting its definition.
This is a common paradox: defining the self-evident seems to make it non-self-evident.
3
Locate the commentator's key distinction
The commentator replies that the definition applies to the universal concept (mafhūm kullī) of necessary knowledge, not to its particular instances (afrād). The concept is theoretical (since it is defined), but the instances remain necessary.
The passage quotes ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm to reinforce this distinction.
4
Apply the distinction to resolve the contradiction
Realize that 'necessary knowledge depends' (the concept depends on definition) and 'necessary knowledge does not depend' (the instances do not depend on reasoning) refer to different subjects. No contradiction arises because the two propositions are about different things.
This resolution preserves both the self-evidence of the instances and the possibility of defining the concept.

Source Text

وهي الدسوقي التي لم يتوقف حصولها على نَظَرٍ وكَسْبٍ . (قَوْلُهُ: وَهِيَ الَّتِي مَا لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ) أي : والضَّرورة بمعنى الضروري: العِلْمُ الَّذِي لا يتوقَّف؛ أي: الصُّورة الحاصلة في العقل التي لا يتوقف حصولها فيه على نظر ... إلخ ، وإنَّما أَنَّكَ الضَّمير وعبَّر بالتي ؛ نظراً لِلّفظِ الضَّرورة لا لمعناها المراد منها، وهو الضَّروريُّ ؛ إذْ لَوْ نظر لِذلكَ ؛ لذكَّرَ الضَّمير والموصول. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهِيَ الَّتِي الضَّمير يعودُ للضَّرورة، والموصول المتبادرُ منه وقوعه على الضَّرورةِ، وهو فاسد ؛ لأدائِهِ لأخذِ الشَّيء جنساً في تعريف نفْسِهِ، ولا محيص عنه إلا بدعوَى وُقُوعِهِ على الصُّورةِ، وهي ، وإن لم تكن مذكورةً؛ لكنَّه يشعرُ بها الضَّرورة الَّتي هِيَ صِفَةٌ لِلعِلْم المفسَّرِ بالصُّورة الحاصِلَةِ عندَ النَّفْسِ، قال عبد الحكيم في تقرير تعريف صاحبِ الشَّمسيَّةِ لِلعِلْم الضَّروريّ بِقولِهِ : وهوَ الَّذي لم يتوقف ... إلخ؛ أي: العِلْمُ بمعنى الصُّورةِ الَّذي لم يتوقف حصوله المعتبر في مفهومِهِ، فَلَا يلزم أن يكونَ للحصولِ حصول، وتعدّيةُ التَّوقُفِ بـ «على» يتضمَّنُ معنى الترتيب؛ فَيُفيدُ قِيدُ التَّوقُفِ أَنَّهُ لولاهُ؛ لَمَا حَصَلَ ، وقَيْدُ التَّرتُبِ التَّقدُّم، فيؤول إلى معنى الاحتياج، فَبِالقَيدِ الأوَّلِ : دخلَ العِلْمُ الضَّروريُّ الَّذي حصلَ بالنَّظَرِ أيضاً كالعِلْم؛ بأن ليس جميعُ التَّصوراتِ والتَّصديقات بديهيا ولا نظريًّا، وبالقيدِ الثَّاني : العِلَّمُ الضَّروريُّ التَّابِعُ للعِلْم النَّظريّ، إذا قُلْنَا : إِنَّهُ ضَروري بمعنى البديهي كَالعِلْمِ بِالعِلْم النَّظريّ ; فَإِنَّهُ وإِنْ كانَ يُصدقُ عليهِ أَنَّهُ لولا النَّظُرُ لَمَا حَصَلَ ; لكنَّهُ ليسَ مُرتَّباً عَلى النَّظَرِ، بل على العِلْم المستَفَادِ مِنَ النَّظرِ ، فإنَّ المتبادرَ مِنَ التَّرتُبِ ; التَّرتُ بِلا واسطةٍ، ثمَّ إِنَّ البديهي والنَّظريّ يختلفان بالنّسبة إلى الأشخاص ، فَرُبَّما يكون نظريًّا لِشخص; بَدِيهِيَّا لِأَخَرَ، وبالعكس , فَقَيدُ الحيثية مُعتبرُ في التَّعريفِ على ما تقرَّرَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ يُعتبرُ في تعريفاتِ الأُمورِ الاعتبارية قيد الحيثية وإن لم نذكر ا.هـ. ملخصاً، لا يُقَالُ: إذا عرف العلم الضروري صار نظريًّا ; لاحتياجه للتَّعريفِ، فينتظِمُ قَضيَّتَانِ مُتَناقِضَتَانِ هُمَا ; الضَّروريُّ : ما لا يتوقَّفُ ... إلخ , الثَّانية : الضَّروري يتوقَّفُ; لأنَّا نقولُ : لا تَنَاقُضَ لاختلافِ الموضوع، فإنَّ قولنا : الضَّروري يتوفَّفُ... إلخ، المرادُ بِهِ المفهوم
And it is al-Dasuqi: [that which] its attainment does not depend on consideration and acquisition. (His saying: 'And it is that which does not depend' means: And necessity in the sense of the necessary: knowledge that does not depend; i.e., the form occurring in the mind whose occurrence in it does not depend on consideration... etc. And indeed he used the feminine pronoun and expressed with 'that' (feminine) with regard to the word 'necessity' not its intended meaning, which is the necessary; for if he considered that, he would have made the pronoun and relative masculine. (His saying: 'And it is that which' the pronoun refers to necessity, and the apparent understanding of the relative is that it falls on necessity, and that is corrupt because it leads to taking the thing as a genus in the definition of itself, and there is no escape from that except by claiming that it falls on the form, and although it is not mentioned, yet necessity, which is an attribute of knowledge interpreted as the form occurring in the soul, suggests it. Abd al-Hakim said in his determination of the definition of the author of al-Shamsiyya for necessary knowledge by his saying: 'It is that which did not depend... etc.' i.e., knowledge in the sense of the form whose occurrence considered in its concept did not depend, so it is not necessary that occurrence has an occurrence. And the transitive of 'depend' with 'on' entails the meaning of order; so the condition of dependence indicates that without it, it would not have occurred, and the condition of order indicates precedence, so it comes to mean need. By the first condition, necessary knowledge that also occurs by consideration enters, like knowledge that not all conceptualizations and assents are intuitive or theoretical. By the second condition, necessary knowledge that follows from theoretical knowledge, if we say it is necessary in the sense of intuitive, like knowledge of theoretical knowledge; for although it is true of it that without consideration it would not have occurred, yet it is not ordered upon consideration, but upon the knowledge obtained from consideration. For the apparent understanding of order is order without intermediary. Then the intuitive and theoretical differ with respect to persons, so perhaps it is theoretical for one person, intuitive for another, and vice versa. So the condition of respect is considered in the definition, as established that the condition of respect is considered in definitions of relational matters even if we do not mention it. Summarized. It is not said: If necessary knowledge is defined, it becomes theoretical because it needs definition, so two contradictory propositions arise: the necessary is what does not depend... etc., and the second: the necessary depends. Because we say: There is no contradiction due to the difference of subject, for our saying 'the necessary depends... etc.' is intended to mean the concept.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī). The commentator al-Dasuqi first presents the definition: it is that which does not depend on consideration (naẓar) or acquisition (kasb). Al-‘Aṭṭār then explains that the word 'necessity' here is used for the lexical meaning, not the intended meaning of 'the necessary', hence the feminine pronoun. He identifies a potential circularity: if the relative clause 'which does not depend' refers back to 'necessity', then one is defining a thing by its own genus. To avoid this, he argues that the relative actually refers to the 'form' (ṣūra) implied by 'necessity', since 'necessity' is an attribute of knowledge which is the form in the soul. This is supported by a quote from ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm interpreting the definition of necessary knowledge from the author of al-Shamsiyya. ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm clarifies that the definition concerns the concept (mafhūm) of knowledge as form, and that 'dependence' with 'on' implies order. He distinguishes two aspects: the condition of dependence (tawaqquf) and the condition of order (tarattub). The first includes knowledge that depends on consideration but still is necessary, like knowing that not all concepts and assents are intuitive or theoretical. The second includes necessary knowledge that follows from theoretical knowledge, such as the knowledge of theoretical knowledge itself—this is not directly ordered on consideration but on the knowledge obtained from consideration. He then notes that intuitive and theoretical vary by person. The passage concludes with an objection: defining necessary knowledge makes it theoretical, leading to a contradiction. The reply is that the contradiction is only apparent because the subject differs: the first proposition ('necessary is what does not depend') refers to instances (afrād), while the second ('necessary depends') refers to the concept (mafhūm). Thus, the definition is of the universal concept, not the individual instances.

Key Terms
الضَّرورة
النَّظَر
المفهوم الكُلِّي
العلم الضروري
الصورة
المعنى الاعتباري

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the objection that defining necessary knowledge makes it theoretical?
A) Because necessary knowledge cannot be defined at all.
B) Because the definition applies to the concept, not to the instances of necessary knowledge.
C) Because theoretical knowledge is a different kind of knowledge.
D) Because the definition uses the same terms as the object.

Key Takeaway
Defining a universal concept does not change the nature of its instances; thus, defining 'necessary knowledge' as a concept does not make any actual necessary knowledge theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'The existence of the world is known necessarily.' Using the concept-instance distinction from this lesson, explain why defining this statement does not make the actual knowledge of the world's existence theoretical.
?
Find a case in Islamic theology where a claim is considered necessary by one school and theoretical by another (e.g., knowledge of the existence of the Creator). Apply the conditions of dependence and order from the commentary to analyze whether the claim fits the definition of necessary knowledge.

Resolving the Objection: Defining the Universal Concept
Author resolves objection about defining necessary matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defining an individual and defining a universal concept, and apply this distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in definitions from Islamic scholarly texts.

Core Idea
Defining individuals is impossible; defining the universal concept is necessary.

In logic, a common objection arises: if necessary matters cannot be defined, how can we define 'necessary knowledge'? The author clarifies that this objection confuses the individuals (afrād) with the universal concept (mafhūm kullī). Definitions in logic are of universals, not particulars. This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions function in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that necessary matters (like necessary knowledge) cannot be defined. Why would someone think that? What is the apparent contradiction?
2
Distinguish individuals from universal concept
The author says 'the individuals of the necessary are not defined' but the universal concept that applies to those individuals is definable. What is the difference between an individual (e.g., this specific necessary knowledge in your mind) and the universal concept (the definition of necessary knowledge)?
In logic, individuals are unique and cannot be captured by a definition; only the common meaning can be defined.
3
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Take the concept of 'wudū' in fiqh. The universal concept of wudū' is defined (specific actions, intention, etc.), but each individual performance of wudū' is not defined per se. How does this parallel the necessary knowledge example?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ إنْ قُلْتَ: الأمورُ الضَّروريّةُ لا تُعرَّف، فكيف عرَّفَ الضَّرورةَ بِقولِهِ : (وَهِيَ الَّتي . . . إلخ)؟ . قُلْتُ : : معنى قولهم : الأمورُ الضَّروريّةُ لَا تُعرَّف أنَّ الأفراد لِلضَّروريَّةِ لا تُعرَّف، وهذا لا يُنافي أنَّ المفهوم الكُلِّيَّ الصَّادق على تلك الأفرادِ يُعرَّف، وما هنا تعريفٌ لِلمفهوم الكُلِّيّ لَا لِفرد من أفراده، وقولُهُ: مَا لَا يتوقَّفُ حصولها على نظر) : هو ترتيب أمورٍ معلومةٍ لِلتَّأدِّي إلى مجهول، والمراد بالكسبِ: الترتيب المذكورُ ، وحينئذٍ ; فالعطفُ مُرادف . ثمَّ إِنَّ كلامَهُ صادقٌ بأنْ لا يتوقَّفُ على شيءٍ أصلاً كإدراك أنَّ الواحد نصفُ الاثنين، وَبِمَا إذا توقَّفَ على حدس كَإدراكِ أنَّ نور القمرِ مُستفاد من نورِ الشَّمس، أو تجربة كَإدراكِ أَنَّ السَّقمونيا مُسهَّلةٌ للصَّفراء، وحينئذ ; فيدخلُ في الضَّروريَّاتِ القضايا الأوَّليَّة، والحدسيَّة والتَّجريبيَّة والضَّروري بهذا المعنى مُرادف للبديهي، وقد يُطلقُ البديهي على ما لا يُتوقَّفُ على شيءٍ أصلاً , فيكون أخصَّ من الضَّروري بالمعنى المذكور; لانفرادِ الضَّروريّ حينئذٍ بالحدسيَّاتِ والتَّجربيات. واعلَمْ أنَّ الضَّرورة في التَّصوراتِ ظاهرةٌ، وأمَّا التصديقُ ; فالمراد بالضَّروريّ منه أن يكون الحكم بعد تصوُّرِ الطَّرفين غير مُتوقِّفٍ على نَظَر، وإِنْ كَانَ تَصوُّرُ كُلِّ من الطَّرفين كسبيا , والنَّظريُّ بِخلافِهِ على ما مرَّ , فالتصديق بأنَّ الممكن يحتاجُ للمؤثّرِ
Introduction of the author. If you say: Necessary matters are not defined, so how did he define necessity by his saying: 'And it is that which... etc.'? I say: The meaning of their saying 'Necessary matters are not defined' is that the individuals of the necessary are not defined, and this does not contradict that the universal concept that applies to those individuals is defined. And what is here is a definition of the universal concept, not of an individual from its individuals. And his saying: 'That whose attainment does not depend on consideration' is an arrangement of known matters to lead to an unknown, and by 'acquisition' is meant the mentioned arrangement. And then the conjunction is synonymous. Then his statement applies to that which does not depend on anything at all, like grasping that one is half of two, and also to that which depends on intuition, like grasping that the light of the moon is derived from the light of the sun, or experience, like grasping that scammony is a purgative for bile. And then the first principles, intuitive, and experiential enter into the necessary matters. And necessary in this sense is synonymous with intuitive. But intuitive may be applied to that which does not depend on anything at all, so it is more specific than necessary in the mentioned sense, because necessary then exclusively includes the intuitive and experiential. And know that necessity in conceptualizations is apparent, but as for assent, the meaning of necessary in it is that the judgment after conceptualizing the two terms does not depend on consideration, even if the conceptualization of each term is acquired. And the theoretical is contrary to that as has passed. So the assent that the possible needs an effect..
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage begins with a new objection: 'If necessary matters cannot be defined, how did the author define necessity?' The answer distinguishes between individuals (afrād) and the universal concept (mafhūm kullī). The definition is of the universal concept, not of any particular instance. This resolves the apparent contradiction. The commentator then expands on the definition: the phrase 'does not depend on consideration' means an arrangement of known things to reach an unknown, and 'acquisition' is synonymous with that arrangement. Next, the definition is shown to be true for three types of necessary knowledge: (1) what does not depend on anything at all, e.g., that one is half of two (first principles); (2) what depends on intuition (ḥads), e.g., that moonlight is from the sun; (3) what depends on experience (tajriba), e.g., that scammony purges bile. Thus, necessary knowledge includes propositions that are primary, intuitive, and experiential. In this broad sense, 'necessary' is synonymous with 'intuitive' (badīhī). However, 'intuitive' is sometimes restricted to what depends on nothing at all, making it more specific. The passage then clarifies that necessity in conceptualizations (taṣawwurāt) is obvious, but in assents (taṣdīqāt), 'necessary' means the judgment after conceptualizing the two terms does not depend on consideration, even if the conceptualization of each term is acquired (kasbī). This contrasts with theoretical knowledge, which requires consideration. An example given (though cut off) is the assent that the possible needs an effective cause.

Key Terms
البديهي (al-badīhī)
الضروري (al-ḍarūrī)
المفهوم الكلي (al-mafhūm al-kullī)
الفرد (al-fard)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'necessary matters are not defined'?
A) No definition of necessary matters can be given.
B) The individuals of necessary matters cannot be defined.
C) The universal concept of necessity cannot be defined.
D) Only necessary matters in conceptualization are definable.

Key Takeaway
Definitions in logic apply to universal concepts, not to individuals; thus defining necessary knowledge does not contradict its indefinability at the individual level.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What Is Mantiq?

The Scope of Necessary Knowledge: Types and Nuances
Author expands necessary knowledge to include intuitive and experiential types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize necessary knowledge into first principles, intuitive, and experiential types, and explain the semantic relationship between 'darūrī' and 'badīhī'.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge includes more than self-evident truths—it also includes intuitive and experiential knowledge.

The definition of necessary knowledge might seem to cover only self-evident truths like 'one is half of two'. However, the author expands the scope to include intuitive knowledge (ḥads) and experiential knowledge (tajriba). Furthermore, the term 'badīhī' can be used synonymously with 'darūrī' in a broad sense, or more narrowly for what depends on nothing at all. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for classifying knowledge in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of necessary knowledge
From the passage, list the three categories: (1) what does not depend on anything at all (first principles), (2) what depends on intuition (ḥads), and (3) what depends on experience (tajriba). What is the example given for each?
First principles: 'one is half of two'. Intuition: 'moonlight from the sun'. Experience: 'scammony purges bile'.
2
Understand the broad vs narrow sense of badīhī
The passage says 'necessary in this sense is synonymous with intuitive (badīhī)'. But then it notes that badīhī can be restricted to what depends on nothing at all, making it more specific. Why might logicians make this distinction?
The broad sense includes all three types; the narrow sense includes only first principles.
3
Apply to an Islamic theological claim
Consider the knowledge that God exists. Some scholars argue it is necessary (obvious to human intuition). Others say it requires consideration. Which type of necessary knowledge might it be, and why?
This is an advanced application; students should recall that some mutakallimūn considered knowledge of God as necessary based on innate disposition (fiṭra), which could be intuitive.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
Defining the Necessary: Universal Concept vs. Individual Instances
Dassuqi explains the definition of necessary and acquired knowledge, distinguishing between tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the definition of the universal concept of necessary knowledge and the indefinability of its individual instances, and identify the three categories of necessary knowledge (primary, intuitive, experiential) as well as the nature of necessary assent.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is definable as a universal concept, not its individual instances.

This passage from Dassuqi's commentary addresses a critical objection to the very project of defining 'necessary knowledge' in logic. How can something that is self-evident be defined? Dassuqi resolves the tension by distinguishing the universal concept from its particular instances, and goes on to map the different types of necessary knowledge, including primary, intuitive, and experiential. This lesson will equip you with the tools to navigate this foundational distinction in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The passage begins with a dialectical question: 'If necessary matters are not definable, how did the author define necessity?' Extract the apparent contradiction between the claim that necessary matters are indefinable and the author's act of defining them.
2
Extract Dassuqi's resolution
Dassuqi clarifies that the statement 'necessary matters are not definable' refers to individual instances (afrad) of necessary knowledge. The universal concept (mafhum kulli) that applies to those individuals can be defined. Locate this distinction in the Arabic.
3
Examine the definition given
The definition is 'what does not depend for its attainment on speculation (نظر).' Note that this is a definition by negation (rasm). Dassuqi then expands its scope: it includes primary propositions (like one is half of two), intuitive (like moonlight from sun), and experiential (like scammony's effect). Identify these three categories.
4
Analyze the scope of 'necessary' vs 'self-evident'
Dassuqi explains that 'daruri' in this broad sense is synonymous with 'badihi', but 'badihi' can be used more narrowly for that which depends on nothing at all. In that narrower sense, 'daruri' becomes broader, including intuitive and experiential. Understand the terminological shift.
5
Apply the distinction to assent (tasdiq)
For assents, necessary judgment can occur even if the conceptualization of the extremes is acquired. Dassuqi's example: the judgment that a possible thing needs an efficient cause is necessary once the concepts of 'possible' and 'need' are conceived. Trace how conceptualization can be theoretical while the judgment remains self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ
الدسوقي
إنْ قُلْتَ: الأمورُ الضَّروريّةُ لا تُعرَّف، فكيف عرَّفَ الضَّرورةَ بِقولِهِ : (وَهِيَ الَّتي . . . إلخ)؟ . قُلْتُ : : معنى قولهم : الأمورُ الضَّروريّةُ لَا تُعرَّف أنَّ الأفراد لِلضَّروريَّةِ لا تُعرَّف، وهذا لا يُنافي أنَّ المفهوم الكُلِّيَّ الصَّادق على تلك الأفرادِ يُعرَّف، وما هنا تعريفٌ لِلمفهوم الكُلِّيّ لَا لِفرد من أفراده، وقولُهُ: مَا لَا يتوقَّفُ حصولها على نظر) : هو ترتيب أمورٍ معلومةٍ لِلتَّأدِّي إلى مجهول، والمراد بالكسبِ: الترتيب المذكورُ ، وحينئذٍ ؛ فالعطفُ مُرادف .
ثمَّ إِنَّ كلامَهُ صادقٌ بأنْ لا يتوقَّفُ على شيءٍ أصلاً كإدراك أنَّ الواحد نصفُ الاثنين، وَبِمَا إذا توقَّفَ على حدس كَإدراكِ أنَّ نور القمرِ مُستفاد من نورِ الشَّمس، أو تجربة كَإدراكِ أَنَّ السَّقمونيا مُسهَّلةٌ للصَّفراء، وحينئذ ؛ فيدخلُ في الضَّروريَّاتِ القضايا الأوَّليَّة، والحدسيَّة والتَّجريبيَّة والضَّروري بهذا المعنى مُرادف للبديهي، وقد يُطلقُ البديهي على ما لا يُتوقَّفُ على شيءٍ أصلاً ، فيكون أخصَّ من الضَّروري بالمعنى المذكور؛ لانفرادِ الضَّروريّ حينئذٍ بالحدسيَّاتِ والتَّجربيات. واعلَمْ أنَّ الضَّرورة في التَّصوراتِ ظاهرةٌ، وأمَّا التصديقُ ؛ فالمراد بالضَّروريّ منه أن يكون الحكم بعد تصوُّرِ الطَّرفين غير مُتوقِّفٍ على نَظَر، وإِنْ كَانَ تَصوُّرُ كُلِّ من الطَّرفين كسبيا ، والنَّظريُّ بِخلافِهِ على ما مرَّ ، فالتصديق بأنَّ الممكن يحتاجُ للمؤثّرِ ضَروريٌّ لأنَّ مَنْ تصوَّرَ الممكن بأنَّه ما تساوى وجوده وعدمُهُ بالنَّظرِ لذاتِهِ ، والاحتياج بأنَّه الافتقار إلى من يرجّح أحدهما على الآخر ؛ جزم بثبوت الاحتياج إلى الممكن فكلٌّ مِنْ تصورِ الطَّرفين؛ نظري، والحكم بديهي.
(قَوْلُهُ : كَتَصَوُّرِ الْحَرَارَةِ وَالْبُرُودَةِ) أي: أفرادهما ؛ لأنَّه حاصل بطريق الإحساس، فهوَ مِن أجلى البديهيَّاتِ، وأمَّا تَصَوُّرُ مَفهومِهمَا ، فَنَظَرِيٌّ . (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِلَى الاكْتِسَابِ بِالنَّظَرِ قال أبو الفتح : هذا القيد غيرُ مُحتاج إليه؛ ضَرورةَ أنَّ الاكتساب يتضمَّنُ النَّظرَ اصطلاحاً ، إلا أنَّهُ أَرادَ تَمهيد تعريفِ النَّظَرِ،
Introduction of the author. Dassuqi: If you say: Necessary matters are not definable, so how did he define necessity by his saying: (It is that which... etc.)? I say: The meaning of their statement 'Necessary matters are not definable' is that the individuals of the necessary are not definable, and this does not negate that the universal concept applicable to those individuals is definable. What is here is a definition of the universal concept, not of an individual of its individuals. And his statement: 'What does not depend for its attainment on speculation' is an arrangement of known matters to arrive at an unknown. What is meant by acquired is that arrangement. Thus the conjunction is synonymous. Then indeed his statement is true for that which does not depend on anything at all, like the perception that one is half of two, and also for that which depends on intuition like the perception that the light of the moon is derived from the light of the sun, or on experience like the perception that scammony is purgative for bile. Thus the primary propositions, intuitive, and experiential are included in the necessary. The necessary in this sense is synonymous with the self-evident. However, the self-evident may be applied to that which does not depend on anything at all, so it is more specific than the necessary in the mentioned sense, because the necessary then is exclusive to the intuitive and experiential. Know that the necessary is clear in conceptions. As for assent, the necessary from it is that the judgment after conceptualizing both extremes does not depend on speculation, even if the conceptualization of each extreme is acquired. And the theoretical is contrary to it as has passed. Thus the assent that the possible needs an efficient cause is necessary, because whoever conceives the possible as that whose existence and non-existence are equal regarding its essence, and need as the dependence upon that which gives preponderance to one of them over the other, he decides on the establishment of need for the possible. So each of the conceptualizations of the two extremes is theoretical, and the judgment is self-evident. His saying: 'Like the conceptualization of heat and cold' means their individuals, because it is attained through sensation, so it is among the clearest of self-evident things. As for the conceptualization of their concept, it is theoretical. His saying: 'And to acquisition by speculation.' Abu al-Fath said: This qualifier is not needed, because necessarily acquisition includes speculation terminologically, but he intended to prepare the definition of speculation.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by Dassuqi on the author's introduction, where he addresses a potential objection about defining necessary knowledge. He begins with a dialectical exchange: 'If you say that necessary matters are not definable, how did the author define necessity?' Dassuqi clarifies that the statement 'necessary matters are not definable' refers to individual instances of necessary knowledge—e.g., the specific intuition that one is half of two cannot be defined as a concept because it is immediately grasped. However, the universal concept of 'necessary' that applies to all such instances can be defined. The definition given ('that which does not depend for its attainment on speculation') is a definition of the universal concept, not of any particular individual. He then explains that 'speculation' (نظر) is the arrangement of known matters to arrive at an unknown, and 'acquisition' (كسب) is synonymous with that arrangement. Next, he expands on the scope of the definition: it includes matters that depend on nothing (like primary propositions), matters that depend on intuition (like the light of the moon), and matters that depend on experience (like the purgative effect of scammony). Thus, the term 'necessary' in this sense includes primary, intuitive, and experiential propositions, making it synonymous with 'self-evident' (بديهي). However, 'self-evident' is sometimes used more narrowly to mean only that which depends on nothing at all, making it a subset of 'necessary' (which then excludes intuitive and experiential). Dassuqi then turns to the distinction between conceptual and assenting knowledge. He states that necessary knowledge is clear in conceptions (تصورات) but in assents (تصديقات), the necessary judgment is one that does not depend on speculation after the extremes have been conceptualized, even if the conceptualization of each extreme is itself acquired (كسبي). He gives the example: the assent that a possible thing needs an efficient cause is necessary, because once one conceptualizes 'possible' (that whose existence and non-existence are equal) and 'need' (dependence on a preponderator), one immediately judges the need. The conceptualizations of the extremes are theoretical, but the judgment is self-evident. Finally, he comments on the examples: 'heat and cold' refer to their individual instances (which are known by sensation and are self-evident), but the conceptual definitions of heat and cold are theoretical. He also notes a remark by Abu al-Fath that the qualifier 'by speculation' in 'acquisition by speculation' is redundant because acquisition terminologically implies speculation, but the author intended to introduce the definition of speculation.

Key Terms
ضَروريّ
نظريّ
بديهي
تصوّر
تصديق
كسب
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The universal concept of 'necessary' cannot be defined.
B) The individual instances of necessary knowledge cannot be defined.
C) Necessary knowledge is only known by intuition, not definition.
D) The definition of necessary is impossible because it is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The universal concept of necessary knowledge is definable, and it includes primary, intuitive, and experiential propositions; necessary assent can occur even when the conceptualizations of the terms are acquired.

What Is Mantiq?

Resolving the Pronoun in Nazar's Definition and the Scope of Nazar
Al-Dasuqi resolves the pronoun referent in the definition of nazar and gives his own definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the pronoun in the definition of nazar must refer to nazar itself, and how al-Dasuqi's definition broadens the scope to include single-term definitions.

Core Idea
Al-Dasuqi refines nazar's definition to include both single-term and multi-term definitions, resolving a pronoun ambiguity.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi critically examines the definition of nazar (consideration/inference) given by the matn author. He first clarifies a grammatical issue: who the pronoun refers to in the definition. He then explains why the author changed the classical definition of nazar as 'arranging known matters' to 'observation of the known'—to include definitions that use only one term. This lesson walks you through al-Dasuqi's two-step refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Pronoun Referent Problem
The pronoun in the definition could refer to 'iktisab' or 'nazar'. Al-Dasuqi argues that it must refer to nazar for two reasons: (1) 'iktisab' earlier meant the acquired knowledge, not the act of observation; (2) the definition is explicitly of nazar.
This is a grammatical-lexical analysis.
2
Understand the Two Reasons
First, if the pronoun referred to 'iktisab', the predication would be incorrect because 'iktisab' is the acquired, not the act. Second, the definition is clearly defining nazar itself.
Reason two is based on the structure of the definition.
3
Recognize the Optional Interpretation
If the 'ba' in 'bil-nazar' is interpreted as for depiction (i.e., acquisition depicted by nazar), then the commentator's view is acceptable but requires 'istikhdam' (using a word in two senses).
This shows al-Dasuqi's flexibility.
4
Grasp the Motivation for the New Definition
The classical definition 'arranging known matters' would exclude single-term definitions like 'human is rational' (definition by differentia alone). The author's new definition 'observation of the known' includes them because 'observation' can focus on a single known.
This is the key refinement.
5
Distribute the Definition
Observation of the conceptual known yields conceptual unknowns; observation of the assentive known yields assentive unknowns. This covers all types of inference.
Conceptual vs. assentive is a fundamental distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصلُ : أَنَّ الصَّواب جعلُ الضَّميرِ راجعاً للنظر؛ لأمرين: الأول: أنَّ الاكتساب فيما مرَّ المراد به المكتسب، وهو غيرُ الملاحظة، فالإخبار حينئذ لا يصح، الأمرُ الثَّاني : أنَّ التَّعريف المذكور تعريفٌ للنَّظرِ لَا لِلاكتسابِ به، نعم إنْ جعلت الباء في قولِهِ : بالنَّظرِ للتّصوير ؛ أي: الاكتساب المصوَّرِ بالنَّظر ؛ صح ما قاله الشَّارح، وعليهِ فيكونُ في كلام المصنفِ استخدام ؛ حيثُ ذكر الاكتساب أوَّلاً بمعنى المكتسب، ثمَّ أعادَ الضَّمير عليه ثانياً بمعنى آخر، وهو النظر، وإنَّما عدل المصنِّفُ في تعريف النَّظرِ بما ذكرَهُ عن تعريفه الواقع في عبارةِ القوم، وهو ترتيب أمور معلومة للتّأدِّي إلى مجهول ؛ ليكونَ التَّعريفُ شاملاً للتّعريف بالمفرد، وهو ما عليه المتقدمون، وبعضُ المتأخّرين كتعريف الإنسانِ بناطقٍ أو ضاحك، وذلك لأنَّ قولَه : ملاحظة المعقول ؛ أي توجهُ النَّفسِ والتفاتُها لِلأمرِ الَّذي حصلَتْ صورته في العقل؛ سواء كان واحداً كَمَا في الحدّ بالفصل وحدَه ، والرَّسم بالخاصَّة وحدها، أو كانَ كثيراً، وفي الكلام توزيع؛ أي ملاحظة المعقولِ التَّصوري لتحصيل المجهول التّصوري، وملاحظة المعقولِ التَّصديقي ؛ لأجل تحصيل المجهول التصديقي. واعلَمْ أنَّ النَّظر والفكر عندَهُم مُترادفان، فيفسرانِ بما قاله المصنِّفُ، وَبِمَا قالَهُ القوم فيما تقدَّم، وقوله : لتحصيل المجهول؛ أي: تصوريَّاً أو تصديقيا . وإنَّما اعتبر المعقولية في الموصل، والمجهوليَّة في المطلوب؛ لأنَّهُ لو كانَ الموصل مجهولاً؛ استحال تحصيل المطلوب المجهول به؛ إذ يستحيلُ بالضَّرورة أن يستلزم مجهول العِلم بمجهول آخر، ولو كان المطلوب معلوماً ؛ استحال تحصيله؛ لأنَّه يستحيلُ تحصيل الحاصل.
And the conclusion is that the correct [view] is to make the pronoun refer to nazar (consideration), for two reasons: first, that in what preceded, 'iktisab' (acquisition) meant the acquired [knowledge], and it is not observation, so the predication then is not correct. Second: that the aforementioned definition is a definition of nazar, not of iktisab. Yes, if you make the 'ba' in his statement 'bil-nazar' for depiction, i.e., acquisition depicted by nazar, then what the commentator said is correct, and accordingly there would be in the author's speech an 'istikhdam' (using a word in two senses) because he mentioned 'iktisab' first meaning the acquired, then returned the pronoun upon it a second time with another meaning, namely nazar. And the author deviated in his definition of nazar from the definition found in the expression of the [logicians], which is 'arranging known matters to arrive at an unknown', in order for the definition to include definition by a single [term], which is the way of the early scholars and some later ones, such as defining 'human' by 'rational' or 'laughing'. That is because his statement 'observation of the known' means the soul's attention and turning toward the matter whose form has been obtained in the intellect, whether it is one, as in definition by the differentia alone and description by the property alone, or many. And in the statement there is a distribution: observation of the conceptual known to obtain the conceptual unknown, and observation of the assentive known in order to obtain the assentive unknown. Know that nazar and fikr (reflection) are synonymous in their terminology, so they are explained by what the author said and by what the [logicians] said previously. And his statement 'to obtain the unknown' means [whether] conceptual or assentive. And he considered the knownness in the conduit and the unknownness in the sought, because if the conduit were unknown, it would be impossible to obtain the sought unknown through it, since it is impossible by necessity that an unknown knowledge entails another unknown. And if the sought were known, its attainment would be impossible, because obtaining the obtained is impossible.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage from al-Dasuqi's super-commentary (hashiya) on Tadhheeb Sharh is a critical refinement of the commentator's (sharh) interpretation of the definition of nazar (consideration/inference). Al-Dasuqi begins by stating the correct view: the pronoun in the definition should refer to nazar, not iktisab (acquisition). He gives two reasons: first, that earlier in the text, 'iktisab' meant the acquired knowledge, which is not the same as observation (mulahazah), so making the pronoun refer to iktisab would make the predication invalid. Second, the definition given is clearly a definition of nazar, not of iktisab. He then qualifies that if one interprets the 'ba' in 'bil-nazar' as instrumental in producing a depiction—i.e., acquisition depicted by nazar—then the commentator's interpretation would be acceptable, but that would entail the rhetorical figure of 'istikhdam' (using a word in two senses) in the author's speech. Al-Dasuqi then explains why the author (the matn writer) changed the definition of nazar from the classical logicians' definition ('arranging known matters to arrive at an unknown') to his own definition ('observation of the known'): to include definitions that use only a single term, such as defining 'human' by 'rational' (definition by differentia alone) or by 'laughing' (description by property alone). The classical definition, which involves 'arranging' multiple items, would not cover such single-term definitions. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'observation of the known' means the soul's attention to a matter whose form is already in the intellect, whether that matter is one (as in definition by differentia alone) or many. He then distributes the definition into two types: observation of conceptual knowns to obtain conceptual unknowns, and observation of assentive knowns to obtain assentive unknowns. Finally, al-Dasuqi notes that nazar and fikr are synonymous in the logicians' terminology, and explains the rationale for requiring the conduit to be known and the sought to be unknown: if the conduit were unknown, it could not lead to knowledge of another unknown; if the sought were already known, it would be impossible to obtain it since it is already obtained.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar): Observation of the known (conceptual or assentive) to obtain the unknown; a mental act distinct from simple observation. [Note: In earlier logical texts, nazar is defined as 'arranging known matters to arrive at an unknown'; al-Dasuqi's definition is broader to include single-term definitions.]
الاكتساب (al-iktisab): The acquired knowledge (al-muktasab), as distinct from the mental act of observation; also used metonymically for nazar itself. [Note: No significant shift; the term is stable as 'acquisition of knowledge'.]
الملاحظة (al-mulahazah): Observation; the attention of the soul toward a known matter present in the intellect. [Note: Often used synonymously with nazar by some authors, but al-Dasuqi distinguishes them.]
التصوري (al-tasawwuri): Conceptual; pertaining to concepts (tasawwurat) as opposed to assent (tasdiqat). [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
التصديقي (al-tasdiqi): Assentive; pertaining to judgments (tasdiqat) as opposed to concepts. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The act of observation
C) The defined term
D) The unknown

Key Takeaway
Al-Dasuqi refines the definition of nazar to include single-term definitions by defining it as 'observation of the known to obtain the unknown', and resolves the pronoun referent issue to maintain logical consistency.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Definition of Nazar and the Condition of the Sought
Author defines nazar and fikr, resolves objection about seeking the absolutely unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that the sought must be partially known and partially unknown, and explain the definition of nazar/fikr as the mental activity to acquire the unknown.

Core Idea
Nazar is not a single glance but a structured mental process—the sum of two movements that acquire the unknown from the known.

This lesson establishes the foundational definitions of logical inquiry: what is nazar? What conditions must the object of inquiry satisfy? The passage resolves a critical puzzle: how can we seek what is unknown without seeking the utterly unknowable? The answer lies in the fact that the sought must be known in one respect to be recognized and unknown in another to be acquired.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find where the author defines nazar and fikr. They are synonymous and described as a mental activity to acquire the unknown (tahsil al-majhul).
The definition includes both conceptual and assertive knowledge.
2
Extract the condition of the means
The means (al-mawsil) must be known (ma'qul). Why? Because if the means were unknown, no unknown could be acquired through it.
The text states that it is necessarily impossible for an unknown to necessitate another unknown.
3
Analyze the condition of the sought
The sought (al-matlab) must be unknown in some respect to be acquirable, but known in another to be recognized. Otherwise, it would either be the absolutely unknown (unseekable) or already known (acquiring the already attained).
The objection about 'seeking the absolutely unknown' is resolved here.
4
Confirm the scope of al-ma'qul
Note that the author uses 'al-ma'qul' (intelligible) rather than 'al-ma'lum' (known) to include what is known, presumed, or even unknown with compound ignorance.
This broadens the definition's applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النظر والفكر عندهم مترادفان، فيفسران بما قاله المصنف، وبما قاله القوم فيما تقدم، وقوله: لتحصيل المجهول؛ أي: تصورياً أو تصديقياً. وإنما اعتبر المعقولية في الموصل، والمجهولية في المطلوب؛ لأنه لو كان الموصل مجهولاً؛ استحال تحصيل المطلوب المجهول به؛ إذ يستحيل بالضرورة أن يستلزم مجهول العلم بمجهول آخر، ولو كان المطلوب معلوماً؛ استحال تحصيله؛ لأنه يستحيل تحصيل الحاصل.

إن قلت: إذا كان المطلوب مجهولاً؛ يلزم أن تكون النفس طالبة للمجهول المطلق، وهو محال، والجواب: أنه قد تحقق أنه لا بد أن لا يكون المطلوب مجهولاً من كل الوجوه، بل لا بد أن يكون معلوماً بوجه؛ لئلا يلزم طلب المجهول المطلق، ومجهولاً بوجه آخر؛ لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل.

(قوله: المعقول) عبر به دون المعلوم؛ ليشمل ما كان معلوماً أو مظنوناً أو مجهولاً جهلاً مركباً، وسواء كان المعقول تصوراً أو تصديقاً، مفرداً أو مركباً.

(قوله: ملاحظة المعقول) تحرير المقام أنه لا شبهة في أن كل مجهول لا يمكن اكتسابه من أي معلوم اتفق؛ بل لا بد من معلومات مناسبة له، ومعلوم أنه لا يمكن تحصيله من تلك المعلومات على أي وجه كان؛ بل لا بد هناك من ترتيب معين فيما بين تلك المعلومات، ومن هيئة مخصوصة عارضة لها بسبب ذلك الترتيب، فإذا حصل لنا شعور بأمر تصوري أو تصديقي وحاولنا تحصيله على وجه أكمل؛ فلا بد أن يتحرك الذهن في المعلومات المخزونة عنده منتقلاً من معلوم إلى آخر؛ حتى يجد المعلومات المناسبة لذلك المطلوب، وهي المسماه بمباديه، ثم لا بد أيضاً أن يتحرك في تلك المبادي بترتيبها ترتيباً خاصا يؤدي إلى ذلك المطلوب، فهناك حركتان؛ مبدأ الأولى منهما: هو المطلوب المشعور به بذلك الوجه الناقص، ومنتهاها: آخر ما يحصل من تلك المبادي، ومبدأ الثانية: أول ما يوضع من الترتيب، ومنتهاها المشعور به على الوجه الأكمل، فحقيقة النظر المتوسط بين المعلوم والمجهول، وهو مجموع هاتين الحركتين اللتين هما من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات، فالمتقدمون ذهبوا إلى أن الفكر مجموع الحركتين، وذهب المتأخرون إلى أنه الترتيب اللازم للحركة الثانية، ويرادف الفكر النظر على القولين، قال أبو الفتح: وربما يفرق بينهما بأن الفكر مجموع الحركتين أو الترتيب اللازم لهما والنظر ملاحظة المعقولات الواقعة في ضمن الحركتين أو الترتيب، ويدل عليه قول ناقد المحصل أنهما كالمترادفين، قال: والظاهر أن تعريف المصنف مبني على
Know that nazar and fikr according to them are synonymous, so they are explained by what the author said, and by what the people said in what preceded. His saying: 'to acquire the unknown' meaning: conceptual or assertive. And indeed, intelligibility is considered in the means, and unknownness in the sought; because if the means were unknown, it would be impossible to acquire the unknown sought by it, since it is necessarily impossible for an unknown knowledge to necessitate another unknown. And if the sought were known, it would be impossible to acquire it, because it is impossible to acquire what is already attained.

If you say: If the sought is unknown, then it necessitates that the soul seeks the absolutely unknown, which is absurd. The answer: It has been verified that the sought must not be unknown in every respect; rather it must be known in one respect to avoid seeking the absolutely unknown, and unknown in another respect to avoid attaining the already attained.

(His saying: 'the intelligible') He used it instead of 'the known' to include what is known, or presumed, or unknown with compound ignorance, whether the intelligible is conceptual or assertive, simple or compound.

(His saying: 'observation of the intelligible') The clarification of the topic: There is no doubt that every unknown cannot be acquired from any known whatsoever; rather it requires information appropriate to it. And it is known that it cannot be attained from that information in any manner whatsoever; rather there must be a specific arrangement among that information, and a specific form arising from that arrangement. So when we have a perception of something conceptual or assertive and we try to attain it in a more complete manner, then the mind must move among the stored information, transferring from one known to another until it finds the information appropriate to that sought, which are called its principles. Then it must also move among those principles with a specific arrangement leading to that sought. So there are two movements: the starting point of the first is the sought perceived in that incomplete manner, and its end is the last thing attained from those principles; the starting point of the second is the first thing placed in the arrangement, and its end is the perception in the more complete manner. So the reality of nazar is intermediate between the known and the unknown, and it is the sum of these two movements, which are of the kind of qualitative motion. The earlier scholars were of the opinion that fikr is the sum of the two movements, and the later scholars were of the opinion that it is the arrangement necessary for the second movement. Fikr and nazar are synonymous on both opinions. Abu al-Fath said: Sometimes a distinction is made between them: fikr is the sum of the two movements or the arrangement necessary for them, and nazar is the observation of the intelligibles occurring within the two movements or the arrangement. This is supported by the statement of the critic of al-Muhassal that they are like synonyms. He said: It appears that the author's definition is based on...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of nazar and fikr, addressing a fundamental epistemological problem. It begins by affirming that nazar and fikr are synonyms, as used by the author (presumably al-Akhdari or another) and earlier scholars. The definition involves 'acquiring the unknown' (tahsil al-majhul), whether that unknown is a concept or an assertion. The commentator emphasizes that the means (al-mawsil) must be known (ma'qul) and the sought (al-matlab) must be unknown (majhul) in the right way; otherwise, if the means were unknown, it could not lead to another unknown, and if the sought were already known, it would be already attained and not require acquisition.

An objection is raised: if the sought is absolutely unknown, the soul would seek something utterly unknown, which is impossible. The response clarifies that the sought is not unknown in every respect; it must be known in one aspect (so it can be sought) and unknown in another (so it can be acquired). This avoids the two extremes: seeking the completely unknown and attaining what is already attained.

The commentator then explains the term 'al-ma'qul' (the intelligible) used in the definition: it is broader than 'al-ma'lum' (the known), covering what is known, presumed, or even unknown with compound ignorance, and includes both concepts and assertions, simple or compound.

A detailed analysis follows: the mind's movement to acquire the unknown involves two motions. The first motion starts from the sought (perceived imperfectly) and ends with gathering the appropriate principles (mabadi') from stored information. The second motion begins with arranging these principles in a specific order and ends with the sought perceived more perfectly. Nazar is the sum of these two motions, which are qualitative (like change in knowledge). There is a difference between earlier and later scholars: the earlier hold fikr to be both motions; the later hold it to be only the arrangement of the second motion. Fikr and nazar remain synonymous in both views, though Abu al-Fath mentions a possible distinction: fikr is the motions or arrangement, while nazar is the observation of the intelligibles occurring within them. The passage concludes by noting that the critic of al-Muhassal considers them synonyms, and the author's definition appears to be based on this understanding.

Key Terms
النظر
الفكر
المجهول
المعقول
المبادئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the sought cannot be absolutely unknown?
A) Because the soul cannot seek something completely unknown, as it would be impossible to recognize what to seek.
B) Because the means would then be unknown, making acquisition impossible.
C) Because the soul only seeks what it already knows perfectly.
D) Because the sought is always a simple concept.

Key Takeaway
For valid inquiry, the sought must be partially known (to be recognized) and partially unknown (to require acquisition).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated. Everything temporally originated has a cause. Therefore the world has a cause.' Identify the 'known' and 'unknown' in this argument per the condition discussed.
?
In fiqh, a new financial transaction appears. A jurist wants to determine whether it involves riba. What must he already know about the transaction (the 'known' aspect) to make inquiry possible?

The Two Movements of Nazar
Author explains the two movements of the mind in nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the two movements of the mind in nazar and distinguish between the views of earlier and later scholars on fikr.

Core Idea
Nazar is a dynamic two-step process: first gather the right materials, then arrange them precisely to reach the goal.

The author provides a detailed analysis of what happens when we think: the mind moves from an imperfect awareness of a sought truth, through stored information, to a perfect grasp. This lesson unpacks the two movements and the scholarly difference about where 'fikr' lies. Understanding this process is crucial for appreciating how definitions and demonstrations are constructed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two movements
The text describes two movements: the first movement goes from the imperfectly known sought to the gathering of appropriate principles (mabadi'). The second movement arranges those principles in a specific order to reach the complete knowledge.
The first movement is searching and collecting; the second is ordering and deducing.
2
Understand the role of principles
The 'principles' are stored information that is appropriate to the sought. The mind transfers from one known to another until it finds these principles.
This is like gathering premises for a syllogism.
3
Compare earlier and later views
Earlier scholars held that fikr is the sum of both movements. Later scholars restricted fikr to the arrangement (the second movement) only. Both agree that nazar and fikr are synonymous.
Abu al-Fath mentions a further nuance: fikr as the movements, nazar as the observation of intelligibles within them.
4
Grasp the reality of nazar
Nazar is the 'middle' between the known and the unknown, consisting of these two qualitative motions. The process transforms an imperfect perception into a perfect one.
This shows that logic is not static but a dynamic cognitive process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النظر والفكر عندهم مترادفان، فيفسران بما قاله المصنف، وبما قاله القوم فيما تقدم، وقوله: لتحصيل المجهول؛ أي: تصورياً أو تصديقياً. وإنما اعتبر المعقولية في الموصل، والمجهولية في المطلوب؛ لأنه لو كان الموصل مجهولاً؛ استحال تحصيل المطلوب المجهول به؛ إذ يستحيل بالضرورة أن يستلزم مجهول العلم بمجهول آخر، ولو كان المطلوب معلوماً؛ استحال تحصيله؛ لأنه يستحيل تحصيل الحاصل.

إن قلت: إذا كان المطلوب مجهولاً؛ يلزم أن تكون النفس طالبة للمجهول المطلق، وهو محال، والجواب: أنه قد تحقق أنه لا بد أن لا يكون المطلوب مجهولاً من كل الوجوه، بل لا بد أن يكون معلوماً بوجه؛ لئلا يلزم طلب المجهول المطلق، ومجهولاً بوجه آخر؛ لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل.

(قوله: المعقول) عبر به دون المعلوم؛ ليشمل ما كان معلوماً أو مظنوناً أو مجهولاً جهلاً مركباً، وسواء كان المعقول تصوراً أو تصديقاً، مفرداً أو مركباً.

(قوله: ملاحظة المعقول) تحرير المقام أنه لا شبهة في أن كل مجهول لا يمكن اكتسابه من أي معلوم اتفق؛ بل لا بد من معلومات مناسبة له، ومعلوم أنه لا يمكن تحصيله من تلك المعلومات على أي وجه كان؛ بل لا بد هناك من ترتيب معين فيما بين تلك المعلومات، ومن هيئة مخصوصة عارضة لها بسبب ذلك الترتيب، فإذا حصل لنا شعور بأمر تصوري أو تصديقي وحاولنا تحصيله على وجه أكمل؛ فلا بد أن يتحرك الذهن في المعلومات المخزونة عنده منتقلاً من معلوم إلى آخر؛ حتى يجد المعلومات المناسبة لذلك المطلوب، وهي المسماه بمباديه، ثم لا بد أيضاً أن يتحرك في تلك المبادي بترتيبها ترتيباً خاصا يؤدي إلى ذلك المطلوب، فهناك حركتان؛ مبدأ الأولى منهما: هو المطلوب المشعور به بذلك الوجه الناقص، ومنتهاها: آخر ما يحصل من تلك المبادي، ومبدأ الثانية: أول ما يوضع من الترتيب، ومنتهاها المشعور به على الوجه الأكمل، فحقيقة النظر المتوسط بين المعلوم والمجهول، وهو مجموع هاتين الحركتين اللتين هما من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات، فالمتقدمون ذهبوا إلى أن الفكر مجموع الحركتين، وذهب المتأخرون إلى أنه الترتيب اللازم للحركة الثانية، ويرادف الفكر النظر على القولين، قال أبو الفتح: وربما يفرق بينهما بأن الفكر مجموع الحركتين أو الترتيب اللازم لهما والنظر ملاحظة المعقولات الواقعة في ضمن الحركتين أو الترتيب، ويدل عليه قول ناقد المحصل أنهما كالمترادفين، قال: والظاهر أن تعريف المصنف مبني على
Know that nazar and fikr according to them are synonymous, so they are explained by what the author said, and by what the people said in what preceded. His saying: 'to acquire the unknown' meaning: conceptual or assertive. And indeed, intelligibility is considered in the means, and unknownness in the sought; because if the means were unknown, it would be impossible to acquire the unknown sought by it, since it is necessarily impossible for an unknown knowledge to necessitate another unknown. And if the sought were known, it would be impossible to acquire it, because it is impossible to acquire what is already attained.

If you say: If the sought is unknown, then it necessitates that the soul seeks the absolutely unknown, which is absurd. The answer: It has been verified that the sought must not be unknown in every respect; rather it must be known in one respect to avoid seeking the absolutely unknown, and unknown in another respect to avoid attaining the already attained.

(His saying: 'the intelligible') He used it instead of 'the known' to include what is known, or presumed, or unknown with compound ignorance, whether the intelligible is conceptual or assertive, simple or compound.

(His saying: 'observation of the intelligible') The clarification of the topic: There is no doubt that every unknown cannot be acquired from any known whatsoever; rather it requires information appropriate to it. And it is known that it cannot be attained from that information in any manner whatsoever; rather there must be a specific arrangement among that information, and a specific form arising from that arrangement. So when we have a perception of something conceptual or assertive and we try to attain it in a more complete manner, then the mind must move among the stored information, transferring from one known to another until it finds the information appropriate to that sought, which are called its principles. Then it must also move among those principles with a specific arrangement leading to that sought. So there are two movements: the starting point of the first is the sought perceived in that incomplete manner, and its end is the last thing attained from those principles; the starting point of the second is the first thing placed in the arrangement, and its end is the perception in the more complete manner. So the reality of nazar is intermediate between the known and the unknown, and it is the sum of these two movements, which are of the kind of qualitative motion. The earlier scholars were of the opinion that fikr is the sum of the two movements, and the later scholars were of the opinion that it is the arrangement necessary for the second movement. Fikr and nazar are synonymous on both opinions. Abu al-Fath said: Sometimes a distinction is made between them: fikr is the sum of the two movements or the arrangement necessary for them, and nazar is the observation of the intelligibles occurring within the two movements or the arrangement. This is supported by the statement of the critic of al-Muhassal that they are like synonyms. He said: It appears that the author's definition is based on...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of nazar and fikr, addressing a fundamental epistemological problem. It begins by affirming that nazar and fikr are synonyms, as used by the author (presumably al-Akhdari or another) and earlier scholars. The definition involves 'acquiring the unknown' (tahsil al-majhul), whether that unknown is a concept or an assertion. The commentator emphasizes that the means (al-mawsil) must be known (ma'qul) and the sought (al-matlab) must be unknown (majhul) in the right way; otherwise, if the means were unknown, it could not lead to another unknown, and if the sought were already known, it would be already attained and not require acquisition.

An objection is raised: if the sought is absolutely unknown, the soul would seek something utterly unknown, which is impossible. The response clarifies that the sought is not unknown in every respect; it must be known in one aspect (so it can be sought) and unknown in another (so it can be acquired). This avoids the two extremes: seeking the completely unknown and attaining what is already attained.

The commentator then explains the term 'al-ma'qul' (the intelligible) used in the definition: it is broader than 'al-ma'lum' (the known), covering what is known, presumed, or even unknown with compound ignorance, and includes both concepts and assertions, simple or compound.

A detailed analysis follows: the mind's movement to acquire the unknown involves two motions. The first motion starts from the sought (perceived imperfectly) and ends with gathering the appropriate principles (mabadi') from stored information. The second motion begins with arranging these principles in a specific order and ends with the sought perceived more perfectly. Nazar is the sum of these two motions, which are qualitative (like change in knowledge). There is a difference between earlier and later scholars: the earlier hold fikr to be both motions; the later hold it to be only the arrangement of the second motion. Fikr and nazar remain synonymous in both views, though Abu al-Fath mentions a possible distinction: fikr is the motions or arrangement, while nazar is the observation of the intelligibles occurring within them. The passage concludes by noting that the critic of al-Muhassal considers them synonyms, and the author's definition appears to be based on this understanding.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the second movement of the mind when acquiring the unknown?
A) Gathering appropriate principles from stored knowledge.
B) Arranging the principles in a specific order leading to the sought.
C) Observing the intelligibles within the movements.
D) Transferring from one known to another until the sought is reached.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the sum of two movements: first gathering appropriate principles, then arranging them in a specific order to attain complete knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a qiyas example: 'Wine is forbidden because of intoxication. Nabidh (date wine) is intoxicating, therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Break this into the two movements: what information was gathered (first movement) and how it was arranged (second movement).
?
In a kalam argument for the existence of God, identify the two movements: what known premises are gathered (first movement) and how they are arranged to reach the conclusion (second movement).

What Is Mantiq?

Distinguishing Nazar from Hads: The Role of the Second Movement
Refining nazar's definition and distinguishing from hads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish nazar from hads based on the presence of voluntary arrangement (second movement) and evaluate definitions of speculative thought against the objections raised.

Core Idea
Nazar requires deliberate arrangement of premises; hads lacks it.

In this dense hashia passage, al-Dasuqi refines the definition of nazar by addressing objections from Mir Ghiyath and others. He insists that the key distinction between speculative thought and intuition lies in the 'second movement'—the deliberate ordering of premises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating whether a cognitive process qualifies as proper reasoning in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two movements
The text distinguishes two movements: the first movement acquires premises, the second movement arranges them. Notice that the author claims intuition only has the first movement, while nazar always includes the second.
This is the core thesis of the passage.
2
Locate Mir Ghiyath's objection
Find the objection that the voluntary nature of premise acquisition does not truly differentiate nazar from hads, since many premises are involuntary (self-evident).
The objection targets the criterion of 'intention and choice'.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that the second movement (arrangement) is absent in hads. Therefore, definitions of nazar that include arrangement or both movements avoid the objection. For definitions using 'observation', the lam (purpose) shows the observation is goal-oriented, which also excludes hads.
The response uses the concept of 'second movement' as the definitive differentia.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider the example of the 'laughing body' (singular). The author argues that if premises are already arranged and known, the conclusion follows necessarily, so it is not a case of seeking unknown knowledge (nazar). This exemplifies how the definition must exclude cases where arrangement is absent or conclusion is forced.
This clarifies the scope of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترضه مير غياث بأنَّهُ إنْ أرادَ أَنَّ حُصول المبادي في الذهن في صورةِ الحدس ليس بالقصدِ والاختيار ؛ فحصولها فيهِ في صورةِ النَّظر؛ كثيراً ما يكون كذلك، كيف لا ؛ وأكثرُ مباديهِ أُمورٌ بديهيَّةٌ لا تعلمُ أَنَّها حصلَتْ وكيف حصلَتْ؟ وإنْ أرادَ أَنَّ التَّوجة والالتفات إلى المبادئ الحاصلة في صورةِ النَّظَرِ بالقصدِ والاختيار دون الحدس ؛ فَممنُوعٌ ، ولا يظهرُ في ذلكَ فَرْقٌ بينَ الصُّورتَينِ أصلاً، كما لا يذهب على ذي مُسْكَةٍ ا.هـ. (أقولُ): ظهر لي عند تقرير هذا المحل أنَّ الحركة الثانية لا توجد في الحدس، بل الأُولَى فقط ؛ فلا يُرَدُّ على مَن فسَّرَ النَّظَرَ بالحركتين أو بالترتيب، أما الأوَّلُ؛ فَظَاهِرُ ، وأمَّا الثَّاني ؛ فَلِمَا عَلمْتُ أنَّ التَّرتيب لازم للحركة الثانية، وأما ورودُهُ على تعريف النَّظَرِ بالملاحظة؛ فمُتَوَهَّمْ، واندفاعه بجعل اللام للأجل كما قُلنا ؛ لأنَّهُ حينئذٍ يكون مدخولها عِلَّةً مُترتِّبَةٌ ، فَفِيهِ إيماء إلى أنَّ المطلوب تحصيلُهُ مُتأخّرُ عَن المعقولات المنظور فيها، والحَدَّس ليس كذلك، وأيضاً المراد بملاحظةِ المعقولات؛ الملاحظة الواقعةُ في ضِمْنِ الانتقالِ مِنَ المطلوبِ إلى المبادي، ثمَّ منها إليهِ، كَمَا صَرَّحَ بذلك الميبديُّ في شرحِ الطَّوالعِ، قال المحشّي : وأورد على التعريف أيضاً صدقَهُ على الحركةِ الأُولى في صورة مجموع الحركتين مع أَنَّ النَّظَرَ هو المجموع في هذهِ الصُّورةِ اتَّفَاقاً، وصدقَهُ على ملاحظة المبادي المترتِّبَةِ المعلومة سابقاً، كما إذا كانَ الجِسْمُ الصَّاحِكُ مَعلُوماً بِهذا الترتيب سَابِقَاً ; فَتُلاحِظُهُ النَّفْسُ قصدَ التَّحصيل الإنسان، ولمْ يَقُلْ أحدٌ بِوجودِ الفِكْرِ مِن غير ترتيب في غير النَّظَرِ في المفردِ ، وأنْ لا تفاوت بينه وبين المفردِ ا.هـ (أقولُ) : لا ورود، أمَّا في الصُّورةِ الأُولَى ; فَلِأَنَّ الحركةَ الأُولَى كَمَا قد سمعت مُحصَّلَةٌ لمبادئ المطلوب، وحصول تلك المبادي عندَ النَّفْسِ لا يؤدى إلَّا بعد الترتيب الحاصل بالحركةِ الثَّانية، فبعد أنْ قُيّدَت الملاحظةُ بالغاية كما قلنا في خروج الحدس; لا يصدقُ التَّعريفُ على هذهِ الصُّورةِ، وأمَّا تَعريفُهُ بِمجموع الحركتين أو بالتّرتيب ; فَعِلْمُ الصِّدْقِ ظَاهِرُ ، ولذلكَ خَصَّ الورود بتعريف المصنّف بالملاحظة، وأمَّا الصُّورةُ الثَّانيةُ ; فَمَع مَا في التّركيبِ مِن القلاقة; فَلَا ترد أيضاً، أمَّا وجه القلاقة; فإِنَّ قولَهُ : ولمْ يَقُلْ أحدٌ ... إلخ; مِن تمام الاعتراض، وربَّما توهم استئنافيَّةَ الجملة، وقَولُهُ: وأن لا تفاوت ... إلخ; معناه أنَّ هذهِ الصُّورة تفاوتُ المفرد، ولم يقع تفاوت بينها وبينه في عدم الترتيب، وقد أشعرَ قولُهُ: في غيرِ النَّظَرِ في المفرد ; أنَّه لا ترتيب فيهِ، وليس كما زعم كيف وقد تكلَّمُوا في تأويله حتّى حتَّقُوا فِيهِ التَّرتيب، وأما عدمُ الورودِ ; فَلِأَنَّهُ حيثُ كانَتِ المبادي مرتبةً معلومة; كانَ العِلْمُ بالمطلوب حاصلاً أيضاً بطريقِ القَهْرِ; بحيث لا يُمكِنُ للنَّفْسِ دفعُهُ، فأينَ المجهول المطلوب تحصيلُهُ؟ وَمَنْ تعقَّبَهُ جَارَاهُ في كلامه وتكلَّفَ في دفعِهِ، وقد علمت مَا فِيهِ، وبَقِيَ هَهُنا شيء ; وهو أنَّهم كثيراً ما يقولون : الفِكْرُ لُغَةٌ : حركةُ النَّفْس في المعقولات، ويُقابِلُهُ التَّخييلُ، وجَعْلُ هذا معنى لغويًّا بعيد; إلا إذا سُمِعَ بهذا المعنى، وبعيد إرادتها عند أهل اللُّغةِ والظَّاهِرُ أَنَّهُ معنِّى عرفي، ثم رأيتُ الميبديَّ في شَرْحِ الطَّوالع صرَّحَ بذلك ; حيثُ قال : المراد بالفكرِ هَهُنا هو النَّظَرُ، وقدْ يُطلَقُ الفِكْرُ على حركةِ النَّفْسِ في المعقولات; أي حركةٍ كانت، ويُقَابِلُهُ التَّخييلُ; وهو حركتها في المحسوسات، وعلى حركتها في المطالبِ . . . إلخ، فدل هذا على أنَّهُ معنِّى عرفيّ لأهل المعقولِ لَا لُغَوِيٌّ ; وإن وقع التصريح بذلك في كلام كثير (قَوْلُهُ: كَمُلَاحَظَةِ الْحَيْوَانِ وَالنَّاطِقِ قِيلَ : كانَ عدولُ الشَّارِح عن الحيوانِ النَّاطِقِ ; أعني الهيئة التَّركيبيَّة; لئلا يتوهَّمُ أَنَّ مُلاحظة المجموع بملاحظة أحدِهِمَا كافية ا.هـ. (وأقولُ) : ملاحظة المجموع قاضية بملاحظةِ الأجزاء وإن لم يكن ذلك على سبيل التفصيل ; لأنَّ المجموع هو عينُ الأجزاء مجتمعةً، فلا تنفك ملاحظة أحدِهِمَا
Mir Ghiyath objected: If he meant that the acquisition of premises in the mind in the case of intuition is not by intention and choice, then often their acquisition in the case of speculation is likewise. How not? Most of its premises are self-evident matters, not known how they were acquired. If he meant that attention and turning to the premises obtained in the case of speculation is by intention and choice, unlike intuition, then it is denied. No distinction appears between the two cases at all, as is not hidden from anyone with understanding. (I say): It became clear to me, when analyzing this issue, that the second movement does not exist in intuition, only the first. So the objection does not apply to one who defined nazar as the two movements or as arrangement. As for the first, it is obvious; as for the second, because I know that arrangement is necessary for the second movement. As for its applicability to the definition of nazar as observation, it is conjectural, and it is repelled by making the lam for purpose, as we said, because then its object is a cause arranged in order, implying that what is sought to be obtained is posterior to the intelligibles under consideration, which is not the case for intuition. Also, what is meant by observation of the intelligibles is the observation that occurs within the transition from the sought to the premises, then from them to it, as al-Maibudi explicitly stated in his commentary on al-Tawali'. The commentator said: An objection was also made against the definition that it applies to the first movement in the case of the two movements combined, even though nazar is the combination in this case by agreement, and it applies to the observation of previously known arranged premises, such as when the laughing body is known previously in this arrangement, and the soul observes it intending to obtain man. No one said that thought exists without arrangement in non-nazar in the singular, and that there is no difference between it and the singular. (I say): There is no applicability. As for the first case, because the first movement, as you have heard, obtains the premises of the sought, and their acquisition by the soul only occurs after the arrangement achieved by the second movement. So after the observation is restricted by the goal as we said in excluding intuition, the definition does not apply to this case. As for defining it as the two movements combined or by arrangement, its truth is obvious, and therefore he limited the applicability to the author's definition by observation. As for the second case, despite the awkwardness in the phrasing, it also does not apply. As for the awkwardness, his statement 'no one said...' etc. is part of the objection, and one might mistakenly think the sentence is independent. His statement 'and that there is no difference...' etc. means that this case differs from the singular, but no difference occurred between them in lack of arrangement. His statement 'in non-nazar in the singular' suggests that there is no arrangement in it, but that is not as he claimed, for they discussed its interpretation until they confirmed arrangement in it. As for its non-applicability, because if the premises are arranged and known, then knowledge of the sought is also obtained by compulsion, such that the soul cannot repel it. So where is the unknown sought to be obtained? Whoever follows him in his speech and troubles himself to repel it knows what is in it. There remains something here: they often say: 'fikr linguistically is the movement of the soul among intelligibles', and its counterpart is takhyil (imagination). Making this a linguistic meaning is far-fetched unless it is heard in that sense. And it is unlikely that linguists intend it. The apparent is that it is a customary meaning. Then I saw al-Maibudi in his commentary on al-Tawali' stated that explicitly: he said: 'By fikr here is meant nazar, and fikr is sometimes applied to the movement of the soul among intelligibles, any movement, and its counterpart is takhyil, which is its movement among sensibles, and to its movement in seeking...' etc. This indicates that it is a customary meaning among the rational scholars, not a linguistic one, even if it is explicitly stated in many statements. (His statement: 'like the observation of animal and rational', it was said: The commentator's deviation from 'animal rational'—meaning the composite form—was to avoid the misunderstanding that observing the whole by observing one of them is sufficient. (I say): Observing the whole necessitates observing the parts even if not in detail, because the whole is the set of parts itself, so the observation of one does not separate from the other.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) by al-Dasuqi on the discussion of the definition of nazar (speculative thought) and its distinction from hads (intuition). The commentary refutes objections raised by Mir Ghiyath and others. The author begins by reporting Mir Ghiyath's objection: if one argues that intuition lacks voluntary acquisition of premises while speculation has it, it is countered that speculation often also involves involuntary premises (self-evident truths). Similarly, if they claim that in speculation one deliberately attends to premises but not in intuition, this is denied as no real distinction exists. The author then presents his own analysis: he discovered that the 'second movement' (arrangement of premises) does not occur in intuition, only the first movement (acquisition of premises). Therefore, objections to definitions of nazar as two movements or as arrangement do not hold. He explains that arrangement is inherent to the second movement, so defining nazar as arrangement implicitly includes the second movement. The objection to defining nazar as 'observation of intelligibles' is speculative but can be repelled by interpreting the lam as indicating purpose, implying that the observed intelligibles are causes arranged toward a conclusion, not the case for intuition. The author quotes al-Maibudi to support that observation must involve transition from sought to premises and back. Then he addresses another objection: the definition could apply to the first movement alone in a case where both movements are present, but he refutes this by noting that the first movement only yields premises after arrangement from the second movement, and the restriction of observation by a goal excludes intuition. For the case of previously known arranged premises, he argues that if premises are already arranged and known, then the conclusion is known by necessity, so it is not a case of seeking unknown knowledge. Finally, the author discusses the term 'fikr' (thought), noting that while it is often defined linguistically as movement of the soul among intelligibles, it is actually a customary (urfī) term among scholars of rational sciences, as al-Maibudi confirmed. He also clarifies a point about the example 'observation of animal and rational': the commentator deliberately used 'animal and rational' instead of 'rational animal' (the composite) to avoid misconception that observing one part suffices. The author concludes that observing the whole implies observing the parts, even if not in detail. The commentary follows the argument structure of the original hashya, explaining each objection and response sequentially.

Key Terms
النَّظَر
الحدس
الفكر
الملاحظة
الحركة الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between nazar (speculation) and hads (intuition)?
A) Nazar involves the first movement of premise acquisition, while hads does not.
B) Hads involves both first and second movements, while nazar involves only the first.
C) Nazar includes the second movement (arrangement) of premises, while hads lacks it.
D) Hads is voluntary, while nazar is involuntary.

Key Takeaway
The essential difference between nazar and hads is that nazar includes deliberate arrangement of premises (second movement), while hads does not.
