Fikr: Technical Term, Not Linguistic
Commentator defines fikr and distinguishes it from takhyil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conventional definition of fikr from its linguistic meaning and identify its contrast with takhyil.

Core Idea
Fikr is the soul's movement in intelligibles, the engine of logical inquiry, not mere imagination.

In everyday Arabic, 'fikr' means any kind of thought or reflection. But in logic, it has a precise technical meaning. This passage clarifies that fikr is the movement of the soul in intelligibles, and it is strictly distinct from takhyil, which is movement in sensibles. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the scope of logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under discussion
Look for the Arabic word الفكر in the passage. It is the term being defined.
2
Extract the definition given
The passage defines fikr as 'حركة النفس في المعقولات' (movement of the soul in intelligibles). Write this definition down.
3
Contrast with takhyil
Note that takhyil is defined as 'حركتها في المحسوسات' (movement in sensibles). This is the opposite of fikr.
4
Determine the nature of this definition
The commentator argues this definition is a conventional (عرفي) meaning among logicians, not a linguistic (لغوي) one. The proof is that al-Mibadi explicitly states that fikr here means nazar (inquiry).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقي ههنا شيء ؛ وهو أنهم كثيراً ما يقولون : الفكر لغة : حركة النفس في المعقولات، ويقابله التخييل، وجعل هذا معنى لغوياً بعيد؛ إلا إذا سمع بهذا المعنى، وبعيد إرادتها عند أهل اللغة والظاهر أنه معنى عرفي، ثم رأيت الميبدي في شرح الطوالع صرح بذلك ؛ حيث قال : المراد بالفكر ههنا هو النظر، وقد يطلق الفكر على حركة النفس في المعقولات؛ أي حركة كانت، ويقابله التخييل؛ وهو حركتها في المحسوسات، وعلى حركتها في المطالب ... إلخ، فدل هذا على أنه معنى عرفي لأهل المعقول لا لغوي ; وإن وقع التصريح بذلك في كلام كثير
And there remains here something: they often say: 'Thought linguistically is the movement of the soul in intelligibles, and it is contrasted with imagination.' Making this a linguistic meaning is far-fetched, unless it is heard in this meaning, and it is unlikely that it is intended among the linguists. The apparent is that it is a conventional meaning. Then I saw al-Mibadi in his commentary on al-Tawali' explicitly stating that: he said 'The intended meaning of thought here is inquiry, and thought may be applied to the movement of the soul in intelligibles, any movement, and it is contrasted with imagination, which is its movement in sensibles, and also its movement in the demands' etc. So this indicates that it is a conventional meaning among the people of intellectual sciences, not a linguistic one, even though it is explicitly mentioned in many discourses.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentator (likely the author of this sharh) clarifying the definition of 'fikr' (thought). The passage begins by noting that scholars frequently define fikr linguistically as 'the movement of the soul in intelligibles', contrasted with 'takhyil' (imagination) which is movement in sensibles. However, the commentator argues that this cannot be a true linguistic meaning; it is unlikely that language users would intend this sense. Rather, it is a technical (conventional) term among logicians. To support this, the commentator references al-Mibadi's commentary on al-Tawali', where al-Mibadi explicitly states that the intended meaning of fikr is 'nazar' (inquiry), and that fikr is also used for the soul's movement in the intelligibles. Thus, the passage establishes that fikr in the context of logic is a technical term, not a linguistic one. The example from al-Mibadi reinforces that fikr is contrasted with takhyil, the latter being movement in sensibles. This distinction is crucial for understanding the epistemological framework of logic, where thought (fikr) is the tool for reasoning from known to unknown.

Why al-Ma'qul Here Means al-Ma'lum: A Definitional Refinement
Commentator refines the definition of nazar by clarifying that al-ma'qul means al-ma'lum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope of the term 'al-ma'qul' in the definition of nazar and explain why the commentator refines it to include non-certain knowledge.

Core Idea
A definition must cover all instances; 'ma'lum' risks excluding opinion and doubt, so 'ma'qul' is broader.

In the Islamic logical tradition, the definition of nazar (inquiry) is central. The matn states that 'al-ma'qul' in this definition means 'al-ma'lum' (the known). However, the commentator warns that 'ilm (knowledge) is often understood as certain belief, which would restrict the definition to only certain inquiries. Since nazar includes opinions, ignorances, and blind following, the definition must be broad enough. This lesson unpacks that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'nazar' in the passage. What is the full definition being discussed? The matn defines nazar using 'al-ma'qul'.
The passage is a commentary on that definition.
2
Locate the commentator's refinement
The commentator says 'the intended meaning of al-ma'qul here is al-ma'lum'. Then he raises a problem: the immediate understanding of 'ilm is authoritative certainty, which would make 'ma'lum' specific to certainties.
Note the shift from 'ma'qul' to 'ma'lum'.
3
Understand why the refinement is problematic
The definition of nazar must include all types of inquiry: certain (yaqini), opinion-based (zanni), ignorant (jahili), and taqlid (followership). If 'ma'lum' is restricted to certainties, the definition becomes incomplete.
Recall the earlier lesson on the scope of nazar.
4
Evaluate the commentator's proposed solution
The commentator first suggests it would have been better to leave 'ma'qul' as it is, because it naturally includes all divisions. Then he presents a counter-argument: the author of the matn equated them to indicate that in this context they are synonyms, and the restriction of 'ilm to certainties is itself a technical convention.
This shows the nuance in Islamic logic: definitions must be precise yet inclusive.
5
Apply the lesson to other definitions
In any definition you encounter in Islamic sciences (e.g., usul al-fiqh), check whether the definiens covers all intended instances. If a term like 'ma'lum' might be too narrow, consider a broader alternative like 'ma'qul'.
This is a tool for evaluating definitions throughout your studies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: والمراد بالمعقول ههنا المعلوم لا يخفى أن المتبادر من العلم هو الاعتقاد الجازم الثابت المطابق للواقع ; فالمعلوم يختص باليقينيات، مع التعريف شامل لأفراد النظر مطلقاً من ظنيات وجهليات وتقليديات ; لوجوب شمول التعريف لها في اصطلاح القوم ; وقد تقدم ذلك، فلو أبقى الكلام على ظاهر لكان أحسن، إذ المعقول شامل لهذه الأقسام، وقد يجاب بأنه نبه بذلك على أن المعقول ههنا والمعلوم على حد سواء، وتخصيص العلم باليقينيات اصطلاح (قوله: بغيرها أتى بالضمير مؤنثاً ومرجعه مذكر وهو العقل لتأويله بمؤنث: أي لطيفة ربانية تدرك بها النفس العلوم الضرورية والنظرية).
His statement: 'And the intended meaning of al-ma'qul here is al-ma'lum (the known).' It is not hidden that what is immediately understood from 'ilm (knowledge) is the firm, fixed, and reality-corresponding belief; thus al-ma'lum is specific to certainties, while the definition is inclusive of all instances of nazar (inquiry) whether they be opinions, ignorances, or taqlid (blind following) – because in the convention of the people (logicians) the definition must encompass them, and that has been mentioned previously. If he had left the statement on its apparent meaning, it would have been better, for al-ma'qul includes these divisions. And it may be answered that by that he alerted that al-ma'qul here and al-ma'lum are on the same level, and the specification of 'ilm to certainties is a technical term. (Footnote: 'without it' – the pronoun is feminine while its antecedent is masculine – 'aql – because it is interpreted as a feminine word: i.e., a divine subtlety by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences.)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the hashiya or super-commentary. It addresses the definition of 'nazar' (inquiry) in the matn, where the term 'al-ma'qul' (the intelligible) is used. The commentator (likely al-Dasuqi or similar) notes that the author of the matn (the one being commented upon) said: 'And what is meant by al-ma'qul here is al-ma'lum (the known).' The commentator then clarifies that the immediate understanding of 'ilm (knowledge) is authoritative certainty, which would restrict 'ma'lum' to certainties. However, the definition of nazar must include all types of human inquiry, including opinion, ignorance, and blind following (taqlid), because that is the convention among logicians. The commentator suggests that leaving the statement as it is (without equating ma'qul to ma'lum) would be better, because ma'qul itself is broad enough to include these divisions. Then he presents a counter-argument: perhaps the author's equation serves to indicate that in this context, ma'qul and ma'lum are synonymous, and the restriction of 'ilm to certainties is itself a technical convention. A footnote explains the grammar of a pronoun in the original commentary. Overall, the passage refines the definition to ensure it captures the full scope of what is studied in logic.

The Definition of 'Ilm in Logic as Acquisition of the Form
Commentators define 'ilm in logic as acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the logical definition of 'ilm and explain how it differs from the kalam definition.

Core Idea
In logic, 'ilm is the mental form itself, not certain belief.

This passage defines 'ilm in the context of logic. Unlike theology, where 'ilm is certain belief matching reality, logic treats 'ilm as the acquisition of the mental representation of the thing. This definition is broader and includes even imaginary forms. Let's dissect its components and understand why this distinction matters for the science of logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition in the text
Find the phrase 'حصول صورة الشيء في العقل' in the Arabic. That is the definiens for 'ilm in logic.
2
Identify genus and differentia
The genus is 'acquisition in the intellect' (husul fi al-'aql); the differentia is 'the form of the thing' (surat al-shay'). This distinguishes it from other mental acquisitions.
Some scholars debate whether the genus is a quality or passion, but the text presents this as the definition.
3
Compare with the definition in kalam
Al-Dasuqi notes that in kalam, 'ilm is firm belief matching reality. In logic, it is the mental form itself, which may be uncertain.
This contrast highlights the different purposes of each science.
4
Test the definition's scope
The text later says this definition covers all types of knowledge: sensation, intellection, estimation, imagination. Does it include presential knowledge? The passage suggests it can.
The definition is intentionally broad to serve logic's concern with mental forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ العِلمَ في هذا الفنّ مُفسَّر بـ : حصول صورةِ الشَّيء في العقل .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ الْعِلْمَ توجيه لكون المراد بالمعقول ههنا المعلوم (قَوْلُهُ : فِي هَذَا الْفَنِّ) أي: فنّ المنطق، وأما في غير هذا الفنّ كعلم الكلام ) ؛ فإنَّ العِلْمَ فيه الجزم المطابق للواقع.
(قَوْلُهُ : مُفَسَّر ... إلخ) أي : وحينئذ؛ فَصورةُ الشَّيء الحاصلة في العقل معلوم، ومعقول.
العطار
المتكلمينَ،  َولِذَلِكَ عقبه بقوله : (فإنَّ العِلْم ... إلخ)، وأيضاً قَد يُتوهّمُ أنَّ المراد بالمعقول؛ المعقولُ الصِّرْفُ المُقَابِلُ لِلمحسوس والمخيَّل ؛ أي : الصُّورةُ الحاصِلَةُ في الخيال، فإنَّها ليست معقولة صرفةٌ، والمعلوم شامل لذلك.
(قَوْلُهُ: حُصُولُ صُوْرَةِ الشَّيْء .. إِلَخ) فيهِ مُسامحة؛ أي: الصُّورةُ الحاصِلَةُ بناءً على أنَّ العِلْمَ مِن مقولةِ الكَيْفِ، وفائدةُ جَعْلِهِ نَفْسَ الحصولِ؛ التنبيه على لزوم الإضافةِ لَهُ، فإنْ كانَ العِلْمُ مِن مقولة الانفعالِ ؛ فالتَّعريفُ على ظاهرِهِ بِلَا تأويل، وأنَّ المراد بحصولِ الصُّورةِ؛ انتقاشُهَا وارتسامُها في الذِّهْنِ وانصافه بها، والقائِلُ بالصُّورةِ هَهُنَا هُمُ الحُكَمَاءُ وبعضُ المتكلمين المثنِينَ لِلوجودِ الذهني، والمنكِرُ لَهُ يُفسِّرُ العِلْمَ بأَنَّهُ تعلَّق بين العالم والمعلوم، أو صفةٌ حقيقيَّةٌ ذاتُ إضافة، وقال مير غياث : العِلْمُ يُطلَقُ على المعنى المصدري الَّذي يُعبر عنه بالفارسية: «دانستن»، وهذا ليس بكيف بل نسبةٌ وأُخرى على الصُّورةِ الَّتي تنكشفُ بها الأشياء، وحينئذ يكون كيفاً ، فَمَنْ عَرَّفَ العِلْمَ بحصولِ الصُّورة؛ أرادَ بِهِ المعنى الأَوَّلَ أَوَّلاً ، ثم جعل ذلكَ ذَريعة إلى المعنى الثَّاني ثانياً ، ومَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بِالصُّورة الحاصلة؛ قصد به المعنى الثَّاني أوَّلاً .ا.هـ. ثم إن جعل هذا تعريفاً للمعنى الأعمّ لِلعِلْمِ الشَّامِلِ لِلحضوري والحصولي بأنواعه الأربعة؛ وهي الإحساس والتَّعقُلُ والتَّوهُمُ والتَّخيُّل، ولما يكونُ
(١) (قَوْلُهُ : كعلم الكلام أدخلت الكاف العلم عند الأصوليين فإنه حكم الذهن الجازم المطابق للواقع عن دليل بناء على أنه نظري وقيل : إنه ضروري فلا يحدّ وقال إمام الحرمين : عسر فالرأي الإمساك عن تعريفه عنده ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
For indeed 'ilm in this science (fan) is interpreted as: the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'For indeed al-'ilm' is a justification for the meaning of al-ma'qul here being al-ma'lum. His statement: 'in this science' means: the science of logic. As for other than this science, like the science of kalam, there al-'ilm is certain belief matching reality. His statement: 'interpreted... etc.' means: then at that point, the form of the thing acquired in the intellect is 'known' (ma'lum) and 'intellectible' (ma'qul). Al-'Attar: And it is also possible to suppose that what is meant by al-ma'qul is the pure intellectual concept opposed to the sensory and imaginary; i.e., the form acquired in the imagination, for it is not a pure intellectual concept. And al-ma'lum includes that. His statement: 'the acquisition of the form of the thing... etc.' contains a leniency; i.e., the acquired form, based on the view that al-'ilm is from the category of quality (kayf). The benefit of making it the acquisition itself is to alert to the necessity of it having a relation (idafa). For if al-'ilm is from the category of passion (infi'al), then the definition is taken literally without interpretation, and that what is meant by 'acquisition of the form' is its engraving and imprinting in the mind and its being endowed with it. Those who affirm the form here are the philosophers (hukama') and some theologians who affirm mental existence. Those who deny it interpret al-'ilm as a relation (ta'allaq) between the knower and the known, or as a real attribute with relation. Mir Ghayath said: al-'ilm is used for the verbal noun meaning expressed in Persian as 'danistan', and this is not a quality but a relation; and another usage is for the form by which things are revealed, and then it is a quality. So whoever defined al-'ilm as the acquisition of the form intended thereby the first meaning initially, then made that a means to the second meaning secondarily. And whoever interpreted it as the acquired form intended thereby the second meaning initially. End. Then if this is made a definition for the general meaning of al-'ilm that includes presential knowledge (huduri) and acquired knowledge (husuli) in its four types: sensation, intellection, estimation (tawahhum), and imagination (takhayyul). And when it is...
Footnote: (His statement: 'like the science of kalam' – the kaf is included. 'Ilm according to usuliyyun is the mind's firm judgment matching reality based on evidence, built on the view that it is theoretical. It is said that it is necessary and thus cannot be defined. Al-Imam al-Haramayn said: it is difficult, so the opinion is to refrain from defining it. End. Al-Sharnubi.)
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines 'ilm in the science of logic. The matn author states that 'ilm in logic is interpreted as 'the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect'. The commentator al-Dasuqi relates this back to the previous discussion: this definition justifies why 'ma'qul' was equated with 'ma'lum'. He notes that in other sciences like kalam, 'ilm is defined as certain belief matching reality, but in logic, the definition is different. Al-'Attar then expands on this, noting that some might think 'ma'qul' refers only to pure intellectual concepts, but the definition of 'ilm as the form includes even imaginary forms. He explains that the formulation 'acquisition of the form' is somewhat lenient; it assumes 'ilm belongs to the category of quality (kayf). If one considers 'ilm to be of the category of passion (infi'al), then the definition is literal: the form is imprinted in the mind. The debate over whether 'ilm is a quality or a relation is highlighted: philosophers and some theologians who affirm mental existence describe 'ilm as a mental form; those who deny mental existence interpret 'ilm as a relation or an attribute. Mir Ghayath's view is cited: 'ilm can refer to the verbal noun (knowing) which is a relation, or to the mental form itself which is a quality. Thus the definition can be understood in two ways. Finally, the commentators note that if this definition is taken broadly, it covers all types of knowledge: presential (huduri) and acquired (husuli), including sensation, intellection, estimation, and imagination. The footnote adds that in usul al-fiqh, 'ilm is defined differently, and some scholars refrain from defining it altogether.

Key Terms
العلم
صورة الشيء
الحصول

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the definition of 'ilm in logic differ from its definition in 'ilm al-kalam'?
A) In logic, 'ilm is defined as the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect; in kalam, it is defined as firm, truth-matching belief.
B) In logic, 'ilm is defined as firm belief; in kalam, it is defined as the acquisition of the form.
C) Both sciences define 'ilm the same way.
D) In logic, 'ilm is defined as a relation between knower and known; in kalam, it is defined as a quality.

Key Takeaway
The logical definition of 'ilm as the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect is broader than the certain belief definition in kalam.

Take the definition of 'ilm from the passage and identify its genus and differentia. Then, explain why this definition would include the mental form of a thing even if that form is not certain (e.g., a conjecture).

Commentary on the Definition: Debates and Distinctions
Commentators define 'ilm in logic as acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentators' expansions on the definition, including the debate on whether 'ilm is a quality or relation and the contrast with kalam.

Core Idea
The logical definition of 'ilm is flexible; it can be taken as a quality or a relation, and it includes presential knowledge.

The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar expand on the definition of 'ilm in logic. They highlight that this definition is specific to logic, unlike kalam. They also discuss whether 'ilm is a quality or a relation, and how the definition accommodates both views. This lesson explores their commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim
The matn says 'ilm is interpreted as 'husul surat al-shay' fi al-'aql'. This is the core claim being commented upon.
2
Extract al-Dasuqi's expansion
Al-Dasuqi notes that in logic it means this, but in kalam it means certain belief. He also relates it to the previous discussion equating ma'qul and ma'lum.
This contextualizes the definition within the broader discussion of mental concepts.
3
Extract al-'Attar's expansion
Al-'Attar discusses the leniency in the phrase: if 'ilm is a quality, the definition means the acquired form; if it is a passion, it means the imprinting. He also references the debate on mental existence and includes Mir Ghayath's distinction between two meanings of 'ilm.
This shows the metaphysical assumptions behind the definition.
4
Synthesize the commentators' contributions
Together, they refine the definition: it is specific to logic, it includes all mental forms, and its formulation can be interpreted in different ontological frameworks.
The commentary chain adds depth and precision to the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ العِلمَ في هذا الفنّ مُفسَّر بـ : حصول صورةِ الشَّيء في العقل .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ الْعِلْمَ توجيه لكون المراد بالمعقول ههنا المعلوم (قَوْلُهُ : فِي هَذَا الْفَنِّ) أي: فنّ المنطق، وأما في غير هذا الفنّ كعلم الكلام ) ؛ فإنَّ العِلْمَ فيه الجزم المطابق للواقع.
(قَوْلُهُ : مُفَسَّر ... إلخ) أي : وحينئذ؛ فَصورةُ الشَّيء الحاصلة في العقل معلوم، ومعقول.
العطار
المتكلمينَ،  َولِذَلِكَ عقبه بقوله : (فإنَّ العِلْم ... إلخ)، وأيضاً قَد يُتوهّمُ أنَّ المراد بالمعقول؛ المعقولُ الصِّرْفُ المُقَابِلُ لِلمحسوس والمخيَّل ؛ أي : الصُّورةُ الحاصِلَةُ في الخيال، فإنَّها ليست معقولة صرفةٌ، والمعلوم شامل لذلك.
(قَوْلُهُ: حُصُولُ صُوْرَةِ الشَّيْء .. إِلَخ) فيهِ مُسامحة؛ أي: الصُّورةُ الحاصِلَةُ بناءً على أنَّ العِلْمَ مِن مقولةِ الكَيْفِ، وفائدةُ جَعْلِهِ نَفْسَ الحصولِ؛ التنبيه على لزوم الإضافةِ لَهُ، فإنْ كانَ العِلْمُ مِن مقولة الانفعالِ ؛ فالتَّعريفُ على ظاهرِهِ بِلَا تأويل، وأنَّ المراد بحصولِ الصُّورةِ؛ انتقاشُهَا وارتسامُها في الذِّهْنِ وانصافه بها، والقائِلُ بالصُّورةِ هَهُنَا هُمُ الحُكَمَاءُ وبعضُ المتكلمين المثنِينَ لِلوجودِ الذهني، والمنكِرُ لَهُ يُفسِّرُ العِلْمَ بأَنَّهُ تعلَّق بين العالم والمعلوم، أو صفةٌ حقيقيَّةٌ ذاتُ إضافة، وقال مير غياث : العِلْمُ يُطلَقُ على المعنى المصدري الَّذي يُعبر عنه بالفارسية: «دانستن»، وهذا ليس بكيف بل نسبةٌ وأُخرى على الصُّورةِ الَّتي تنكشفُ بها الأشياء، وحينئذ يكون كيفاً ، فَمَنْ عَرَّفَ العِلْمَ بحصولِ الصُّورة؛ أرادَ بِهِ المعنى الأَوَّلَ أَوَّلاً ، ثم جعل ذلكَ ذَريعة إلى المعنى الثَّاني ثانياً ، ومَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بِالصُّورة الحاصلة؛ قصد به المعنى الثَّاني أوَّلاً .ا.هـ. ثم إن جعل هذا تعريفاً للمعنى الأعمّ لِلعِلْمِ الشَّامِلِ لِلحضوري والحصولي بأنواعه الأربعة؛ وهي الإحساس والتَّعقُلُ والتَّوهُمُ والتَّخيُّل، ولما يكونُ
(١) (قَوْلُهُ : كعلم الكلام أدخلت الكاف العلم عند الأصوليين فإنه حكم الذهن الجازم المطابق للواقع عن دليل بناء على أنه نظري وقيل : إنه ضروري فلا يحدّ وقال إمام الحرمين : عسر فالرأي الإمساك عن تعريفه عنده ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
For indeed 'ilm in this science (fan) is interpreted as: the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'For indeed al-'ilm' is a justification for the meaning of al-ma'qul here being al-ma'lum. His statement: 'in this science' means: the science of logic. As for other than this science, like the science of kalam, there al-'ilm is certain belief matching reality. His statement: 'interpreted... etc.' means: then at that point, the form of the thing acquired in the intellect is 'known' (ma'lum) and 'intellectible' (ma'qul). Al-'Attar: And it is also possible to suppose that what is meant by al-ma'qul is the pure intellectual concept opposed to the sensory and imaginary; i.e., the form acquired in the imagination, for it is not a pure intellectual concept. And al-ma'lum includes that. His statement: 'the acquisition of the form of the thing... etc.' contains a leniency; i.e., the acquired form, based on the view that al-'ilm is from the category of quality (kayf). The benefit of making it the acquisition itself is to alert to the necessity of it having a relation (idafa). For if al-'ilm is from the category of passion (infi'al), then the definition is taken literally without interpretation, and that what is meant by 'acquisition of the form' is its engraving and imprinting in the mind and its being endowed with it. Those who affirm the form here are the philosophers (hukama') and some theologians who affirm mental existence. Those who deny it interpret al-'ilm as a relation (ta'allaq) between the knower and the known, or as a real attribute with relation. Mir Ghayath said: al-'ilm is used for the verbal noun meaning expressed in Persian as 'danistan', and this is not a quality but a relation; and another usage is for the form by which things are revealed, and then it is a quality. So whoever defined al-'ilm as the acquisition of the form intended thereby the first meaning initially, then made that a means to the second meaning secondarily. And whoever interpreted it as the acquired form intended thereby the second meaning initially. End. Then if this is made a definition for the general meaning of al-'ilm that includes presential knowledge (huduri) and acquired knowledge (husuli) in its four types: sensation, intellection, estimation (tawahhum), and imagination (takhayyul). And when it is...
Footnote: (His statement: 'like the science of kalam' – the kaf is included. 'Ilm according to usuliyyun is the mind's firm judgment matching reality based on evidence, built on the view that it is theoretical. It is said that it is necessary and thus cannot be defined. Al-Imam al-Haramayn said: it is difficult, so the opinion is to refrain from defining it. End. Al-Sharnubi.)
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines 'ilm in the science of logic. The matn author states that 'ilm in logic is interpreted as 'the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect'. The commentator al-Dasuqi relates this back to the previous discussion: this definition justifies why 'ma'qul' was equated with 'ma'lum'. He notes that in other sciences like kalam, 'ilm is defined as certain belief matching reality, but in logic, the definition is different. Al-'Attar then expands on this, noting that some might think 'ma'qul' refers only to pure intellectual concepts, but the definition of 'ilm as the form includes even imaginary forms. He explains that the formulation 'acquisition of the form' is somewhat lenient; it assumes 'ilm belongs to the category of quality (kayf). If one considers 'ilm to be of the category of passion (infi'al), then the definition is literal: the form is imprinted in the mind. The debate over whether 'ilm is a quality or a relation is highlighted: philosophers and some theologians who affirm mental existence describe 'ilm as a mental form; those who deny mental existence interpret 'ilm as a relation or an attribute. Mir Ghayath's view is cited: 'ilm can refer to the verbal noun (knowing) which is a relation, or to the mental form itself which is a quality. Thus the definition can be understood in two ways. Finally, the commentators note that if this definition is taken broadly, it covers all types of knowledge: presential (huduri) and acquired (husuli), including sensation, intellection, estimation, and imagination. The footnote adds that in usul al-fiqh, 'ilm is defined differently, and some scholars refrain from defining it altogether.

Key Takeaway
The commentators show that the definition of 'ilm in logic is nuanced and depends on whether 'ilm is considered a quality or a relation.

What Is Mantiq?

Refining the Definition of Knowledge in Logic: Acquisition of the Form of a Thing in the Mind
Commentators refine the definition of 'ilm in mantiq and analyze its ontological status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical definition of 'ilm, distinguish it from the theological definition, and explain how different ontological interpretations of the definition affect its meaning.

Core Idea
In logic, knowledge is mental representation, not certainty.

The passage begins with a clear definition: knowledge in the art of logic is 'the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind.' But as the commentators show, this simple phrase opens up a rich debate about the nature of knowledge. Al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar guide us through the nuances: distinguishing logical knowledge from theological certainty, and showing how the phrase can be interpreted differently depending on whether knowledge is considered a quality, a passion, an action, or a relation. This lesson will walk through that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition and its context
Locate the opening phrase: 'فإنَّ العِلمَ في هذا الفنّ مُفسَّر بـ : حصول صورةِ الشَّيء في العقل'. Note that it is specific to logic. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that in kalam, knowledge is defined as 'certain judgment conforming to reality'.
This shows that the logical definition is broader.
2
Analyse the syntactical ambiguity in 'حصول صورة'
The phrase can be read as idafa of attribute to described ('the acquired form') or real idafa ('acquisition of the form'). Al-Dasuqi explains that the first reading treats knowledge as a quality; the second requires interpreting 'acquisition' differently (passion, action, relation).
The grammatical structure affects ontological commitment.
3
Examine the commentators' refinement on the scope of knowledge
Al-'Attar argues that if the definition is restricted to acquired knowledge, many forced interpretations are avoided. He distinguishes between presential and acquired knowledge.
The definition is not static but refined by context.
4
Map ontological commitments to interpretations
List the four categories: quality (knowledge is the form itself), passion (impression in the mind), action (attaining the form), relation (relation between acquirer and acquired). Identify which view is preponderant according to al-Dasuqi (quality).
Understanding this helps evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

فإنَّ العِلمَ في هذا الفنّ مُفسَّر بـ : حصول صورةِ الشَّيء في العقل .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ الْعِلْمَ توجيه لكون المراد بالمعقول ههنا المعلوم (قَوْلُهُ : فِي هَذَا الْفَنِّ) أي: فنّ المنطق، وأما في غير هذا الفنّ كعلم الكلام )؛ فإنَّ العِلْمَ فيه الجزم المطابق للواقع.
(قَوْلُهُ : مُفَسَّر ... إلخ) أي : وحينئذ؛ فَصورةُ الشَّيء الحاصلة في في العقل معلوم، ومعقول.
العطار
المتكلمينَ،  َولِذَلِكَ عقبه بقوله : (فإنَّ العِلْم ... إلخ)، وأيضاً قَد يُتوهّمُ أنَّ المراد بالمعقول؛ المعقولُ الصِّرْفُ المُقَابِلُ لِلمحسوس والمخيَّل ؛ أي : الصُّورةُ الحاصِلَةُ في الخيال، فإنَّها ليست معقولة صرفةٌ، والمعلوم شامل لذلك.
(قَوْلُهُ: حُصُولُ صُوْرَةِ الشَّيْء .. إِلَخ) فيهِ مُسامحة؛ أي: الصُّورةُ الحاصِلَةُ بناءً على أنَّ العِلْمَ مِن مقولةِ الكَيْفِ، وفائدةُ جَعْلِهِ نَفْسَ الحصولِ؛ التنبيه على لزوم الإضافةِ لَهُ، فإنْ كانَ العِلْمُ مِن مقولة الانفعالِ ؛ فالتَّعريفُ على ظاهرِهِ بِلَا تأويل، وأنَّ المراد بحصولِ الصُّورةِ؛ انتقاشُهَا وارتسامُها في الذِّهْنِ وانصافه بها، والقائِلُ بالصُّورةِ هَهُنَا هُمُ الحُكَمَاءُ وبعضُ المتكلمين المثنِينَ لِلوجودِ الذهني، والمنكِرُ لَهُ يُفسِّرُ العِلْمَ بأَنَّهُ تعلَّق بين العالم والمعلوم، أو صفةٌ حقيقيَّةٌ ذاتُ إضافة، وقال مير غياث : العِلْمُ يُطلَقُ على المعنى المصدري الَّذي يُعبر عنه بالفارسية: «دانستن»، وهذا ليس بكيف بل نسبةٌ وأُخرى على الصُّورةِ الَّتي تنكشفُ بها الأشياء، وحينئذ يكون كيفاً ، فَمَنْ عَرَّفَ العِلْمَ بحصولِ الصُّورة؛ أرادَ بِهِ المعنى الأَوَّلَ أَوَّلاً ، ثم جعل ذلكَ ذَريعة إلى المعنى الثَّاني ثانياً ، ومَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بالصُّورة الحاصلة؛ قصد به المعنى الثَّاني أوَّلاً .ا.هـ. ثم إن جعل هذا تعريفاً للمعنى الأعمّ لِلعِلْمِ الشَّامِلِ لِلحضوري والحصولي بأنواعه الأربعة؛ وهي الإحساس والتَّعقُلُ والتَّوهُمُ والتَّخيُّل، ولما يكونُ
واعلَمْ أَنَّهُ إِنْ جُعِلَتْ إضافة حصول من إضافةِ الصِّفة للموصوف؛ أي: صورة الشَّيء الحاصلة في العقل؛ كانَتْ تلكَ الصُّورة مجزوماً بها أو مظنونةٌ؛ كانَتْ مطابقة للواقع أوْ لَا ، كانت تصوريَّةٌ أو تصديقيَّة ؛ كان مارا على القول بأنَّ العِلْمَ مِن قبيل الكيف، وهو الراجح، وإن جعلت الإضافة حقيقية، وهو المتبادر من كلامه، فإِنْ فُسِّرَ حصولُ الصُّورة بانتقاشِهَا في العقل؛ كانَ مارا على القول بأنَّ العِلْمَ انفعال، وإنْ فُسِّرَ بتحصيل الصُّورة في العقل؛ كانَ مارا على القول بأنَّهُ من قبيل الفعل، وإنْ فُسِّرَ بالنّسبة الحاصلة بين الحاصل والمحصول؛ كان مارا على القول بأنَّ العِلْمَ مِن قبيل الإضافة .
العطّار
نفس المدركِ وغيره؛ فالمراد بالعقل : الذَّاتُ المجرَّدَةُ المعبَّرُ عنهَا بِالنَّفْسِ النَّاطِقَةِ عندَهُم، وبالصُّورةِ ما يعم الخارجيَّة والذهنية، وبالحصول: الحصول ؛ سواء كان بنفسِهِ أو بمثالِهِ، وبالمغايرة المستفادةِ مِنَ الظرفيَّةِ أعمُّ مِنَ الذَّاتِيَّةِ والاعتباريَّةِ، وبلفظة «في الجارَّةِ معنى «عند»، وهذهِ كُلُّهَا تكلُّفات، وأَمَّا إِنْ جَعَلَ التَّعرِيفَ لِلعِلْمِ الحصوليّ لأنَّ الكلامَ هُنَا في تعريفِ العِلْمِ الَّذي يقعُ بِهِ الكَشبُ والعِلْمُ المكتَسَبُ ؛ لم يحتج إلى هذه التَّأويلات، والمراد بالعقل: قوَّةُ تدركُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ بِنَفْسِهَا ، والمحسوسات بالواسطة وبصورةِ الشَّيء ما يكونُ الةٌ لامتيازه؛ سواء كان نفس ماهية الشَّيء أو شَبَحاً له، بناءً على ما تقدَّمَ مِنَ الخِلَافِ في أنَّ الحاصِلَ في الذهن الأشياءُ أنفسها أو أشباحُها وأمثالها ، والظرفية باقية على معناها الحقيقي، ولَيْسَتْ بمعنى عندَ كَمَا هو على التَّأويل الأوَّلِ ، وقد سبق لكَ كَلامٌ يتعلَّقُ بِمَا هنا فَضَمَّهُ إِلَيهِ.
For knowledge in this art is interpreted as: the acquisition of the form of the thing in the mind.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'For knowledge' is a direction that what is meant by 'the intelligible' here is 'the known'. His statement: 'in this art' means the art of logic. As for other arts like theology, knowledge therein is judgment conforming to reality. (His statement: 'interpreted... etc.') means: and thus the form of the thing acquired in the mind is known and intelligible.
Al-'Attar: The theologians, and therefore he followed it with his statement: 'For knowledge... etc.' Also it might be imagined that what is meant by 'the intelligible' is pure intelligible as opposed to sensory and imagined, i.e., the form acquired in the imagination, for it is not pure intelligible, and 'the known' includes that. (His statement: 'the acquisition of the form of the thing... etc.') contains tolerance; i.e., the acquired form, based on the claim that knowledge is of the category of quality. The benefit of making it the acquisition itself is to alert to the necessity of relation to it. If knowledge is of the category of passion, then the definition is literal without interpretation, and what is meant by acquisition of the form is its impression and engraving in the mind and the mind's being characterized by it. The one who posits the form here are the philosophers and some theologians who affirm mental existence. The one who denies it interprets knowledge as a relation between the knower and the known, or a real attribute with relation. Mir Ghiyath said: knowledge is applied to the verbal noun meaning expressed in Persian as 'danistan', and this is not a quality but a relation; and another meaning is the form by which things are disclosed, and then it is a quality. Whoever defined knowledge as acquisition of the form intended the first meaning first, then made that a means to the second meaning secondarily. Whoever interpreted it as the acquired form aimed at the second meaning primarily. End. Then if this is made a definition of the broader meaning of knowledge encompassing presential and acquired knowledge in its four types: sensation, intellection, estimation, and imagination, and since it may be...
And know that if the idafa of 'acquisition' is considered as idafa of the attribute to the described, i.e., the form of the thing acquired in the mind, then that form may be certain or probable, conforming to reality or not, conceptual or assertoric; it proceeds on the view that knowledge is of the category of quality, which is the preponderant view. If the idafa is considered real, which is the apparent from his speech, then if acquisition of the form is interpreted as its engraving in the mind, it proceeds on the view that knowledge is a passion; if interpreted as the attaining of the form in the mind, it proceeds on the view that it is of the category of action; if interpreted as the relation obtaining between the acquirer and the acquired, it proceeds on the view that knowledge is of the category of relation.
Al-'Attar: The same as the perceiver and other; so what is meant by 'the mind' is the abstract essence expressed as the rational soul according to them; by 'form' what includes both external and mental; by 'acquisition' the acquisition, whether by itself or its likeness; by the distinctiveness derived from the spatial preposition it is more general than essential and considerational; and the preposition 'in' has the meaning of 'at'. All of these are forced interpretations. But if the definition is made for acquired knowledge because the discussion here is about defining knowledge by which acquisition occurs and acquired knowledge, then there is no need for these interpretations. What is meant by 'the mind' is the faculty that apprehends universals by itself and sense-perceptibles by means; and by 'form of the thing' that which is the instrument for its discrimination, whether it is the essence of the thing itself or a likeness of it, based on the preceding disagreement about whether what obtains in the mind is the things themselves or their likenesses and exemplars. The spatial preposition remains on its real meaning, not meaning 'at' as in the first interpretation. And there has preceded for you some speech related to what is here, so attach it to it.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of 'ilm (knowledge) in the art of logic, as presented in the matn (the original text) and expanded upon by the commentators. The primary definition given is: 'the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind' (حصول صورة الشيء في العقل). The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, engage with this definition from multiple angles, showing that it is not a straightforward settled matter.

Al-Dasuqi first clarifies the context: 'knowledge in this art' refers specifically to logic, distinguishing it from other disciplines like kalam (theology), where knowledge is defined as 'certain judgment conforming to reality' (الجزم المطابق للواقع). In logic, knowledge is understood more broadly as the mental representation (form) that is both 'known' (معلوم) and 'intelligible' (معقول).

Al-'Attar then addresses a potential misunderstanding: that 'the intelligible' might be taken as 'pure intelligible' (المعقول الصرف) separate from sensory or imagined forms. He shows that the term 'known' (معلوم) is more inclusive, covering both pure intelligibles and sensory/imaginary forms. He then dives into the ontological classification of knowledge. The phrase 'acquisition of the form' (حصول صورة) contains a certain tolerance (مسامحة). If knowledge is considered a quality (كيف), the acquisition itself is the form; if knowledge is a passion (انفعال), then the definition is literal, meaning the impression of the form in the mind. He also mentions the views of the philosophers and theologians who affirm mental existence (الوجود الذهني) and those who deny it, interpreting knowledge as a relation or a real attribute with relation.

Al-'Attar further cites Mir Ghiyath, who distinguishes two meanings of 'ilm: the verbal noun (meaning 'knowing') which is a relation, and the form by which things are disclosed, which is a quality. This leads to different definitions: someone might define knowledge as 'acquisition of the form' meaning the first meaning, then use that as a means to the second; or define it as 'the acquired form' meaning the second meaning primarily.

Al-Dasuqi returns to explain the syntactical construction: if the phrase 'حصول صورة' is understood as an idafa of attribute to described (i.e., 'the acquired form'), then the definition covers all types of knowledge (certain or probable, conceptual or assertoric) and aligns with the view that knowledge is a quality. If the idafa is real (meaning 'acquisition of the form'), then different interpretations of 'acquisition' lead to different categories: passion (engraving), action (attaining), or relation.

Finally, al-'Attar offers a more straightforward interpretation: if the definition is restricted to acquired knowledge (علم حصولي) as opposed to presential knowledge (علم حضوري), then many of the forced interpretations (تكلفات) are unnecessary. In that case, 'mind' (عقل) refers to the faculty apprehending universals directly and sense-perceptibles indirectly, and 'form of the thing' is the means of distinguishing the thing, whether the essence itself or a likeness. The preposition 'in' retains its real spatial meaning.

The passage thus demonstrates how a seemingly simple definition opens up into a complex discussion about the nature of knowledge, mental existence, and the categories of being. The commentators are not merely repeating the definition but critically refining it, showing where it is ambiguous and how different foundational commitments (quality vs. passion vs. action vs. relation) produce different understandings.

Key Terms
العلم
صورة
العقل
حصول
الكيف

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge in logic is broader than in theology and is ontologically flexible, accommodating different views on the category of knowledge.

Al-Dasuqi's Analysis of Fikr and Salb al-Umum
Al-Dasuqi explains the matn phrase and its logical form as particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical form of 'salb al-umum' and distinguish it from a universal negative, using al-Dasuqi's commentary on the definition of fikr.

Core Idea
Salb al-umum (negation of universality) is a particular negative: 'some are not', not 'none are'.

This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary explains a key phrase in the matn: 'because thought is not always correct'. He first defines what 'fikr' means in this context and then clarifies the precise logical meaning of the qualification 'always'. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how the possibility of error in thought motivates the need for logic. Al-Dasuqi's analysis shows how to correctly parse propositions with universal quantifiers and negations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'fikr' as defined
Find where al-Dasuqi defines 'fikr' as 'al-nazar al-muktasab bihi' (the consideration by which acquisition occurs). Note that he equates it with 'nazar' and emphasizes it is the means of acquiring theoretical knowledge, both conceptual and assenting.
This definition gives the genus (nazar) and differentia (bi-yaktasibu).
2
Locate the qualifier 'daiman' (always)
Examine the phrase 'ليس بصواب دائماً'. Notice that 'daiman' is attached to 'صواب' (correct), not to 'ليس'. Al-Dasuqi explains that this is a restriction on the negated attribute, not on the negation itself.
The placement of the quantifier affects logical meaning.
3
Distinguish between two possible readings
Al-Dasuqi says that if 'daiman' were attached to the negation, it would imply that incorrectness is permanent (universal negative: all thought is incorrect). Instead, the intended meaning is 'not (always correct)', which is a particular negative: some thought is not correct.
This is the key logical insight: 'not always correct' = 'some thought is not correct'.
4
Identify the two truth conditions
Al-Dasuqi explains that 'salb al-umum' (negation of universality) is true in two scenarios: (1) no individual of thought is correct, (2) some are not correct while others are. He selects the second as intended because it is the actual state.
This shows how to determine the correct logical form based on reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الْفِكْرَ) أي: الَّذي هو النَّظرُ المكتسب به؛ لأنَّه يكون به اکتساب العلوم النَّظريَّةِ ؛ تصوريَّةً كانت أو تصديقية، وقوله: (ليس بصواب)؛ أي: بمصيب دائماً؛ أي: في كلِّ الأوقات، وهذا قيد في المنفي لا في النَّفي، وإلا ؛ لاقتضى أنَّ عدمَ الصَّوابِ دائم، مع أنه ليس بمراد . والحاصلُ : أنَّ قولَه : (ليس بصواب دائماً ) من باب سلب العموم ، وحينئذ ؛ فيصدقُ بصورتين؛ إحداهما : أنْ لا يكون فرد من أفراد الفكر صواباً، والآخر: أن يكونَ بعض أفرادِهِ ليس بصواب، وبعضُهُ الآخَرُ صواباً ، وهذه الصورة هي المرادةُ ؛ لأنَّها المحققة.
(His statement: 'because thought') i.e., which is the consideration (nazar) by which acquisition is made; because by it the acquisition of theoretical sciences occurs, whether conceptual or assenting. And his statement: 'is not correct' i.e., not hitting the mark always; i.e., at all times. And this is a restriction on the negated, not on the negation. Otherwise, it would imply that incorrectness is permanent, which is not intended. The upshot: his statement 'is not correct always' is a case of negation of universality (salb al-umum). Therefore it is true in two forms: one: that no individual of thought is correct, and the other: that some individuals of it are not correct while others are correct. And this latter form is the intended one, because it is the one that is actual.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Dasuqi on the matn statement 'because thought is not always correct'. He first defines 'fikr' (thought) as the consideration (nazar) by which theoretical knowledge is acquired, whether conceptual (tasawwuri) or assenting (tasdiqi). Then he clarifies the phrase 'not always correct': it means it is not always hitting the mark, and the word 'always' is a restriction on the negated attribute (being correct always), not on the negation (so it does not mean thought is always incorrect). If it meant always incorrect, that would be false and unintended. He then analyzes the logical form: 'not always correct' is a case of 'salb al-umum' (negation of universality), which is equivalent to the particular negative proposition 'some thought is not correct'. This proposition could be true in two ways: either no thought is correct (universal negative) or some thought is correct and some not. The first is false in reality, so the intended meaning is the second: error occurs in some instances of thought, not all. This establishes the possibility of error, which is the foundation for the study of logic.

Key Terms
الفكر
سلب العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Particular negative: Some thought is not correct.
C) Universal affirmative: All thought is correct.
D) Particular affirmative: Some thought is correct.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'not always correct' in logic is interpreted as a particular negative (salb al-umum), not a universal negative, because the negation applies to the universality of the attribute, not to every instance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Right and Wrong in Thought: The Conditions for Valid Reasoning
Author defines sawab and khata for actions and explains conditions for right thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of sawab (right) and khata (wrong) and identify the conditions required for a thought to be considered right in the logical tradition.

Core Idea
Right thought is not just any thought—it must meet specific structural conditions for definition and syllogism.

This passage from the Tadhheeb defines the key terms sawab and khata as they apply to the act of reasoning itself. It is crucial to recognize that these terms have different meanings when applied to judgments versus actions. Here, the author focuses on thought as an action and lays down the precise conditions under which a thought is considered valid: for conceptualization, it must follow the proper order of definition; for assent, it must follow the rules of the first figure syllogism. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating whether your own reasoning is sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two domains of application
Notice that sawab/khata apply both to judgments (ahkam) and to actions (af‘al). In this passage, the author specifies which domain is relevant: actions, specifically the action of thinking.
2
Extract the definition of right thought
Find the phrase: 'the meaning of thought being right is that it conforms to the purpose by being comprehensive of the conditions.' This is the definition.
3
List the conditions for conceptualization (tasawwur)
The author mentions: genus before differentia in the explanatory statement (qawl sharih).
This ensures a proper definition.
4
List the conditions for assent (tasdiq)
The author mentions: minor premise affirmative, major premise universal, in a first-figure syllogism.
This ensures a valid categorical syllogism.
5
Contrast with wrong thought
What does it mean for thought to be wrong? It fails to meet these conditions. Use this to reinforce the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الصواب ضد الخطأ، ثم تارة يوصف بهما الحكم، وحينئذ يكون المراد بالصواب مطابقة الحكم للواقع، وبالخطأ عدم مطابقته للواقع، وتارة يوصف بهما الفعل كما هنا، وحينئذ يكون المراد بالصواب موافقة الفعل للغرض، وبالخطأ عدم مطابقته للغرض، فمعنى كون الفكر صواباً أنه موافق للغرض بأن يكون مستجمعاً للشروط؛ كأن يقع الجنس مقدماً على الفصل في ترتيب القول الشارح الموصل للتصور، وكأن تكون الصغرى موجبة، والكبرى كلية في ترتيب قياس من الشكل الأول موصل للتصديق، ومعنى كونه ليس بصواب؛ أنه لم يكن موافقاً للغرض؛ لكونه لم يحتو على الشروط كلها.
Know that right (sawab) is the opposite of wrong (khata). Then sometimes they are described to the judgment, and in that case the intended meaning of right is the correspondence of the judgment to reality, and of wrong is its non-correspondence to reality. And sometimes they are described to the action, as here, and in that case the intended meaning of right is the conformity of the action to the purpose, and of wrong is its non-conformity to the purpose. So the meaning of thought being right is that it conforms to the purpose by being comprehensive of the conditions: such as the genus being placed before the differentia in the arrangement of the explanatory statement leading to conceptualization (tasawwur), and such as the minor premise being affirmative and the major premise universal in the arrangement of a syllogism from the first figure leading to assent (tasdiq). And the meaning of it not being right is that it does not conform to the purpose because it does not encompass all the conditions.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'right' (sawab) and 'wrong' (khata) as applied to actions, specifically to the act of thinking (fikr). It begins by noting that these terms can apply either to judgments (ahkam) or to actions. When applied to judgments, right means correspondence to reality; wrong means lack of correspondence. When applied to actions, as in this context of thought, right means conformity with the intended purpose, and wrong means lack of such conformity. The commentary then specifies the conditions under which thought is considered right: if the thought aims at conceptualization (tasawwur), it must arrange the genus before the differentia in a definition; if it aims at assent (tasdiq), it must construct a syllogism in the first figure with an affirmative minor and a universal major. If these conditions are not met, the thought is not right. This definitional passage sets the framework for subsequent arguments about the possibility of error in thought.

Key Terms
صواب
خطأ
فكر
قول شارح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Right thought is defined by its conformity to purpose through specific structural requirements: proper definition order for conceptualization and proper syllogistic form for assent.

The Need for Logic: Safeguarding the Mind from Error
Author justifies the need for logic by demonstrating human fallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is necessary for avoiding contradictions in thought, using the example of the world's temporality.

Core Idea
Logic is the law that protects the mind from error when acquiring theoretical knowledge.

We all think, but thinking can lead to error. The passage shows how even rational thinkers can hold contradictory beliefs, such as believing the world is created at one time and eternal at another. This proves that we need a tool to ensure our reasoning is correct. That tool is logic, which safeguards the mind from falling into error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: thought is not always correct
The author points out that people may think and arrive at contradictory conclusions at different times, as in the example of someone who first assents to the temporality of the world and later to its pre-eternity. Since both cannot be true, at least one thought is erroneous.
This demonstrates the fallibility of human reason without a guiding law.
2
Understand the solution: a law (qanun) to safeguard
The solution is a 'qanun' (law) that protects from error. This law is the science of logic, which provides methods for acquiring theoretical knowledge from necessary premises.
The term 'qanun' here is not a legal code but a methodological standard.
3
See the method: acquiring theoretical from necessary
Logic provides methods (turuk) for proofs, their conditions, and explanatory statements. Theoretical knowledge (nazari) is acquired by building from necessary (daruri) premises, avoiding circularity or infinite regress.
This foundational idea ensures that all knowledge ultimately rests on self-evident truths.
4
Apply to the example: the thinker's contradiction
The commentator uses the example of the world's temporality to illustrate how thought can lead to contradiction. Without logic, one might accept both that the world is created and that it is pre-eternal. Logic helps us see the inconsistency and avoid it.
This example is directly from the passage: 'he assents to the temporality of the world... then to its pre-eternity'.
5
Conclusion: the purpose is protection (isma)
The passage concludes that the purpose of logic is 'ismat al-dhihn' (protection of the mind) from error in thought. This answers the question: 'In what do people need logic?' – for this protection.
The term 'isma' is usually used for prophetic infallibility, here applied to methodological protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فاحتجنا . . . إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَذَلِكَ بَيَانُ الْحَاجَةِ) أي : وذكرُ ذلكَ تبيين، أو: وذلك ذو بيانٍ العصمة المذكورة ؛ أي: التصديقُ بأنَّها غاية هذا العِلْمِ، وفائدته .
للحاجة، وهي
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ النَّاسَ ... إلخ) المصدرُ المنسبِكُ مِن إِلَخ) المصدرُ المنسيكُ مِن (أَنَّ) معَ صِلَتِها مِن مادَّةِ خبرها، وهو يحتاجون، وهو نائب فاعل عُلِمَ بحذف مُضَاف، والتَّقديرُ : عُلِمَ جوابُ احتياج النَّاسِ ؛ أي: جوابُ السُّؤال عن ذلكَ، فإذا قيل : في أي شيء قِيْلَ يحتاجُ النَّاسُ إِلى المنطق؟ يُقَالُ في الجواب: لعصمةِ الفكر عن الخطأ، فـ «أيُّ» ههنا استفهاميَّةٌ ، وهي ومجرورُهَا مُتعلّقان بيحتاجونَ قُدِّما للصَّدارةِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَذَلِكَ) أي : أنَّ النَّاسَ ... إلخ، وقوله : (بيان)؛ أي: تبيين الحاجة المحتاج فيه إلى المنطق والمحتاج فيه إلى المنطق هو العصمة، ومحصّله أنَّ العصمة تقع جواباً عن سؤالِ السَّائلِ عنِ الاحتياج لهُ .
We needed a law that safeguards against error, beneficial for the methods of acquiring theoretical [knowledge] from necessary [knowledge], and that law is logic. Therefore, it is possible that they did not believe what their expressions imply, so there is no error in their thoughts, even if that possibility is remote. Contrary to when a rational thinker reflects on his states and investigates them; he finds that he believes contradictory things at different times, and does not doubt that, such as when he thinks at one time and his thinking leads him to assent to the temporality of the world, then thinks at another time; his thinking leads him to assent to the pre-eternity of the world. Then one of the two thoughts is not correct due to what preceded, so thought is not always correct. (His statement: 'we needed a law... etc.') This is the meaning of the author's subsequent statement: 'it was needed... etc.' He only brought it here for the sake of his statement: 'and the conclusion... etc.' He only brought this conclusion to indicate the interconnection of the text's words. (His statement: 'to a law') i.e., possessing a law, or what is meant is the science itself. (His statement: 'beneficial for methods') They are proofs and their conditions, and the explanatory statement and its conditions; i.e., the methods of theoretical assents and concepts. (His statement: 'from necessary') is related to 'acquiring', meaning attaining, and his statement 'necessary' i.e., even with respect to their instruments, and then it is true to acquire the theoretical from another theoretical, and the other theoretical from a third theoretical, and so on until it ends at a necessary [premise], so ending at the necessary is inevitable to prevent circularity or infinite regress. For concepts and assents, and al-Razi in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya restricted it to the case of assents; where he said: 'so from one whose thinking leads to assent to the temporality of the world... etc.', and they excused him that he did not address concepts due to the lack of appearance of error in them, for every concept of the meanings has no contradiction nor repugnance between them, only repugnance occurs between the implicit judgments necessary for them, and similarly acquisition in them is not apparent based on the imam's doubt claiming all of them are necessary, and what our commentator did is more beneficial and better. So it is known from this that people need logic in something, and that is the statement of the need. (His statement: 'from this') i.e., from this exposition, which is his statement: 'because thought is not always correct, so it was needed... etc.' Thus some decided, but what is more in accordance with the commentator's previous statement: 'And when the statement of need was inclined to the definition of logic... etc.', is to say: 'it is known from this'; i.e., the sum of the author's statement: 'knowledge if it is assent to the relation...' up to his statement: 'and error may occur in it', with the commentator's statement: 'we needed a law... etc.' (His statement: 'that people') i.e., the answer of 'that'... etc., and it is the infallibility of the mind from error in thought; i.e., from what preceded the answer to this question is known, formulated by our saying: 'In what thing do people need logic?' And its answer: they need it for the mentioned infallibility, and his statement: 'in what thing' is related to 'they need', and he gave it precedence because 'what?' is interrogative, so it has precedence. (His statement: 'and that') i.e., what the answer is known from, which is the author's statement: 'knowledge if it is assent to the relation...' up to the end of his statement: 'and error may occur in it' with the commentator's statement, 'so we needed... etc.' (His statement: 'and that is the statement of the need') i.e., mentioning that is an explanation, or: and that possesses an explanation of the mentioned infallibility; i.e., the affirmation that it is the goal of this science and its benefit. For the need, and it is (His statement: 'that people... etc.') The source derived from 'that' with its clause from the substance of its predicate, which is 'they need', and it is the deputy subject of 'it was known' with the elision of an annex, and the estimation is: 'the answer of the need of people was known'; i.e., the answer to the question about that. So if it is said: 'In what thing are people said to need logic?' It is said in answer: for the infallibility of thought from error. So 'what?' here is interrogative, and it and its genitive are related to 'they need', given precedence for primacy. (His statement: 'and that') i.e., 'that people... etc.', and his statement: 'statement'; i.e., explaining the need that is needed in logic, and what is needed in it is infallibility, and its conclusion is that infallibility occurs as an answer to the questioner's question about the need for it.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage explains the fundamental need for logic. The main text (matn) states that humans need a law that protects them from error when acquiring theoretical knowledge from necessary knowledge, and that law is logic. The commentator then expands by arguing that if humans never erred, they would not need logic, but in fact they do err, as shown by the example of someone who at one time assents to the temporality of the world and later assents to its pre-eternity. Since both cannot be true, thought is not always correct. The commentator clarifies the phrases: 'law' refers to logic itself, 'methods' are proofs and their conditions as well as explanatory statements and their conditions, i.e., the methods for theoretical assents and concepts. The acquisition must eventually reach necessary premises to avoid circularity or infinite regress. A side discussion notes that al-Razi restricted the need to assents, not concepts, because error is not apparent in concepts alone. The commentator, however, considers the broader view more beneficial. The passage concludes that the purpose of logic is to safeguard the mind (ismat al-dhihn) from error, answering the question: 'In what do people need logic?' The answer is: for this protection.

Key Terms
قانون
فكر
ضروري
نظري
عصمة

Checkpoint

D) To define concepts clearly

Key Takeaway
Logic is the law that protects the mind from error, ensuring that our reasoning leads to truth and avoids contradiction.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
The Division of Knowledge and the Necessity of Logic
Division of knowledge and need for logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the division of knowledge into necessary and acquired and explain why the possibility of error necessitates a logical canon.

Core Idea
Knowledge is fallible; logic is the canon that safeguards it from error.

This passage establishes a foundational premise of Islamic logic: knowledge is not monolithic. It is divided into necessary (self-evident) and acquired (derived through reasoning). But since human reasoning is fallible, acquired knowledge can contain errors. This is why a canon—logic—is indispensable for distinguishing truth from falsehood. Let's unpack why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division of knowledge
Find where the passage divides knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). Understand that these are the two fundamental types of mental content.
2
Subdivide each into necessary and acquired
Notice that both tasawwur and tasdiq are further divided into daruri (necessary) and kasbi (acquired). Necessary knowledge is self-evident, while acquired knowledge is gained through reasoning.
3
Understand the process of acquisition (iktisab)
The passage states that acquired knowledge is derived from necessary knowledge via iktisab. This process involves qawl sharh for concepts and qiyas for assents.
4
Recognize the possibility of error
The passage explicitly warns: 'Error may occur in acquisition because thinking is not always correct.' This is the key insight—human reasoning is not infallible.
5
Conclude the need for a canon
Because of the risk of error, a set of rules (qanun) is needed to guide reasoning and ensure correctness. This canon is the subject of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المستلزم لتعريف العِلمِ برَسْمِه ; إذ يُعلَمُ مِن بيان الحاجة غايةُ العِلْم، والتَّعريفُ بالغايةِ رَسُمٌ ; فَإِذا أَدْرَجَ المصنِّفُ التَّعريف في بيانِ الحاجة كما سيجيءُ . والحاصل : إنَّ العِلم : إمَّا تصوُّرٌ ساذج، أو تصديق. وكلُّ واحدٍ منَ التَّصور والتّصديقِ ينقسمُ بحَسَبِ الضَّرورة إلى : الضَّروريّ، والكسبي . والكسبيُّ مُستفاد من الضَّروري بطريق الاكتساب . وقد يقع في الاكتساب الخطأ؛ لأنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً ; فَاحْتِيجَ إِلَى قَانُونٍ
What necessitates defining knowledge by its description; because from the explanation of the need, the goal of knowledge is known, and definition by the goal is a description. So when the author includes the definition in the explanation of the need, as will come. The summary: knowledge is either a simple concept or an assent. Each of concept and assent is divided by necessity into the necessary and the acquired. The acquired is derived from the necessary by the method of acquisition. Error may occur in acquisition because thinking is not always correct. So the need arose for a canon.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the need for logic. The sharih begins by noting that the author's mention of 'need' (haja) implies a definition of knowledge by its goal (ghayah), which is a descriptive definition (rasm). He then presents a summary: knowledge is either tasawwur (concept) or tasdiq (assent). Each of these is either daruri (necessary, self-evident) or kasbi (acquired through reasoning). The acquired is derived from the necessary through a process called iktisab, which involves qawl sharh for concepts and qiyas for assents. However, this acquired knowledge is fallible because human thinking is not always correct. Therefore, a canon (qanun) is needed to guide reasoning and avoid error. This canon is the subject of logic.

Key Terms
التصور (al-tasawwur): Simple mental representation without judgment, such as the concept of 'human'.
التصديق (al-tasdiq): Judgment or assent, such as affirming that 'the world is created'.
الضروري (al-daruri): Knowledge that is self-evident and does not require reasoning, like the impossibility of a thing being both existent and non-existent.
الكسبي (al-kasbi): Knowledge acquired through reasoning or contemplation, as opposed to innate.
الاكتساب (al-iktisab): The process of reasoning that leads to acquired knowledge; for concepts it is al-qawl al-sharh, for assents it is al-qiyas.
القانون (al-qanun): The canon or set of rules of logic that governs correct reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do we need a canon (qanun) in logic according to the passage?
A) Because the definition of knowledge requires it
B) Because acquired knowledge (al-kasbi) can contain errors
C) Because all knowledge is necessary
D) Because the canon was invented by Greeks

Key Takeaway
Because acquired knowledge is fallible, a logical canon is necessary to ensure correct reasoning and prevent error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a novice jurist who attempts to derive a ruling on the permissibility of cryptocurrency using qiyas. He starts from necessary knowledge (e.g., 'every intoxicant is forbidden') and attempts to analogize. Explain, using the divisions in this lesson, why his reasoning might contain error and why a canon (usul al-fiqh) is essential.

Why Logic is Called Qanun
Dusuqi defines qanun and explains its application to logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the art of logic is called 'qanun' despite comprising multiple rules.

Core Idea
Logic is called qanun because its many rules act as one canon.

In this passage, al-Dusuqi unpacks the term 'qanun' (canon) as it applies to logic. He traces its linguistic origin, defines it as a universal rule, and then explains the conceptual leap from many rules to one canon. Understanding this helps you grasp the unifying purpose of logic in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of qanun
First, qanun as a single universal rule (qa'ida). Second, qanun as the collection of rules of the art of logic.
2
Analyse the part-whole naming
The whole (totality of rules) is named after one of its parts (the term qanun). Why? Because the collection functions as a single entity.
3
Find the unifying aspect
The rules are multiple but united in one aspect (jihah wahidah). What is that aspect? The passage cuts off, but from context it is the aim of guiding thought correctly.
This is a common logical move: treating a collection as one for a specific purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون : لفظ يوناني معناه في الأصل: القاعدة، وهي قضيَّةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ يُتعرَّفُ منها أحكام جزئيات موضوعِهَا . والمراد بالقانون هنا : مجموع قواعدِ هذا الفن، وتسمية هذا المجموع قانوناً من باب تسمية الشَّيء باسم بعض أجزائه، وإنَّما قِيلَ لهذا الفنّ قانون معَ أَنَّهُ قوانين مُتعدِّدَة؛ لِكَونِهَا كَالواحدِ من حيثُ إِنَّها مُشتركةٌ في جهةٍ واحدةٍ تجمعها، وهي كونُها
Al-qanun: a Greek word originally meaning 'rule', which is a universal proposition from which the judgments of the particulars of its subject are known. What is meant by 'qanun' here is the totality of the rules of this art. Naming this totality 'qanun' is from the category of naming a thing after one of its parts. It is called 'qanun' for this art despite being multiple rules because they are like one due to being united in a single aspect that gathers them, which is being...
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is a definitional passage from the glossator Dusuqi. He explains the term 'qanun' (canon). He notes its Greek origin, meaning 'rule' (qa'ida). A rule is a universal proposition used to derive judgments about the particulars of its subject. In logic, 'qanun' refers to the entire set of rules governing reasoning. He justifies calling the collection of rules a 'qanun' by analogy: the whole is named after a part. Despite being multiple rules, they are considered one because they share a common purpose. The passage cuts off, but the intended meaning is that the rules of logic are unified under the single aim of guiding thought correctly.

Key Terms
القانون
القاعدة
الجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because they are united in a common purpose
C) Because the word is Greek
D) Because it defines logic

Key Takeaway
The term 'qanun' in logic refers not to a single rule but to the unified set of rules that guide reasoning.

What Is Mantiq?

Definition of Logic as a Protecting Law
Author defines logic and addresses an objection about its necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of the term 'mantiq' and explain how the definition of logic as a protecting law addresses the objection that logic is unnecessary.

Core Idea
Logic is a universal law protecting the mind, rooted in inner and outer speech.

This passage introduces the foundational definition of logic as a law that safeguards the mind from error. It also anticipates a common objection: if error can be avoided without a law, why need logic? The response reveals the necessity of a universal guide. By unpacking this definition and the ensuing debate, you will grasp the purpose and scope of logic in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition of logic
Find the phrase that directly defines logic: 'a universal law whose observance protects the mind from error in thought'. Note the genus (law) and the differentia (protects from error).
This definition is from the author's matn; al-Dasuqi clarifies it.
2
Analyze the three meanings of 'mantiq'
Al-Dasuqi explains why the law is called 'mantiq': it relates to universal perceptions (inner speech), their verbal expression (outer speech), and the rational faculty. Identify each component in the text.
This etymological analysis shows how logic connects to both thought and language.
3
Understand the objection to logic's necessity
The objection states that one can avoid error without a universal law. Al-Dasuqi refutes it by pointing out that error is not specific ('ghayr mu'ayyan'), so a universal law is needed. Find the Arabic phrase for 'not specific'.
This logical move parallels arguments for the necessity of usul al-fiqh.
4
Examine the supplementary role of fitra
Al-Attar cites the commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulum, arguing that ultimate protection comes from fitra, not logic. However, logic provides 'weak support' after fitra distinguishes truth from falsehood. Identify the Arabic for 'weak support'.
This nuance clarifies that logic is not the primary protector but a useful tool.
5
Synthesize the definition and its defense
Combine the definition with the response to the objection to form a complete picture: logic is necessary because potential errors are not specific, and it is a universal law that strengthens the rational faculty, even if final discernment comes from innate disposition.
This synthesis shows how the author defends logic without overclaiming its role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَعْصِمُ عَنْهُ؛ وَهُوَ المُنْطِقُ).
الدسوقي
تعصمُ الذّهن عن الخطأ في الفكر، لا يُقالُ: يمكنُ التَّباعدُ عَن الخطأ في الفكر، وحينئذ؛ فَلَا يحتاج للقانون المذكور؛ لأنَّا نقولُ: إِنَّ ذلك الخطأ غير معيَّن حتَّى يتباعد عنه، وحينئذ؛ فيحتاجُ للقانون المذكور.
(قَوْلُهُ: يَعْصِمُ عَنْهُ) أي: يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ) أي: القانونُ الَّذي يعصم عن الخطأ؛ (الْمَنْطِقُ)، وإِنَّما سُمِّيَ ذلكَ القانونُ بالمنطق؛ لأنَّهُ يُطلقُ فى الأصل على الإدراكاتِ الكُلَّيَّة، وهي نطق باطني. وعلى التلفظ بدال متعلّق تلك الإدراكات، وهو نطق ظاهري، وعلى القوَّةِ العاقلة التي هي محلُّ صدور تلك الإدراكات.
والقانون المذكورُ به تصيب الإدراكات الكُلّيَّة، وبه تكون القدرة على التلفظ بدالّ مُتعلّقِ الإدراكات الكُلَّيَّة، وَبِهِ تتقوّى القوَّةُ العاقلة وتكمل قوله: (وهو المنطق) وحينَئِذٍ؛ فَقولُهُ: تعريف؛ أي: دال تعريف المنطق، فهو قانون كلّي تعصم مراعاتُه الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر، وقولُه في بيان الحاجة؛ أي: في تبيين ما يفيد التصديق بالحاجة.
العطار
وقوع الخطأ فيه من صاحبها، فلا حاجة إلى إثباتِ عدمِهِ، وأمَّا الأنظار الجزئيَّةُ؛ فإِنَّهُ يتعذَّرُ ضبطها؛ لتكثُرِها بتكثرِ الأزمانِ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِن أمرٍ كُلِّي ينطلق عليها، قالَ شَارِحُ سُلَّمِ العُلومِ: إِنَّ الأعَاظِمَ الماهرين في المنطِقِ رَبَّما يُخطئون خطاً لا يكادُونَ ينتبهونَ لهُ، ولا يُجديهمُ المنطقُ نفعاً، كيفَ والمنطِقُ قد حكم مثلاً بانتهاء مُقدِّمَاتِ البرهان إلى الضَّروريَّاتِ؟ وربَّما يلتبس الوهمي الكاذبُ بالضَّروريّ؛ فلا يحصل التَّمييز بينَهُما باستعمال المنطق، وبعد تمييز العقل بين الكاذِبِ الوهمي والضَّروريّ؛ لا يحتاج كثيراً إلى المنطق، فإذن؛ العاصم: ما به يحصلُ التَّمييرُ ما بين الكاذب والضَّروريّ، وهو الفطرة الإنسانيَّةُ المجرَّدةُ عَن شائبة مُخالطة الوهم، وللمنطقِ إمداد ضعيفٌ بعد هذا التمييز، فإليه حاجةٌ ضَعيفةٌ .
He protects from it; and it is Mantiq. Al-Dasuqi: It protects the mind from error in thought. It is not said: 'It is possible to avoid error in thought, and then there is no need for the mentioned law' because we say: That error is not specific so that one could avoid it, and then he needs the mentioned law. His statement: 'He protects from it' means: He protects the mind from error. His statement: 'And it is' i.e. the law that protects from error; (is Mantiq). It is only called that law Mantiq because it is originally applied to universal perceptions, which are inner speech, and to the utterance of the indicator related to those perceptions, which is outer speech, and to the rational faculty that is the place of issuance of those perceptions. And the mentioned law perfects universal perceptions, and by it one has the ability to utter the indicator related to universal perceptions, and by it the rational faculty is strengthened and completed. His statement: 'And it is Mantiq'. Then his statement: 'definition' i.e. the indicator of the definition of Mantiq, so it is a universal law whose observance protects the mind from error in thought, and his statement in 'Bayān al-Ḥājah' i.e. in clarifying what affirms the need. Al-Attar: The occurrence of error in it from its possessor, so there is no need to prove its absence. As for particular thoughts, it is impossible to control them due to their multiplicity over time, so a universal matter is necessary to apply to them. The commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulum said: 'Indeed, the great masters in logic sometimes make an error that they hardly notice, and logic does not benefit them. How, when logic has ruled, for example, that the premises of demonstration end in necessary truths? And sometimes the false imaginary is confused with the necessary; then distinction between them is not obtained by using logic. And after the mind distinguishes between the false imaginary and the necessary, it does not need much logic. So then, the protector is what causes distinction between the false and the necessary, and it is the human innate disposition (fitra) stripped of any admixture of imagination, and for logic there is a weak support after this distinction, so there is a weak need for it.'
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage defines logic (mantiq) as a universal law that protects the mind from error in thought, and then addresses an objection that questions the necessity of such a law. Al-Dasuqi begins by explaining that the objection that one can avoid error without a law is invalid because error is not specific enough to be avoided without a universal guide. He then clarifies the definition: logic is a law that protects the mind, and it is called 'mantiq' because the term originally refers to three things: universal perceptions (inner speech), the utterance indicating those perceptions (outer speech), and the rational faculty from which these perceptions emanate. The law of logic perfects universal perceptions, enables verbal expression, and strengthens the rational faculty. Al-Dasuqi further explains that the author's statement 'definition' refers to the indicator of the definition of logic, which is that it is a universal law whose observance protects from error, and his 'explanation of the need' clarifies that this definition itself demonstrates the need for logic. Al-Attar then adds a deeper discussion: one might think that since the protector from error is the innate human disposition (fitra), logic is unnecessary, but he responds that while the ultimate distinction between truth and falsehood comes from fitra, logic provides a weak support after that distinction, so there remains a weak need for logic. He cites the commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulum arguing that even experts sometimes err in applying logic, but this does not eliminate its utility; rather, it shows that logic is not the primary protector but an auxiliary tool.

المنطق
القانون
عصمة
إدراكات كلية
نطق
فطرة

B) Because the error in thought is not specific, so a universal law is needed to avoid it.

D) Because the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the definition.

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that logic is a universal law protecting the mind from error, and its necessity arises from the non-specific nature of potential errors, even though ultimate discernment may come from innate disposition.
