Logic as a Universal Law: Why the Rules of Reasoning Form a Single System
Author defines logic as a law and explains its universality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is called a law (qanun) by analyzing the universality and unity of its rules, using the commentary of Dassuqi and 'Attar.

Core Idea
Logic is called a law because its many rules share a single unifying purpose.

In this passage, the commentators unpack the definition of logic as a law (qanun). They explain that the term 'law' is not arbitrary: logic's issues (masa'il) are universal rules applicable to any reasoning. The multiplicity of these rules is unified by a common objective—protecting the intellect from error. This lesson will clarify the conceptual foundation of logic as a universal tool, not a specific science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition being expanded
The passage begins: 'This is the definition of logic included in the explanation of the need.' Recognize that the author is referencing the well-known definition: 'Logic is a law that protects the intellect from error.'
2
Extract the justification for calling logic a law
The commentator says: 'Logic is only a law because its issues are universal laws.' Note the reasoning: the content of logic consists of universal rules (qawanin kulliyya), hence it is called a law.
3
Understand the synecdochical naming
'Attar explains that naming logic as 'law' is naming the whole by the part: the many rules are like parts that constitute a single law due to their shared aspect.
4
Clarify the applicability to particulars
Dassuqi clarifies that the rules apply potentially, not actually, to particular cases. The law itself deals with universal judgments; the application to particulars is indirect.

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: مُنْطَبِقَةٌ) أي : مُشتملة اشتمالاً بالقوَّةِ القريبة من الفعل؛ لا اشتمالاً بالفعل؛ لأنَّ الحاصل بالفعل الحكم المتعلّق بالأمرِ الكُلِّي الذي هو موضوع القانون؛ لا الأحكام المتعلّقة بجزئياتِ الموضوع . (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى جُزْئِيَّاتٍ) أي : على جزئيات موضوعها؛ لأنَّ القانونَ نَفْسَهُ لا جزئيات له؛ لأنَّ الجزئيات أفراد المفهومِ الكُلّيّ، وفي الكلام حذفٌ مُضاف; أي: على أحكام الجزئيات.
Introduction by the author: This is the definition of logic included in the explanation of the need. And logic is only a law because its issues are universal laws. Dassuqi: His statement 'included' is an adjective for definition. His statement 'in the explanation of the need' means by his statement: 'Knowledge, if it is assent...' up to his statement: 'and it may occur...' etc.; and because of its inclusion, the author did not bring it independently, but sufficed with its inclusion in the explanation of the need. His statement: 'because its issues' meaning its propositions and the addition is of the parts to the whole. His statement: 'laws' meaning rules, and his statement: 'universal' is a clarifying description, and this description is by virtue of the universality of their subject. 'Attar: His statement: 'This is the definition of logic' refers to his statement: 'A law that protects...' as evidenced by the commentator's statement: 'And logic is only a law...' etc., and making the referred to his statement: 'And it is logic' as was said is evidently corrupt, and what was strained in its interpretation is speech that ears reject. His statement: 'because its issues are universal laws' i.e., the naming of logic as a law is by way of naming the whole by the part. And since those laws, despite their multiplicity, share a single aspect that controls them and makes them like one thing, they were made one law, because for every science there are many issues that have a single aspect specific to it, by which it is considered one science. 'Applicable to particulars.' Dassuqi: His statement: 'applicable' meaning containing inclusion in potentiality close to actuality, not actual inclusion; because what is actually attained is the judgment related to the universal matter which is the subject of the law, not the judgments related to the particulars of the subject. His statement: 'to particulars' meaning to the particulars of its subject; because the law itself has no particulars; for particulars are individuals of the universal concept, and in the expression there is an omitted annexed noun; i.e., upon the judgments of the particulars.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the definition of logic as a law (qanun). The matn (core text) states: 'This is the definition of logic included in the explanation of the need.' The commentator then explains why logic is called a law: because its issues (masa'il) are universal laws (qawanin kulliyya). Dassuqi clarifies that 'issues' refers to propositions (qadaya) and that the phrase 'universal laws' is a clarifying description, emphasizing that the universality pertains to the subject matter of logic. 'Attar further elaborates: the definition of logic referred to in the matn is the well-known definition 'a law that protects the intellect from error in thinking.' He argues that the naming of logic as a 'law' is synecdochical (naming the whole by the part), because the rules of logic are many but they share a single unifying aspect—namely, they are all rules for correct thinking. This unity makes them a single law, just as any science has many issues unified by a single subject. The passage then addresses the phrase 'applicable to particulars.' Dassuqi explains that the applicability is potential, not actual; the actual judgments are about universal matters, not individual particulars. The law itself has no particulars; rather, 'particulars' refers to the particulars of the law's subject, with an ellipsis: the judgments of the particulars are meant. This commentary ensures precision: logic is a universal tool, its rules are potential guides for any particular reasoning.

Key Terms
قانون (qanun): A universal rule or set of rules that governs a field; here, logic as a safeguard for correct thinking. [Note: In earlier texts, qanun often refers to a canon or standard; here it is synonymous with 'law' as an instrument of reasoning.]
مسائل (masa'il): The propositions or issues that constitute the content of a science; in logic, the universal rules themselves. [Note: In later texts, masa'il can refer to specific problems or questions; here it is synonymous with qadaya (propositions).]
قوانين كلية (qawanin kulliyya): Universal laws; the rules of logic that apply to all subject matters, not just a specific domain. [Note: This term is stable across the tradition, though the classification of these rules may vary.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because its rules are only about particulars.

D) Because it is a science like fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Logic is a universal law because its rules are universal in scope and unified by a single purpose—protecting the intellect—making them applicable across all sciences.

Defining a Science: Hadd, Rasm, and the Role of Subject Matter
Author explains why a science is considered one based on its subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition) of a science, and explain why subject matter is the primary basis for unifying and differentiating sciences.

Core Idea
A science is one by its subject, not by its definitions or ends.

How do we know that a field like usul al-fiqh is a single science despite containing many different rules and issues? 'Attar explains that a science gains its unity from its subject matter—the overarching topic that all its issues investigate. This also determines how we define the science: either by capturing its essence (hadd) or by describing its utility (rasm). Understanding this distinction helps us see that the essential identity of a science lies in what it studies, not in how it is defined or used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim about unity
The author asserts that a science is considered one because its many issues share a common subject. Find the Arabic phrase that expresses this—'الاتحاد في جهة الموضوع'.
Unity in the aspect of subject.
2
Distinguish between hadd and rasm definitions
The author gives two ways to define a science: hadd if the definition reveals the essence (e.g., 'a science in which such-and-such is investigated'), and rasm if it only mentions utility (e.g., 'a science by which one is empowered to do such-and-such'). Note the criteria for each.
Hadd: reality, rasm: description.
3
Apply the subject-matter principle to differentiate sciences
The text says sciences are essentially differentiated only by their subject. Consider two sciences like nahw (grammar) and fiqh (jurisprudence): they differ because their subjects (words vs. actions) differ in essence.
4
Connect to the universal-applicable phrase
The passage returns to the earlier phrase 'كُلِّيَّةٌ مُنْطَبِقَةٌ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّاتٍ' and clarifies that the particulars are those on which the truth of an affirmative categorical proposition depends. This shows that the applicability of universal statements is linked to subject matter.
The text cuts off, but the link is clear.

العطار
بالتَّدوين والتسمية والتَّعليم؛ نظراً إلى ما لِتلكَ الطَّائفة على كثرتها واختلافِ محمولاتِها مِنَ الاتحادِ في جهة الموضوع؛ أي: الاشتراك فيه على الوجه المذكور ، ثمَّ  َقدْ تَتَّحِدُ مِن جِهَاتٍ أُخَر كالمنفعة والغاية ونحوهما، ويؤخذُ لها مِن بعض تِلكَ الجهاتِ مَا يفيدُ تصورها من حيثُ الإجمال، ومن حيثُ إِنَّ لها وحدة، فيكونُ حدًّا للعلم إنْ دلَّ على حقيقةٍ مُسمَّاه ، أعني ذلك المركَّبَ الاعتباري كما يُقالُ: هو عِلْمٌ يُبَحثُ فيه عن كذا ، أو عِلْمٌ بقواعدِ كذا، وإلا ، فَرسماً كما يُقَالُ :
أَنَّ

(قَوْلُهُ: كُلِّيَّةٌ مُنْطَبِقَةٌ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّاتٍ) الوصفَانِ كاشِفَانِ لدخولهما في مفهوم الموصوف ؛ وهوَ الكُلِّيَّةُ، والمراد بالجزئيات جزئيات لَهَا زيادة تعلُّق بتلك القضيَّةِ بِأَنَّ يتوقَّفَ صِدقُها على وجودِهَا ، وهي جزئيَّاتُ موضوع الموجبة الحمليّة؛ ضرورةً أنَّ صدقَ السَّالبةِ لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِ موضوعِهَا ، وصدقَ الشَّرطيَّةِ لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِ
'Attar: By codification, naming, and teaching, considering what that group, despite its multiplicity and the difference of their predicates, has of unity in the aspect of subject: i.e., sharing in it in the manner mentioned. Then it may be united from other aspects such as benefit and end and the like, and for it is taken from some of those aspects what benefits its conceptualization in terms of summary and in terms that it has unity; so it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named, i.e., that composite mental construct, just as it is said: It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated, or a science of such-and-such rules; otherwise, it is a description (rasm), as it is said: It is a science by which one is empowered to do such-and-such, or by which one is guarded from such-and-such, or it is an instrument for such-and-such. So it became clear that the subject is the aspect of the unity of the issues of a single science in terms of its essence; even if it has other aspects like definition and end, and that there is no meaning to this being a science and that being another science except that it investigates the states of something else different from it in essence or consideration. So the differentiation of sciences in themselves and with regard to their essences is only by the subject, even if they are differentiated for the learner by their definitions and ends and the like. This is a summary discussion of the aspect of unity of sciences; its detail is in the extended books, and it has been compiled separately. His statement: 'universal applicable to particulars' – the two descriptions are revealing of their inclusion in the concept of the described, which is universality. And by particulars is meant particulars that have additional relation to that proposition, such that its truth depends on their existence. And they are particulars of the subject of the affirmative categorical proposition, necessarily because the truth of the negative does not depend on the existence of its subject, and the truth of the conditional does not depend on the existence...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and classificatory explanation of how sciences are defined and distinguished. 'Attar discusses the unity of sciences: although a science comprises many issues with different predicates, they are unified by sharing a common subject matter. This unity can also be seen from other perspectives like benefit or purpose, but the essential unity is by subject. Based on this, a science can be defined in two ways: if the definition captures its essence (hadd), it describes what it investigates (e.g., 'a science in which such-and-such is investigated'); if not, it is a description (rasm) that mentions its utility or protective role. Thus, sciences are essentially distinguished only by their subject, not by accidental features like definitions or goals. The passage then returns to the earlier phrase 'universal applicable to particulars', clarifying that the two descriptions ('universal' and 'applicable') are explicative of the concept of universality. The 'particulars' are those that are related to the truth of the proposition: specifically, for an affirmative categorical proposition, its truth depends on the existence of its subject particulars; but this is not the case for negative or conditional propositions. The passage ends incomplete, but the point is made that the applicability of universal rules is conditioned by the nature of the propositions.

رسم
كُلِّيَّةٌ مُنْطَبِقَةٌ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّاتٍ

Key Takeaway
A science is essentially defined by its subject matter; definitions (hadd) capture that essence, while descriptions (rasm) only provide utility.

Definition of a Logical Law (Qanun) as a Universal Proposition
Commentators define a logical law as a universal affirmative categorical proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given universal proposition qualifies as a 'law' (qanun) in logic by testing its truth-dependence on particulars and distinguishing affirmative categoricals from negatives and conditionals.

Core Idea
A law is only an affirmative categorical universal whose truth depends on its particulars.

In Islamic logic, a 'law' (qanun) is not any universal proposition—it is specifically an affirmative categorical universal whose truth rests on the existence of its subject's particulars. The commentary unpacks this definition, explaining why negatives and conditionals are excluded, and addresses an objection by considering two interpretations of what 'applying to particulars' means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'law' (qanun). It is defined as a universal proposition applying to particulars (qadiyya kulliyya munṭabiqa ʿalā juzʾiyyāt).
The definition appears in the main text and is glossed by the commentators.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'universal proposition'. The differentia is 'applying to particulars such that its truth depends on the existence of those particulars'. This differentia is key because it restricts the definition.
The commentary clarifies that 'applying' means potential inclusion, not actual.
3
Test which proposition types satisfy the differentia
Check whether a proposition's truth depends on the existence of its subject's particulars. Only affirmative categoricals satisfy this; negative categoricals are true even if the subject has no existent particulars, and conditionals do not require the existence of the subject of their parts.
This is why the commentary explicitly excludes negatives and conditionals from the definition.
4
Handle the objection and reframe the meaning of 'particulars'
Some scholars object that negatives can also be laws. The reply distinguishes two interpretations of 'applying' (inṭibāq): (1) predication (ḥaml) – then particulars are individuals of the subject concept; (2) inclusion (ishtimāl) – then particulars are branches subsumed under the rule. Under the first interpretation, negatives fail; under the second, they may still be considered laws if they potentially include branches.
This shows the flexibility of the logical analysis and resolves the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُنْطَبِقَةٌ على جزئياتٍ . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: مُنْطَبقَةٌ) أي : مُشتملة اشتمالاً بالقوَّةِ القريبة من الفعل؛ لا اشتمالاً بالفعل؛ لأنَّ الحاصل بالفعل الحكم المتعلّق بالأمرِ الكُلِّي الذي هو موضوع القانون؛ لا الأحكام المتعلّقة بجزئياتِ الموضوع . (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى جُزْئِيَّاتٍ) أي : على جزئيات موضوعها؛ لأنَّ القانونَ نَفْسَهُ لا جزئيات له؛ لأنَّ الجزئيات أفراد المفهومِ الكُلّيّ، وفي الكلام حذفٌ مُضاف؛ أي: على أحكام الجزئيات. العطار بالتَّدوين والتسمية والتَّعليم؛ نظراً إلى ما لِتلكَ الطَّائفة على كثرتها واختلافِ محمولاتِها مِنَ الاتحادِ في جهة الموضوع؛ أي: الاشتراك فيه على الوجه المذكور ، ثمَّ  َقدْ تَتَّحِدُ مِن جِهَاتٍ أُخَر كالمنفعة والغاية ونحوهما، ويؤخذُ لها مِن بعض تِلكَ الجهاتِ مَا يفيدُ تصورها من حيثُ الإجمال، ومن حيثُ إِنَّ لها وحدة، فيكونُ حدًّا للعلم إنْ دلَّ على حقيقةٍ مُسمَّاه ، أعني ذلك المركَّبَ الاعتباري كما يُقالُ: هو عِلْمٌ يُبَحثُ فيه عن كذا ، أو عِلْمٌ بقواعدِ كذا، وإلا ، فَرسماً كما يُقَالُ : أَنَّ هو عِلْمٌ يُقتَدرُ بِهِ على كذا، أو يحتَرَزُ بِهِ عَن كذا، أو يكونُ آلةٌ لِكَذا، فظهر الموضوع هو جهةً وحدةِ مسائلِ العِلْم الواحِدِ نظراً إلى ذاتها ؛ وإن عرضت لها جِهَاتٌ أَخَر كالتَّعريف والغاية، وأنَّهُ لا معنى لِكَونِ هذا عِلْماً وذاكَ عِلْماً آخَرَ؛ سِوَى أَنَّهُ يبحثُ عَن أحوالِ شَيْءٍ آخَرَ مُغَايرِ لَهُ بالذَّاتِ أو الاعتبار، فلا يكونُ تَمايز العُلومِ في أَنْفُسِهَا وبالنَّظَرِ إلى ذواتها إلا بِحَسب الموضوع، وإنْ كانت تتمايز عندَ الطَّالِبِ بِمَا لَهَا مِنَ التَّعريفات والغايات ونحوهما ، هذا حديث إجمالي في جهة وحدةِ العِلْمِ؛ تفصيلُه في الكتب المبسوطة، وقد أفرد بالتدوين. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلِّيَّةٌ مُنْطَبِقَةٌ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّاتٍ) الوصفَانِ كاشِفَانِ لدخولهما في مفهوم الموصوف ؛ وهوَ الكُلِّيَّةُ، والمراد بالجزئيات جزئيات لَهَا زيادة تعلُّق بتلك القضيَّةِ بِأَنَّ يتوقَّفَ صِدقُها على وجودِهَا ، وهي جزئيَّاتُ موضوع الموجبة الحمليّة؛ ضرورةً أنَّ صدقَ السَّالبةِ لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِ موضوعِهَا ، وصدقَ الشَّرطِيَّةِ لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِ موضوع طرفيها ، فَخَرَجَتِ السَّالبةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ مِن تعريف القانونِ كالشَّرِطَيَّةِ؛ فَوَافَقَ مَا تَقَرَّرَ عندَهُم أنَّ أجزاء الفنّ قضايا حمليّات موجبات كُلِّيَّات، وإنْ قال عبد الحكيم : إِنَّ السَّالبة مِنَ القوانين، وعَلَّلَ ذلكَ بأنَّ استنباط الفروع كما يكونُ مِنَ الموجبات؛ يكونُ مِنَ السَّوالِبِ، ثمَّ إِنْ فُسِّر الانطباق بالحمل ؛ فالمراد بالجزئيات : أفراد ذلكَ المفهوم الكُلِّيّ الَّذي هو موضوعُ القانون، مثلاً إذا قِيلَ : كُلُّ فاعل مرفوعٌ ؛ مَفهوم فاعل وهو مَن قامَ بِهِ الحدثُ أمرُ كُلّيّ، وله أفراد واقعةٌ في التّراكيب؛ كَقَامَ زِيدٌ وسَافَرَ عمرو ... إلخ، ولا خفاء في صحة حملِ ذلك المفهوم الكُلِّي على تلك الأفرادِ، فَإِنَّكَ تقولُ : زيدٌ مَنْ قامَ ؛ زيدٌ فاعل، وعمرُو مَن سافر؛ عمرو فاعل ... إلخ، وإِنْ فُسِّرَ بالاشتمال ؛ فالمراد بالجزئيات فروع تلك القاعدة تشبيهاً لها بالجزئيات في الاندراج ؛ على خلافِ ما هو شائع مِن إطلاق الجزئيَّاتِ على أفرادِ الكُلّيّ، والمراد باشتمال القاعدة على تلك الفروع ؛ وجودُهَا فيها بالقوَّةِ لا بالفعل، فإنَّ الحاصل بالفعل ليسَ إلا حكم واحدٌ، وهو الحاصِلُ في حمل محمول القضيَّةِ على موضوعِهَا، ثمَّ . الشَّارِحَ حذف قيدَ التَّعرُّفِ لِظهورِهِ ولِدَلالةِ قولِهِ : كما إذا ... إلخ، قال بعضُ الفُضَلاءِ وفي صيغةِ التَّفَقُلِ ؛ أي: قولُهُم يتعرَّفُ ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ تلك المعرفة بالكلفة والمشقة، فخرجَ مِنَ التَّعريف القضيَّةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ الَّتي تكون فروعُهَا بديهيَّةً غيرَ مُحتاجةٍ إلى التَّخريج ؛ كَقولنا : الشَّكلُ الأَوَّلُ مُنتج، فيكونُ ذِكْرُهَا في الفنّ بِطريق المبدئية لمسائِلَ أُخَرَى . (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا إِذَا عُلِمَ ...إلخ) لا ارتباط بما قَبلَهُ إِلَّا بِتقدير : ليتعرَّفَ أحكامها منها الذي هو بقيَّةُ تَعريف القانون، والكافُ لمجرَّدِ قرانِ الفعلين في الوجودِ كما في الرّضى
Applying to particulars. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'applying' meaning including with potential close to actuality, not actual inclusion; because what is actually obtained is the judgment concerning the universal matter which is the subject of the law, not the judgments concerning the particulars of the subject. His statement: 'to particulars' meaning to the particulars of its subject; because the law itself has no particulars; for particulars are individuals of the universal concept, and in the speech there is an omitted augment, i.e., to the judgments of the particulars. Al-Attar: By codification, naming, and teaching; in view of what belongs to that group, despite their multiplicity and the difference of their predicates, of unity in the aspect of subject, i.e., sharing in it in the mentioned manner. Then they may be united from other aspects like benefit, goal, and the like, and for them from some of those aspects what benefits their conception in terms of summary, and in terms of their having unity, so it is a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates a real essence of its name, i.e., that composite intellectual thing, as it is said: it is a science in which such-and-such is investigated, or a science of such-and-such rules; otherwise, it is a description (rasm) as it is said: it is a science by which one is able to do such-and-such, or by which one guards against such-and-such, or it is an instrument for such-and-such. So it became clear that the subject is the aspect of the unity of the questions of a single science in view of their essence; even if other aspects like definition and goal occur to them, and that there is no meaning for this being a science and that being another science except that it investigates the states of another thing different from it in essence or in consideration. So the differentiation of sciences in themselves and in view of their essences is only by the subject, although they are differentiated for the student by what they have of definitions and goals and the like. This is a summary discourse on the aspect of the unity of science; its detail is in the expansive books, and it has been written separately. His statement: 'a universal applying to particulars' – the two descriptions are revealing their inclusion in the concept of the described, i.e., the universality. And by 'particulars' are meant particulars that have an additional connection to that proposition such that its truth depends on their existence. They are the particulars of the subject of the affirmative categorical proposition; necessarily because the truth of the negative does not depend on the existence of its subject, and the truth of the conditional does not depend on the existence of the subject of its two parts. So the universal negative is excluded from the definition of the law like the conditional; thus it agrees with what is established with them that the parts of the art are affirmative categorical universal propositions. Even if 'Abd al-Hakim said that the negative is among the laws, and he justified that by saying that deriving the branches is from the affirmatives as well as from the negatives. Then if 'applying' (inṭibāq) is interpreted as predication (ḥaml), then by 'particulars' are meant the individuals of that universal concept which is the subject of the law. For example, if it is said: 'Every agent (fā'il) is raised (marfū')', the concept of an agent, i.e., the one who performed the event, is a universal, and it has individuals occurring in constructions like 'Zaid stood' and 'Amr traveled', etc. There is no hiddenness in the correctness of predicating that universal concept on those individuals; for you say: 'Zaid is the one who stood; Zaid is an agent', 'Amr is the one who traveled; Amr is an agent', etc. And if it is interpreted as inclusion (ishtimāl), then by 'particulars' are meant the branches of that rule, likening them to particulars in subsumption, contrary to the common usage of 'particulars' for individuals of the universal. And what is meant by the rule's inclusion of those branches is their existence in it potentially, not actually; for what is actually obtained is only one judgment, which is obtained in the predication of the predicate of the proposition on its subject. Then the commentator omitted the condition of 'learning' (taʿarruf) due to its obviousness and due to the indication of his statement 'as when it is known...' etc. Some virtuous ones said: and in the form of 'tafaʿʿul' (i.e., the word yataʿarrafu) there is an indication that that knowledge is with difficulty and effort. So the universal proposition whose branches are self-evident and not needing derivation is excluded from the definition; like our statement: 'The first figure produces conclusions', so its mention in the art is by way of being first principles for other questions. (His statement: 'as when it is known...' etc.) has no connection with what preceded except by appreciation: 'to learn its judgments from it' which is the remainder of the definition of law. And the kāf is merely for the conjunction of the two actions in existence, as in al-Rida.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the definition of 'law' (qānūn) in logic explains that a law is a universal proposition applying to particulars, but only affirmative categoricals qualify. The passage begins with al-Dasuqi's gloss on 'applying' (munṭabiqa), stating that it means potential inclusion close to actuality, not actual inclusion, because only the universal judgment is actually present in the law, while the particular judgments are present potentially. He then glosses 'to particulars' as the particulars of the subject of the law, noting an omitted phrase: 'judgments of' the particulars. Al-Attar then provides a broader discussion on the unity of sciences by their subject matter, showing that the subject is the aspect of unity. He then returns to the definition: a universal proposition applying to particulars. The two descriptors 'universal' and 'applying to particulars' clarify that the proposition's truth depends on the existence of the particulars, which is only true for affirmative categoricals (since negatives are true even if subject non-existent, and conditionals do not require existence of subject terms). Thus, negative and conditional universals are excluded from the definition of 'law', in agreement with the principle that the parts of logic are affirmative categorical universals. There is an objection from 'Abd al-Hakīm that negative propositions can also be laws since branches can be derived from them. The response is by interpreting 'applying' (inṭibāq) in two ways: (1) as predication (ḥaml) – then particulars are individuals of the subject concept, and the example 'Every agent (fā'il) is raised (marfū')' shows how the universal concept is predicated of its individuals. (2) as inclusion (ishtimāl) – then particulars are the branches (derived rules) subsumed under the law, and the inclusion is potential, not actual. This distinction resolves the objection: under the predication interpretation, negatives do not have their truth dependent on individuals, so they are not laws; under the inclusion interpretation, negatives can still be considered laws if they potentially include branches. The passage concludes with a note that the commentator omitted the word 'learning' (taʿarruf) from the definition because it is obvious, and that self-evident propositions like 'The first figure produces conclusions' are not considered laws because they do not require effortful derivation.

Key Terms
قانون (qānūn): A universal proposition from which particular judgments are derived; specifically an affirmative categorical universal proposition whose truth depends on the existence of its subject's particulars.
منطبقة (munṭabiqa): Applying to particulars potentially, meaning the inclusion of particulars under the universal concept is potential, not actual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No human is immortal') not considered a 'law' (qanun)?
A) Because it is not a categorical proposition.
B) Because its truth does not depend on the existence of its subject's particulars; it could be true even if no humans exist.
C) Because it is always false.
D) Because it cannot be converted to an affirmative.

Key Takeaway
A logical law must be an affirmative categorical universal whose truth depends on the existence of its subject's particulars; negatives and conditionals do not satisfy this, but the two interpretations of 'applying' (predication vs. inclusion) provide flexibility in analyzing borderline cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the proposition 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)'. Determine whether it qualifies as a 'law' (qanun) according to the definition in this lesson. Explain your reasoning, referencing the condition of truth-dependence on particulars.
?
Task 2: The proposition 'No riba (usury) is permissible' is a universal negative. Using the predication interpretation of 'applying', explain why it does not satisfy the definition of a law. Then, using the inclusion interpretation, discuss whether it could be considered a law and why the author still excludes it.

What Is Mantiq?

Logic as a Defender: Understanding Metaphorical Attribution (Majaz Aqli)
Author answers objection that logic itself is not a defender; explains the attribution is metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the metaphorical attribution (majaz aqli) in the statement 'logic is a defender against error' and distinguish it from a literal claim.

Core Idea
Logic is a defender metaphorically — true protection comes from Allah.

In this passage, the author anticipates an objection: how can logic be called a defender if it does not act? The answer reveals a deep rhetorical tool: mental metaphor (majaz aqli), where an effect is attributed to its cause. This lesson unpacks the dialectical exchange and the commentators' refinements, showing how Islamic scholars carefully distinguish literal and figurative language in defining the role of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the mu'tarid's question in the Arabic: 'Logic itself is not a defender against error, but observation of it is; so how is the defender applied to it?' Note that the objector distinguishes between logic and its observance.
2
Extract the author's answer
The author replies: 'This application is metaphorical.' Then Dusuqi clarifies that in appearance the defender is observance, but in reality the defender is Allah. So the metaphor attributes the effect (protection) to its cause (logic) rather than its true agent.
Dusuqi also rewords the objector's question to be about 'attribution of infallibility' rather than 'application of defender'.
3
Understand the commentators' refinement
Al-Attar explains that the objection is merely an inquiry (istifsar), not a genuine refutation (man'), because there is no evidence presented. He also notes that the denial of logic's defensive property before evidence is not accepted; after evidence, denial becomes metaphorical itself.
This shows the dialectical rules: a claim is not denied before evidence is given.
4
Recognize the type of metaphor
Dusuqi points out that the metaphor is mental (majaz aqli), not linguistic (majaz lughawi). In mental metaphor, an action is attributed to something other than its true doer, e.g., attributing infallibility to logic instead of observance or to Allah. The gist is that attributing infallibility to logic is a case of attributing to a cause.
Distinguish from linguistic metaphor which changes the meaning of a word; here the attribution itself is figurative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: المنطقُ نفْسُه ليسَ عاصماً الخطأ، عن مراعاته، فكيف يُطلَقُ العاصِمُ عليه ؟! . قُلْتُ : هذا الإطلاق مجازي، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : بَلِ الْعَاصِمُ مُرَاعَاتُهُ) أي : بل العاصم بحسبِ الظَّاهِرِ مُراعاتُهُ؛ أي: ملاحظته ، فلا ينافي أنَّ العاصمَ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ؛ المولى جَلَّ وَعَزَّ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَكَيْفَ يُطْلَقُ . . . إِلَخ) المناسب : فكيف يسند العصمة إليه؟ . (قَوْلُهُ : قُلْتُ : هَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ مَجَازِيٌّ) ظاهرُهُ : أَنَّهُ مجاز لغوي، مع أنَّهُ مجاز عقليّ، وهو إسنادُ الفعل أو ما في معناه لغير من هو له، فحقُ العصمة أن تسند للمراعاة؛ لا للمنطق، فالمناسب كذلكَ أن يقول : قُلْت : هذا الإسناد مجازي. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ قُلْتَ هذا السُّؤالُ مُجرَّدُ استفسارٍ عَن صِحَّةِ وَصْفِ المنطقِ بِالعصمة؛ يدلُّ عليهِ قولُهُ: فكيفَ يُطلَقُ)، وليسَ مِن قَبيل المنوَّع؛ إذْ لا دليل، ومنع الدعوى الضّمنيَّة الَّتي تَضمَّنَها قَولُهُ: يعصم ، وهو أنَّ المنطقَ عَاصِمٌ غيرُ مَسموع ؛ إذ لا تمنعُ الدعوى قبل الاستدلال، أمَّا بعدَهُ فَاستعمال المنع فيها مَجاز كَمَا قرَّرَهُ. (قَوْلُهُ : هَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ مَجَازِيٌّ) أي: إطلاقُ العِصْمَةِ على المنطق، ولو عُبّرَ بالإسنادِ لَكَانَ أحسنَ ، ومُحصِّلُهُ أنَّ إسناد العصمةِ لِلمنطقِ مِن قبيل الإسنادِ لِلسَّبَبِ.
For if you say: Logic itself is not a defender against error, but rather observation of it [is], so how is the defender applied to it? I say: This application is metaphorical. Dusuqi: (His statement: 'Rather the defender is observation of it') meaning: Rather the defender in appearance is its observation, i.e., heeding it, so it does not contradict that the defender in reality is Allah, glorified and exalted. (His statement: 'So how is it applied... etc.') The appropriate [wording] is: So how is infallibility attributed to it? (His statement: I say: This application is metaphorical.) Its apparent meaning is that it is a linguistic metaphor, while it is a mental metaphor, which is the attribution of an action or something in its meaning to other than the one to whom it truly belongs. So the right of infallibility is that it be attributed to observation, not to logic. So the appropriate [response] would be to say: I say: This attribution is metaphorical. Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'For if you say') This question is merely an inquiry about the correctness of describing logic with infallibility; his statement 'so how is it applied' indicates that. And it is not of the type of objection because there is no evidence, and denial of the implicit claim contained in his statement 'it defends' — that logic is a defender — is not heard; for a claim is not denied before evidence. As for after [evidence], using denial in it is metaphorical as he established. (His statement: 'This application is metaphorical') meaning: the application of infallibility to logic. And if he had expressed it as attribution, it would be better. The gist of it is that attributing infallibility to logic is a case of attributing to a cause.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and answer regarding the characterization of logic as a 'defender' (asim) against error. The objector argues that logic itself does not actually defend; rather, it is the act of observing its rules that protects one from error. So how can logic be called 'defender'? The answer is that this attribution is metaphorical. Dusuqi elaborates: the apparent defender is the observation of logic, but the real defender is God. The objector's wording should have been 'attribution of infallibility' rather than 'application of the defender'. Dusuqi notes that the metaphor is not linguistic but mental (majaz 'aqli), where the action is attributed to something other than its true agent (the cause instead of the effect). Here, infallibility is rightly attributed to observing logic, but it is figuratively attributed to logic itself as a cause. Al-'Attar adds that the objection is not a genuine refutation (man') but merely an inquiry seeking clarification, because there is no argument given. The denial of logic's defensive property is not accepted before evidence; after evidence, denial becomes metaphorical. He agrees that the attribution is a mental metaphor of cause.

Key Terms
العاصم (al-‘āṣim): Literally 'the defender'; here used metaphorically for logic as a tool that prevents error when observed. [Note: In earlier texts, logic is considered a defensive science; this passage refines the attribution to metaphorical.]
مجاز عقلي (majāz ‘aqlī): A mental metaphor where an action is attributed to something other than its true doer, e.g., attributing protection to logic instead to its observance. [Note: Standard rhetorical term; no significant shift.]
إسناد (isnād): Attribution of a predicate to a subject; here specifically the attribution of 'infallibility' to logic. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dusuqi, why is calling logic a 'defender' not a literal statement?
A) Because logic cannot prevent error at all.
B) Because the real defender is God, and logic is only a cause.
C) Because the objector's argument is invalid.
D) Because logic is not a science.

Key Takeaway
In the Islamic dialectical tradition, attributing an effect to an intermediate cause (like logic) is a mental metaphor; true agency belongs to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a similar mental metaphor in a statement from a fiqh text, e.g., 'The sunnah indicates this ruling.' Explain why this is majaz aqli (attributing indication to the sunnah when true guidance comes from Allah).
?
Analyze the objector's question: Why does al-Attar say it is merely an inquiry (istifsar), not a refutation (man')? Use the passage to support your answer.

Defending Logic as 'Protective' – The Mental Metaphor
Author defends describing logic as protective by explaining it as figurative attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain how mental metaphor (majaz aqli) is used to defend logical terminology, such as calling logic 'protective'.

Core Idea
Calling logic 'protective' is not literal but a mental metaphor that emphasizes its importance.

A student objects: 'Logic itself doesn't protect; only observing it does. So why call it protective?' The teacher answers: 'This is a figure of speech.' But what kind of figure? The commentators explain it's a mental metaphor—a deliberate attribution to the cause, not the real agent. This lesson unpacks that defense and shows how such figurative language works in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
The objector says logic itself is not protective; the protection comes from observing its rules. Therefore, calling logic 'protective' seems contradictory.
The objection points to a real distinction: tool vs. application.
2
Find the author's reply (jawab)
The author replies that the application is figurative (majaz). This immediately signals that the word 'protective' is not used in its literal sense.
The author intentionally avoids claiming literal protection.
3
Distinguish linguistic vs. mental metaphor
Commentator Attar clarifies that this is not a linguistic metaphor but a mental metaphor (majaz aqli). In mental metaphor, an action is attributed to something other than its real doer, like attributing protection to logic when the real agent is observance.
Mental metaphor is common in Arabic: 'Zaid built the house' when Zaid only ordered it.
4
Understand the purpose: emphasis and encouragement
Dassuqi explains that attributing protection to logic itself emphasizes the need to learn, teach, and apply logic. The figurative attribution serves a pedagogical purpose.
The metaphor makes the point more forceful than a literal statement would.
5
Connect to the broader principle of attribution
Attar quotes al-Sakkaki: events are attributed to the apparent agent. Here, the apparent agent of protection is observance, but figuratively it is logic. The real agent in reality is God. This shows the layered nature of causation in Islamic thought.
Understanding this helps avoid theological confusion about agency.

فَهوَ مجاز عقلي، والإسنادُ الحقيقي إنَّما هو للمراعاة ، وقَوْلُ المحنِّي : التَّحقيقُ أَنَّ العَاصِمَ هو الله ؛ إنْ أرادَ أَنَّ العَاصِمَ هو الله بالنِّسبة للحقيقة؛ أي: الواقع؛ فَجَميعُ الأفعالِ كُلُّها مَخلوقةٌ لهُ سبحانَهُ ، فهوَ فاعِلٌ في الحقيقة؛ أي: بالنسبة للواقع، وهذا غير منظور إليه، بل المنظورُ إليه في الإسنادِ ؛ الفاعِلُ الظَّاهِرُ ، كما قالَ الشَّكَّاكِيُّ : إنَّ الحدثَ الَّذي يظهرُ فاعلُهُ يُنسَبُ إليه، والذي لا يظهرُ ؛ يُنسَبُ إلى ذاتِهِ تعالى .ا.هـ. ولا خفاءَ أَنَّ الفَاعِلَ الظَّاهري للعصمةِ هو المراعاة، ولو التفثنا للواقع؛ لانسدَّ بَابُ الحقيقة العقلية.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَفِيهِ مِنَ التَّأْكِيْدِ) فإنَّ إسناد العصمةِ إليهِ أَزيَدُ تأكيداً في الاحتياج إليهِ مِن إسنادها للمراعاة، وقوله : (والمبالغة) أي : في الاحتياج إليه، وهو بمعنى ما قبله.
If you say: Logic itself is not protective against error, by observing it, so how is the title 'protective' applied to it? I say: This application is figurative. [Dassuqi] Rather, the protective is its observance. (His saying: 'And likewise its parallels' — it is possible that what is meant is parallels of the mentioned particular, like 'Every horse is an animal', and it is possible that what is meant is parallels of the mentioned rule, that the particular affirmative converts as itself, and the particular negative converts as itself; so if it is known that the universal negative converts as itself, it is known that 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human'.) (His saying: 'Logic itself' means the specific rules.) (His saying: 'Rather the protective is its observance' means: rather the protective, apparently, is its observance, i.e., paying attention to it; this does not contradict that the protective in reality is God, may He be glorified and exalted.) (His saying: 'So how is it applied... etc.' The appropriate wording is: How is protection attributed to it?) (His saying: 'I say: This application is figurative' — its apparent meaning is that it is a linguistic metaphor, but it is actually a mental metaphor, which is attributing an action or what is in its meaning to other than its doer. So the right of protection is to be attributed to observance, not to logic. So the appropriate response would be to say: This attribution is figurative.) [Attar] (His saying: 'If you say' — this question is merely an inquiry about the validity of describing logic as protective; his saying 'So how is it applied' indicates that. It is not of the type of objection (man') since there is no proof, and preventing the implicit claim contained in his saying 'it protects' — that logic is protective — is not permissible; for the claim is not prevented before evidence, but after it, using prevention (man') in it is figurative as he established.) (His saying: 'This application is figurative' means the attribution of protection to logic; and if he had expressed it as attribution it would be better. The gist is that attributing protection to logic is of the type of attribution to the cause.) In it is emphasis and exaggeration that are not hidden. [Dassuqi] (His saying: 'In it' means in this figurative application. (His saying: 'Of emphasis' means because attributing protection to logic contains a sign urging learning it, teaching it, and observing it.) (His saying: 'And exaggeration' means in that he attributed protection to it while its right is to be attributed to its observance.) [Attar] So it is a mental metaphor, and the real attribution is only to observance. And the saying of al-Muhanni: 'The reality is that the protective is God' — if he means that the protective is God in terms of reality, i.e., the actual, then all actions are created by Him, so He is the agent in reality, i.e., relative to the actual. This is not taken into consideration; rather what is taken into consideration in attribution is the apparent agent, as al-Sakkaki said: 'An event whose agent is apparent is attributed to it, and one whose agent is not apparent is attributed to God Most High.' And it is obvious that the apparent agent of protection is observance. If we turned to the actual, the door of mental attribution would be closed. (His saying: 'In it is emphasis') — for attributing protection to it is more emphatic in indicating the need for it than attributing it to observance. And his saying: 'and exaggeration' means in the need for it, and it is synonymous with what preceded.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and its answer concerning the description of logic as 'protective' ('asima). The objector argues that logic itself is not inherently protective; rather, protection from error comes from actually observing its rules. So how can logic be called 'protective'? The author (al-musannif) replies that the application is figurative (majaz). The commentators then clarify that this is a mental metaphor (majaz aqli), not a linguistic one. In a mental metaphor, an action is attributed to something that is not the actual doer but a cause or instrument. Here, protection is rightly attributed to the observance of logic, but figuratively attributed to logic itself as if it were the agent. This is an attribution to the cause (al-isnad ila al-sabab). Dassuqi explains that the apparent agent (al-fa'il al-zahiri) of protection is the observance, while the real agent in reality is God. The figurative attribution serves to emphasize the importance of studying and applying logic. Attar adds that the objection is merely an inquiry (istifham) and not a full objection (man'), since no proof is given. The passage illustrates a standard technique in Islamic logic: defending a technical description by clarifying its figurative nature.

Key Terms
العاصِم
المجاز العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is describing logic as 'protective' considered a mental metaphor (majaz aqli)?
A) Because logic is actually protective in reality.
B) Because protection is attributed to logic when the real agent is observance.
C) Because the description is linguistically incorrect.
D) Because logic protects from all errors without effort.

Key Takeaway
The description of logic as 'protective' is a mental metaphor that attributes protection to logic as a cause, while the real agent is the observance of its rules.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following statement uses mental metaphor (majaz aqli) and explain: 'The Quran guides to the truth.' Who is the real agent of guidance? What is the figurative attribution?
?
Construct a defense of the phrase 'Hadith protects from innovation' using the same reasoning as the logic passage. Explain who the real agent is and why the figurative attribution is beneficial.

What Is Mantiq?

Using the Definition of Logic to Classify Problems
Commentary explains how knowledge of the definition of logic enables one to determine whether a given problem belongs to logic via a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism using the definition of logic to determine whether a given problem belongs to the science of logic.

Core Idea
The definition of logic provides a universal premise that, combined with a particular problem's attribute, yields a decisive syllogistic judgment.

This passage expands on how knowing the definition of logic as a tool that protects the mind from error allows one to classify any problem. Al-Dasuqi demonstrates a syllogism: every problem that participates in this protection is from logic. If a specific problem has that role, it follows that it belongs to logic. The commentary emphasizes that this knowledge is a capability (tamakkun) rather than automatic recognition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of logic
Identify that logic is defined as a legal tool whose observance protects the mind from error. This definition gives the essential characteristic shared by all logical problems.
The definition is the source of the major premise.
2
Formulate the universal premise
From the definition, derive: 'Every problem that has a role in protecting the mind from error is a problem of logic.' This is the major premise of the syllogism.
This premise is universal affirmative (A).
3
Encounter a specific problem
When a particular problem arises (e.g., the problem of conversion of propositions), examine whether it involves protecting the mind from error through logical rules.
This becomes the minor premise.
4
Check the minor premise
If the problem has a role in that protection, state: 'This specific problem has a role in protecting the mind from error.' If not, state the negation.
The minor premise must be true for the syllogism to be sound.
5
Draw the conclusion
Combine the major and minor premises in a categorical syllogism. For example: Major: Every problem with a role in protection is from logic. Minor: This problem has a role. Conclusion: Therefore, this problem is from logic.
The syllogism follows the first figure (Barbara) if both premises are universal affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّ كلَّ مسألة من هذا العِلْمِ تَرِدُ عليه يَعلَمُ أَنَّهَا الدسوقي منه وكلُّ مسألة لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة؛ فهي من المنطق، ينتج أنَّ هذه المسألة مِنَ المنطق، وإن لم تكن المسألة الواردة عليكَ لَها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة. قُلْتُ : هذه المسألةَ ليس لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة، وكلُّ مسألةٍ كَذلِكَ، فليسَتْ مِنَ المنطق، ينتجُ : هذه المسألة ليسَتْ مِنَ المنطقِ . إذا عَلِمْتَ هذا ؛ فقولُ الشَّارح: (وإذا تصوّره برسمه)؛ أي: بأن تصوّره بأنَّهُ آلةٌ قانونيَّةٌ تعصمُ مُراعاتُهَا الزّهنَ عَن الخطأ، والحالُ أَنَّهُ عارفٌ أَنَّ ذلك تعريف للمنطق، وقوله : (حصلَ لَهُ العِلْمُ ... إلخ)؛ هو العِلْمُ بالمقدِّمة الحاصلة من تصورِ العِلْم برسمه، ومعرفةُ أَنَّهُ تَعريفه، وهي القائلةُ : كلُّ مَسألة من مسائل المنطق لها دخل في العصمة المذكورة، وقوله : ( حتَّى إن ... إلخ) غاية؛ لقوله : حصل له العِلْمُ . . . إلخ، وقولُه : علم أنَّها ؛ أي: تلك المسألة الواردة عليه منه؛ أي: من ذلك العِلم، والمراد بقوله : (علم أنَّها منه ؛ تمَكُنْ مِنْ عِلْمٍ أَنَّها منه تمكناً تاما، بأن يأتي بالقياس السابق المنتج ؛ لأنَّها منه، وحينئذ ؛ يعلم أنَّها منه، وَلَمَّا كانَ هذا التَّمْكُنُ تامَّا قويًّا؛ عبّر عنه بالعِلم، وليس المرادُ أنَّه بمجرَّدِ ورودِ تلك المسألة عليه ; يعلمُ بالفعل أنَّها منه بدونِ تأمل وقياس؛ لأنَّ هذا خلافُ الواقع. العطار مِنْهُ لَهَا مَدْخَلٌ فِي تِلْكَ العِصمةِ، ويُمكِن بسبب معرفةِ تِلكَ المقدَّمَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ مسائله وتمييزها عن غيرها تَمكُناً تاما، فإذا وردّ عليهِ مسألةٌ مُعيَّنَةٌ لَهَا مَدخل في تلكَ العِصمةِ؛ تَمَكَّنَ مِن أنْ يعلم أنَّها مِنَ المنطقِ ; لِوُجودِ قِيَاسِ عِندَهُ؛ هُوَ أَنَّ هذهِ المسألةَ لها مَدخَلٌ في العصمةِ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وكُلُّ مَسألةٍ كَذلكَ؛ فَهِيَ مِنَ المنطق، فهذه المسألةُ مِنَ المنطقِ، وَقِش على ذلك بقيَّةَ العُلُوم. (قَوْلُهُ : يَعْلَمُ أَنَّهَا مِنْهُ) أي: تمكَّنَ مِن علمها تمكُناً تاما بواسطة المقدِّمةِ الَّتي حصَّلَها مِنَ التّعريف فينتظم عندَهُ القياسُ السَّابِقُ، قال عبد الحكيم : والتَّمكُنُ المذكورُ لا يُنافي عدم حصولِ التَّمييز بالفعل في بعض المسائل، كما أنَّ التَّمْكُنَ مِنَ الاجتهادِ لا يُنافي وقوع «لا أدري» في بعض المسائل مِنَ المجتهد، وهذا التَّمَكِّنُ مَنشَؤُهُ كَوْنُ التّعريفِ مَأخوذاً من جهة الوحدة التي تشترك فيها جميعُ المسائل .
Indeed, every problem from this science that occurs to him, he knows that it is from it. al-Dasuqi: From it, and every problem that has a role in the mentioned protection is from logic; it follows that this problem is from logic. And if the problem that occurs to you does not have a role in the mentioned protection, I say: This problem has no role in the mentioned protection, and every problem like that is not from logic; it follows: This problem is not from logic. If you know this, then the commentator's statement: (And if he conceives it by its description) i.e., by conceiving it as a legal tool whose observance protects the mind from error, while he knows that that is the definition of logic. And his statement: (He obtains knowledge... etc.) is the knowledge of the premise obtained from conceiving the science by its description and knowing that it is its definition, which says: Every problem of logic has a role in the mentioned protection. And his statement: (Until... etc.) is an ultimate end for his statement: He obtains knowledge... etc. And his statement: He knows that it is from it; i.e., that problem that occurs to him is from that science. The intended meaning of his statement: (He knows that it is from it) is that he is capable of knowing that it is from it with complete capability, by bringing the preceding syllogism that proves that it is from it, and then he knows that it is from it. And since this capability is complete and strong, he expressed it as knowledge. It is not meant that merely by the occurrence of that problem to him he actually knows that it is from it without reflection and syllogism, because that is contrary to reality. al-Attar: From it, it has a role in that protection, and it is possible, by knowing that universal premise, to know its problems and distinguish them from others with complete capability. So when a specific problem that has a role in that protection occurs to him, he is capable of knowing that it is from logic, because he has a syllogism: This problem has a role in protection from error in thought, and every problem like that is from logic, so this problem is from logic. And analogize to that the rest of the sciences. (His statement: He knows that it is from it) i.e., he is capable of knowing it with complete capability by means of the premise that he obtained from the definition, so the preceding syllogism is organized for him. 'Abd al-Hakim said: The mentioned capability does not preclude the non-occurrence of actual distinction in some problems, just as the capability of ijtihad does not preclude the occurrence of 'I do not know' in some problems from the mujtahid. And this capability originates from the fact that the definition is taken from the aspect of the unity that all problems share.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on how the definition of logic serves as a criterion for identifying whether any given problem belongs to the science of logic. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by presenting a syllogism: the universal premise is that every problem that has a role in protecting the mind from error is a problem of logic. If a specific problem (such as the definition of subject-predicate propositions) has such a role, then it is from logic. Conversely, if a problem lacks that role, it is not from logic. He then explains the statement of the earlier commentator (al-sharih) about conceiving logic by its description, i.e., as a legal tool whose observance protects the mind from error. Knowing this definition gives one the universal premise "Every problem of logic has a role in protection." With this premise, when a specific problem is encountered, one can construct a categorical syllogism to determine its membership. Al-Dasuqi emphasizes that this knowledge is a complete capability (tamakkun tamm), not necessarily actual instantaneous knowledge. The student should not think that merely knowing the definition automatically makes one recognize all problems without reflection. Al-Attar adds that with the universal premise, one can distinguish the problems of logic from others by applying the syllogism. For example, the problem of whether a syllogism is valid falls under this criterion. Finally, 'Abd al-Hakim notes that the capability of distinction does not require that one never says 'I don't know'; just as a mujtahid may occasionally be uncertain despite having the ability to derive rulings. The capability arises because the definition captures the common aspect (al-wahda) shared by all problems of logic.

Key Terms
العصمة
التمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the function of the definition of logic in relation to specific problems?
A) It directly identifies all problems as logical without reasoning.
B) It provides a universal premise that enables a syllogism to determine membership.
C) It is irrelevant to distinguishing logical from non-logical problems.
D) It only works for problems that are already known to be logical.

Key Takeaway
The definition of logic functions as the major premise in a categorical syllogism that determines membership of any problem in the science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the definition of logic as 'a tool whose observance protects the mind from error', construct a categorical syllogism to determine whether the problem 'Is the law of non-contradiction a necessary condition for valid inference?' belongs to logic.
?
Explain in your own words why the capability (tamakkun) provided by the definition does not guarantee that every logician will instantly recognize all logical problems without reflection.

5 min
The Subject of a Science: Definition and Function
Commentary defines the subject of a science and gives an example from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the subject of a science (mawdu' al-'ilm) and identify it in any Islamic science.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is the foundation upon which all its problems are built.

The commentator explains that after defining the need for logic, the text turns to its subject. Understanding what a 'subject of a science' means is key to grasping how each Islamic science (like fiqh or usul al-fiqh) is structured. In this lesson, we will break down the definition and see it applied.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The passage defines the subject of a science as 'that about which its essential accidents are investigated.' The essential accidents are the properties that naturally pertain to the subject. For example, in fiqh, the subject is the act of the mukallaf, and the accidents include rulings like wajib, haram, etc.
This is the core concept.
2
See the fiqh example
The commentator gives fiqh as an example: its subject is the act of the legally responsible person (mukallaf). All legal rulings (wajib, haram, etc.) are accidents studied about that act.
Remember this well.
3
Apply to other sciences
Now think of other Islamic sciences. For usul al-fiqh, the subject is the evidence (dalil) from which rulings are derived. For tafsir, the subject is the words of the Quran. Identifying the subject helps you understand the scope of each science.
Practice with kalām too: its subject is existence in general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمٍ الْمَنْطِقِ) وَلَمَّا فَرَغَ من بيانِ الحاجةِ المُنْساقِ إلى تعريف العلم برسمه؛ شرع في بیان موضوع العِلم ، فقال : (وَمَوْضُوعُهُ)؛ أي: موضوع المنطق : الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَلَمَّا فَرَغَ مِنْ بَيَانِ الْحَاجَةِ أي: مِن تبيين ما يفيد التصديق بالحاجة؛ أي: التصديق بأنها كذا ، وقوله : (المنساق)؛ صفةٌ لبيان، وقوله : (لتعريف العلم)؛ أي : المفيد لتصورِهِ، وقوله : (برسمه)؛ مُتعلّق بتعريف، وقوله: (شرع في بيانِ موضوعِ العِلْمِ؛ أي: في تبيين ما يفيد التصديق بموضوعيَّةِ العِلْمِ؛ أي: التصديق بأنَّ المعلومَ التّصوري، والتصديقي موضوع هذا العِلْم. (قَوْلُهُ : وَمَوْضُوْعُهُ ... إِلَخ) اعلَمْ أنَّ موضوع العِلْمِ هو ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ، وذلك بأن تجعل موضوع العِلْم موضوعاً لمسائله، وتحمل عليه عوارضه الذاتية، فإذا أخذْتَ موضوعَ العِلْم، وحملت عليه عارضاً من عوارضهِ الذَّاتِيَّة؛ حصلت مسألةٌ مِن مسائل ذلكَ العِلم، فالمراد بالبحث في ذلك العِلْمِ عن العوارض؛ إثباتُها لِمَوضوعات المسائل، مثلاً : علم الفقه؛ موضوعُهُ فِعْلُ المكلَّف، العطار (قَوْلُهُ: وَمَوْضُوْعُهُ) قَالَ میر زاهد : ذهب المتقدِّمونَ إلى أنَّ موضوعَهُ المعقولات الثَّانيةُ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها تُوصِلُ إلى المجهول، وعدل المتأخّرونَ عنه إلى ذلكَ؛ لأنَّ كثيراً ما يبحث في المنطقِ عن نفس المعقولاتِ الثَّانيةِ ; كالذَّاتيّةِ والعَرَضيَّةِ، والمبحوث عنه في العِلْمِ هو أحوال الموضوعِ لَا نَفْسُهُ، وأنتَ خبير بأنَّه لا يبحث في المنطقِ عن المعقولِ الثَّاني من حيثُ هو معقول ثانٍ، بَل مِن حيثُ هو أحوال معقول ثانٍ آخَرَ ، مثلاً : يبحث عن أحوالِ الذَّاتيّةِ والعَرَضيَّةِ مِن حيثُ إنَّهما مِن أحوالِ الكُلِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِنَ المعقولاتِ الثَّانية، ثمَّ المعلومُ التَّصوريُّ والتَّصديقي؛ مَفهومُهُمَا لا يصحُ لِأَنْ يبحث عنهُ مِن حيثُ الإيصال على الوجهِ الكُلّيّ، وكذا مَا صَدقًا عليهِ مِنَ المعقولاتِ الأُولى كما يظهرُ بالتَّأمُّلِ الصَّادِقِ، فَلَا بُدَّ هَهُنا مِن رُجوعهما إلى المعقولات الثانية،
(The subject of the science of logic) And when he finished from the explanation of the need that leads to defining the science by its description, he began explaining the subject of the science, so he said: (And its subject) i.e., the subject of logic. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: And when he finished from the explanation of the need) i.e., from clarifying what gives assent (tasdiq) to the need, i.e., the assent that it is such. And his statement: (the leading) is an adjective for explanation. And his statement: (to defining the science) i.e., that which gives conception (tasawwur) of it. And his statement: (by its description) is related to defining. And his statement: (he began explaining the subject of the science) i.e., in clarifying what gives assent to the subjectivity (mawdu'iyya) of the science, i.e., the assent that the conceptual known (al-ma'lum al-tasawwuri) and the assent known (al-tasdiqi) are the subject of this science. (His statement: And its subject... etc.) Know that the subject of a science is that about which its essential accidents (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) are investigated. That is by making the subject of the science the subject of its problems and ascribing to it its essential accidents. So when you take the subject of the science and ascribe to it an accident from its essential accidents, a problem from the problems of that science results. Thus the intended meaning of investigation in that science about accidents is to establish them for the subjects of the problems. For example, the science of fiqh; its subject is the act of the legally responsible person. al-Attar: (His statement: And its subject) Mir Zahid said: The earlier scholars held that its subject is the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) insofar as they lead to the unknown. And the later scholars deviated from that to (the view that the subject is) the conceptual and the assent known, because often in logic one investigates the secondary intelligibles themselves, such as essentiality and accidentally. But what is investigated in a science is the states of the subject, not the subject itself. And you are an expert that logic does not investigate the secondary intelligible insofar as it is a secondary intelligible, but rather insofar as it is a state of another secondary intelligible. For example, one investigates the states of essentiality and accidentally insofar as they are states of universality (al-kulliyya), which is among the secondary intelligibles. Moreover, the conceptual known and the assent known: their concepts cannot be investigated from the aspect of leading (to the unknown) in a universal way, nor can their instantiations from the primary intelligibles, as appears by true reflection. So here they must be reduced to the secondary intelligibles.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines what the subject (mawdu') of a science is and then applies it to logic. The commentator al-Dasuqi first explains that after finishing the discussion of why a definition of logic is needed (the 'need' being to conceive the science), the text turns to the subject. The subject of a science is that thing whose essential accidents are studied. For example, in fiqh, the subject is the act of the mukallaf (legally responsible person), and the problems of fiqh are propositions about that act. For logic, the subject is traditionally the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya), i.e., concepts like universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentally, etc., insofar as they lead to knowledge of the unknown. Al-Attar then relates the view of Mir Zahid, noting a shift: earlier logicians held that the subject is the secondary intelligibles as they lead to the unknown, but later logicians modified this to the conceptual and assent forms of knowledge. The commentary resolves the disagreement by pointing out that logic does not study secondary intelligibles in themselves, but rather studies the accidents of other secondary intelligibles. For instance, it studies essentiality and accidentally not as independent concepts but as states of universality. The conceptual and assent known cannot be studied directly as subjects because they are not universal enough; they must be considered as secondary intelligibles. Thus, the true subject of logic is the secondary intelligibles considered as such, in their role leading to the unknown.

Key Terms
موضوع العلم
عوارض ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the subject of the science of fiqh?
A) The rulings (ahkam)
B) The act of the legally responsible person (fi'l al-mukallaf)
C) The Quran and Sunnah
D) The scholars (fuqaha)

Key Takeaway
Every Islamic science has a defined subject (mawdu') whose essential accidents (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) are investigated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify the subject of the science of usul al-fiqh and give one example of an essential accident studied in it. Use the definition from the lesson.
?
2. For the science of kalām, what would be its subject according to the definition? Think of a problem from kalām and show how it is formed by ascribing an accident to the subject.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
7 min
The Subject of Logic: Secondary Intelligibles and the Scholarly Disagreement
Commentary discusses the disagreement on the subject of logic between earlier and later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the earlier and later views on the subject of logic and why the later view must reduce to secondary intelligibles.

Core Idea
Logic investigates secondary intelligibles, not in themselves, but as states of other secondary intelligibles.

The passage touches on a debate: earlier logicians held that logic's subject is the secondary intelligibles insofar as they lead to the unknown. Later logicians shifted to tasawwur and tasdiq. The commentator argues that this shift is problematic and that the later view must ultimately refer back to secondary intelligibles. Let's unpack this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier view
According to Mir Zahid (as quoted), earlier logicians said the subject is the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) from the aspect that they lead to the unknown. This means concepts like universality, particularity, essentiality, etc., are studied because they help us reach unknown knowledge.
Secondary intelligibles are concepts about primary concepts (e.g., 'universal' applies to 'man').
2
Identify the later view
Later scholars changed the subject to 'the conceptual known (al-ma'lum al-tasawwuri) and the assent known (al-ma'lum al-tasdiqi).' They argued that the earlier view is problematic because logic often investigates the secondary intelligibles themselves (e.g., essentiality), but a science's subject is the thing whose accidents are studied, not the accidents themselves.
So they tried to broaden the subject.
3
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator notes that logic does not study secondary intelligibles in themselves; it studies the accidents of other secondary intelligibles. For example, it studies essentiality and accidentally as states of universality. So the subject must be the secondary intelligibles under a certain aspect.
He argues that the later view's subject—tasawwur and tasdiq—cannot be studied directly because they are not universal enough; they must be reduced to secondary intelligibles.
4
Grasp the resolution
The passage concludes that tasawwur and tasdiq must be considered as secondary intelligibles when studied in logic. Therefore, the true subject remains the secondary intelligibles, as the earlier scholars held.
This resolves the disagreement by showing the later view is a refinement, not a rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : وَمَوْضُوْعُهُ ... إِلَخ) اعلَمْ أنَّ موضوع العِلْمِ هو ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ، وذلك بأن تجعل موضوع العِلْم موضوعاً لمسائله، وتحمل عليه عوارضه الذاتية، فإذا أخذْتَ موضوعَ العِلْم، وحملت عليه عارضاً من عوارضهِ الذَّاتِيَّة؛ حصلت مسألةٌ مِن مسائل ذلكَ العِلم، فالمراد بالبحث في ذلك العِلْمِ عن العوارض؛ إثباتُها لِمَوضوعات المسائل، مثلاً : علم الفقه؛ موضوعُهُ فِعْلُ المكلَّف، العطار (قَوْلُهُ: وَمَوْضُوْعُهُ) قَالَ میر زاهد : ذهب المتقدِّمونَ إلى أنَّ موضوعَهُ المعقولات الثَّانيةُ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها تُوصِلُ إلى المجهول، وعدل المتأخّرونَ عنه إلى ذلكَ؛ لأنَّ كثيراً ما يبحث في المنطقِ عن نفس المعقولاتِ الثَّانيةِ ; كالذَّاتيّةِ والعَرَضيَّةِ، والمبحوث عنه في العِلْمِ هو أحوال الموضوعِ لَا نَفْسُهُ، وأنتَ خبير بأنَّه لا يبحث في المنطقِ عن المعقولِ الثَّاني من حيثُ هو معقول ثانٍ، بَل مِن حيثُ هو أحوال معقول ثانٍ آخَرَ ، مثلاً : يبحث عن أحوالِ الذَّاتيّةِ والعَرَضيَّةِ مِن حيثُ إنَّهما مِن أحوالِ الكُلِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِنَ المعقولاتِ الثَّانية، ثمَّ المعلومُ التَّصوريُّ والتَّصديقي؛ مَفهومُهُمَا لا يصحُ لِأَنْ يبحث عنهُ مِن حيثُ الإيصال على الوجهِ الكُلّيّ، وكذا مَا صَدقًا عليهِ مِنَ المعقولاتِ الأُولى كما يظهرُ بالتَّأمُّلِ الصَّادِقِ، فَلَا بُدَّ هَهُنا مِن رُجوعهما إلى المعقولات الثانية،

Commentary
This passage defines what the subject (mawdu') of a science is and then applies it to logic. The commentator al-Dasuqi first explains that after finishing the discussion of why a definition of logic is needed (the 'need' being to conceive the science), the text turns to the subject. The subject of a science is that thing whose essential accidents are studied. For example, in fiqh, the subject is the act of the mukallaf (legally responsible person), and the problems of fiqh are propositions about that act. For logic, the subject is traditionally the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya), i.e., concepts like universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentally, etc., insofar as they lead to knowledge of the unknown. Al-Attar then relates the view of Mir Zahid, noting a shift: earlier logicians held that the subject is the secondary intelligibles as they lead to the unknown, but later logicians modified this to the conceptual and assent forms of knowledge. The commentary resolves the disagreement by pointing out that logic does not study secondary intelligibles in themselves, but rather studies the accidents of other secondary intelligibles. For instance, it studies essentiality and accidentally not as independent concepts but as states of universality. The conceptual and assent known cannot be studied directly as subjects because they are not universal enough; they must be considered as secondary intelligibles. Thus, the true subject of logic is the secondary intelligibles considered as such, in their role leading to the unknown.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
The Two Types of Secondary Intelligibles and the Subject of Logic
Al-Attar divides secondary intelligibles and states logic's subject is the second type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the two types of secondary intelligibles and state why logic's subject is only the second type.

Core Idea
Logic studies mental attributes like universality, not existence itself.

Al-Attar's division of secondary intelligibles is essential for understanding the scope of logic. In this passage, he distinguishes concepts that require mental existence from those that do not. This distinction establishes the subject matter of logic as distinct from metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of secondary intelligible
A secondary intelligible is a concept that exists only in the mind as an attribute, like universality. Its defining characteristic is that the mind is the only locus for its occurrence.
The term 'secondary' indicates it is considered as an attribute of a mental concept.
2
Identify the two types based on the condition
Al-Attar divides secondary intelligibles into two types: those where mental existence is not a condition for attribution (e.g., existence, thingness) and those where it is a condition (e.g., universality, particularity).
The Arabic phrases are: 'لا يكون الوجود الذهني شرطاً للعروض' and 'يكون شرطاً له'.
3
Examine the examples given
The first type includes 'existence' (al-wujud) and 'thingness' (al-shay'iyya). The second type includes 'universality' (al-kulliyya) and 'particularity' (al-juz'iyya). These examples clarify the distinction.
Existence and thingness can be predicated of things whether mental or external; universality only applies mentally.
4
Determine the subject of logic
Al-Attar states that the subject of logic is the second division: 'وموضوعُ المنطق هو القسم الثاني'. Logic deals with concepts like universal and particular, which only apply when considered in the mind.
This separates logic from metaphysics, which studies existence as such.
5
Apply the distinction
Consider another concept like 'necessity' (al-darura). Is it a secondary intelligible? If so, which type? Test if it requires mental existence as a condition for attribution.
Necessity can be attributed both mentally and externally, so it likely belongs to the first type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار : ومِمَّا يَنبغي أن يعلم أنَّ المعقول الثَّاني وهو ما يكونُ الذَّهنُ فقط ظرفاً لِعروضِهِ على قِسمين : الأول : أن لا يكون الوجود الذهني شرطاً للعروض كالوجود والشيئية ونحوهما، والثاني : أن يكون شرطاً له كالكليَّة والجزئية ونظائرهما، وموضوعُ المنطق هو القسم الثاني ا.هـ.
Al-Attar: Among what should be known is that the secondary intelligible — which is that whose only locus of occurrence is the mind — is divided into two divisions: first, that mental existence is not a condition for its occurrence, like existence and thingness and their likes; second, that it is a condition for it, like universality and particularity and their analogues. And the subject of logic is the second division. End.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage, from the marginal notes of al-Attar on al-Dasuqi's commentary, defines the subject matter of logic. It begins by stating that one must know that the 'secondary intelligible' (al-ma'qul al-thani) — a concept that exists only in the mind as an attribute — is divided into two types. The first type does not require mental existence as a condition for its attachment to an object; examples include 'existence' and 'thingness', which can be predicated of things whether mentally or externally. The second type requires mental existence as a condition; examples include 'universality' and 'particularity', which only apply when the object is considered mentally. The passage concludes that the subject matter of logic (mawdu' al-mantiq) is specifically this second type. This is a foundational definition: logic studies concepts like universal and particular, not existence itself, which is studied in metaphysics.

Key Terms
المعقول الثاني
موضوع المنطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the second type of secondary intelligible, which is the subject of logic?
A) Existence (al-wujud)
B) Thingness (al-shay'iyya)
C) Universality (al-kulliyya)
D) Whiteness (al-bayad)

Key Takeaway
Logic studies only those secondary intelligibles that require mental existence as a condition for their attachment, such as universality and particularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the concept 'possibility' (al-imkan), determine whether it is a secondary intelligible and, if so, which type. Justify your answer using Al-Attar's criteria.
?
In a debate, someone claims that 'existence' is the subject of logic. Using Al-Attar's division, explain why this claim is mistaken.
